القدير: جعل الشهر قليلاً قوله: (وللمشتري لو في الرؤية أي القول للمشتري مع يمينه لو قال البائع له رأيت قبل الشراء وقال المشتري ما رأيت أو قال رأيت بعد الشراء ثم رضيت فقال رضيت قبل الرؤية، ولذا أطلق في الكتاب لأن البائع يدعي أمراً عارضاً هو العلم بالصفة والمشتري ينكره فالقول له وما في فتح القديره من أنه ينبغي أن يكون القول للبائع لأن الغالب في البياعات في الأسواق كون المشترين رأوا المبيع، فدعوى البائع رؤية المشتري تمسك بالظاهر لأن الغالب هو الظاهر والمذهب أن القول لمن تمسك بالظاهر لا بالأصل إلا أن يعارضه ظاهر آخر اهـ. مدفوع بما ذكرناه في قاعدة أن الأصل العدم فراجعها إن شئت. وفي المحيط : لو أراد المشتري أن يرده فأنكر البائع كون المردود مبيعاً فالقول للمشتري، وكذلك في خيار الشرط لأنه انفسخ العقد برده وبقي ملك البائع في يده فيكون القول قول القابض في تعيين ملكه أميناً كان أو ضميناً كالمودع والغاصب. فلو اختلفا في الرد بالعيب فالقول للبائع لأن العقد لا ينفسخ بفسخ المشتري حتى يلزمه القاضي فبقي المشتري مدعياً حق الفسخ والبائع ينكر فيكون القول له اهـ. وهذا ما كتبناه في الفوائد أن القول للقابض، إلا في هذه المسألة. وفي الظهيرية في مسألة الإختلاف في التعيين في خيار الشرط للمشتري وكانت السلعة غير مقبوضة فأراد المشتري إجازة العقد في عين في يد البائع فقال البائع ما بعتك هذا وقال المشتري بل بعتني هذا لم يذكر محمد هذه الصورة في شيء من الكتب وقالوا : ينبغي أن يكون القول قول البائع كما لو بيع هذه العين وأنكر البائع البيع أصلاً، وأما إذا كان الخيار للبائع والعين غير مقبوضة فأراد البائع الزام البيع في عين وقال المشتري ما اشتريت هذا ذكر أن القول للمشتري اهـ. والحاصل أن الخلاف إن كان في التعيين مع خيار الشرط والسلعة مقبوضة فالقول للمشتري سواء كان الخيار له أو للبائع وإن لم تكن مقبوضة فإن كان الخيار للمشتري فالقول للبائع وعكسه فالقول للمشتري، وإذا اختلفا في اشتراط الخيار فالقول لمنكره عندهما، وعنده المدعيه كما في المجمع لأن منكره يدعي لزوم العقد ومدعيه ينكر اللزوم فالقول له وتمامه في شرح المجمع وفي القنية إختلفا في شرط الخيار وأقاما الخيار أولى وفي البزازية : أقر بقبض المشتري ثم قال لم أر كله لا أدعي البينة فبينة مدعي القبض أولى اهـ .
His saying: 'And if they differ about the change, the word is that of the seller with his oath' because change is an accident and the cause of binding is apparent. It is unrestricted but restricted to when the time is near, because the apparent evidence is for him. If the time is distant, the word is for the buyer because the apparent evidence is for him. In al-Mabsut: if the time is distant, e.g., he saw a young slave-girl then bought her after twenty years, and the seller claimed she did not change, the word is for the buyer. The chief al-Sadr al-Shahid and Imam Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani gave fatwa accordingly. In al-Dhakhirah: no limit is fixed for change. In al-Zahiriyyah: if he saw something then bought it, no option unless time is long; a month is long, less is short. If it changed, he has the option in any case, and he is not believed in claiming change except with evidence unless the time is long. In Fath al-Qadir: they considered a month as short. His saying: 'And the buyer [has the word] regarding seeing' i.e., the word is for the buyer with his oath if the seller said 'you saw before purchase' and the buyer said 'I did not see' or 'I saw after purchase then I agreed' and so on. The author released it because the seller claims a contingent matter (knowledge of attribute) and the buyer denies, so the word is his. What is in Fath al-Qadir that the word should be for the seller because most market transactions involve seeing, etc., is refuted by the rule that the original is absence. In al-Muhit: if the buyer wants to return and the seller denies that the returned item was sold, the word is for the buyer. Similarly in khiyar al-shart because the contract is rescinded by return and the seller's ownership remains in his hand, so the word is for the possessor in identifying his property, whether trustee or guarantor. If they differ about return for defect, the word is for the seller because the contract is not rescinded by the buyer's rescission until the judge imposes it. In al-Zahiriyyah: regarding dispute about identification in khiyar al-shart for the buyer when the goods are not in his possession, the word is for the seller. If the option is for the seller and goods not possessed, the word is for the buyer. The conclusion: if the dispute is about identification with khiyar al-shart and goods are possessed, the word is for the buyer; if not possessed and the option is for the buyer, the word for the seller, and vice versa. If they differ about stipulating the option, the word is for the denier according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], and according to Abu Hanifa for the claimer. In al-Bazzaziyyah: if he acknowledged receipt and then said 'I did not see all' without evidence, the burden is on the claimant of receipt.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage deals with evidentiary disputes in khiyar al-ru'yah. Two main disputes are covered: (1) whether the item changed, and (2) whether the buyer saw it before purchase. Regarding change, the default rule is that the seller's statement under oath is accepted because change is a new occurrence and the contract's binding is apparent. However, this is confined to cases where the time between sighting and purchase is short; if a long time has passed, the buyer's word is taken because it is more plausible that change occurred. The commentator cites various authorities: 'a month is long, less is short.' Regarding the dispute about prior sighting, the buyer's word is accepted with his oath because the seller claims a contingent fact which the buyer denies. The principle applied is 'the original is absence of knowledge' (al-asl al-'adam). The passage then branches into related disputes about identification in khiyar al-shart, and the general rule that the person in possession of the item (qabid) has the word regarding its identity, except in cases of defect when the buyer seeks to return because the contract is not automatically rescinded.

Key Terms
القول قول البائع (al-qawl qawl al-ba'i'): The evidentiary presumption in favor of the seller's statement under oath in certain disputes.
الظاهر (al-zahir): The apparent evidence or common practice that is considered as proof in the absence of stronger evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a buyer and seller dispute about whether the buyer saw the item before purchase, whose word is accepted with oath?
A) The seller's, because the seller knows best
B) The buyer's, because the original is absence of seeing
C) The seller's, because most buyers see the item
D) The buyer's, but only if the price is high

Key Takeaway
The buyer's word is always accepted in disputes about prior sighting; for change, the seller's word in short periods and the buyer's in long periods, with a month as the threshold.

Returning Items from a Bundle: Defect vs. Inspection Option
Ruling on returning part of a bundled purchase for defect vs. inspection/stipulation options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning part of a bundled purchase and distinguish between khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ru'yah/khiyar al-shart in that context.

Core Idea
After possession, a defect allows returning part of a bundle; inspection or stipulation options do not.

In Hanafi fiqh, buying a bundle (adl) is considered a single sale. If the buyer later sells or gifts one item from the bundle and discovers a defect in the rest, can he return the remaining items? The answer depends on which option (khiyar) is invoked. This lesson explores the ruling from Al-Bahr al-Raiq and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the bundle as a single contract
Recognize that purchasing a bundle (adl) is a single sale (safqah wahidah). Returning only part of the bundle would split the contract (tafriq al-safqah).
Hanafis generally discourage splitting a single sale.
2
Identify the scenario: part of bundle alienated
The buyer has sold or gifted one or more items from the bundle. He now wishes to return the remaining items due to a defect or other option.
The passage specifically mentions 'sold' or 'gave as gift'.
3
Distinguish between defect and other khiyar options
After possession (al-qabd), khiyar al-'ayb allows returning the remainder because the contract is already complete with possession. Khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-shart do not allow partial return because they prevent the contract from being fully concluded.
Contract completion is key: defect option arises after the contract is final, while inspection and stipulation options exist before finality.
4
Note the exception before possession
If the return is sought before possession, all three options are treated equally: returning part of the bundle is not allowed for any of them, as the contract is not yet complete.
This is a refinement from the author, explaining that the earlier rule applies only after possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو اشترى عدلاً وباع منه ثوباً أو وهب رد بعيب لا بخيار رؤية أو شرط) لأنه تعذر الرد فيما خرج عن ملكه وفي رد ما بقي تفريق الصفقة قبل التمام لأن خيار الرؤية والشرط يمنعان تمامها بخلاف خيار العيب لتمامها معه بعد القبض. وترك المصنف قيد قوله : (أما قبله فالكل سواء أي خيار العيب والرؤية والشرط .
His saying: 'And if he buys a bundle and sells from it a garment or gives it as a gift, he may return [the rest] for defect, not for option of inspection or stipulation' because it is impossible to return what has left his ownership, and returning the remainder would split the contract before completion, since khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-shart prevent its completion, unlike khiyar al-`ayb because the contract is complete with it after possession. The author omitted the condition of his saying: 'As for before [possession], all are equal, i.e., the options of defect, inspection, and stipulation.'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage explains the difference between khiyar al-`ayb (defect) and khiyar al-ru'yah/khiyar al-shart when a bundle (adl) was purchased and then one item from the bundle has been sold or given away by the buyer. If the buyer later discovers a defect in the remaining items, he may return them for the defect (khiyar al-`ayb) because the contract was already complete with possession for that part. However, he cannot return the remainder based on khiyar al-ru'yah or khiyar al-shart because those options exist before the contract is deemed fully concluded; returning only part would split the contract (tafriq al-safqah) before its completion, which is not allowed. When the contract is complete with possession, the defect option can be exercised on the remaining items even if some have been alienated. The final sentence notes that if the return is sought before possession, all three options are equal in that they cannot be exercised on part of the bundle.

Key Terms
عدل (adl): A bundle or package containing multiple items sold together at a single price.
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting the bundle sale by returning only part of the items.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to return goods due to a defect that existed before purchase.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option to confirm a sale after inspecting the item.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The option stipulated in the contract to cancel within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can a buyer return the remaining items from a bundle for defect but not for khiyar al-ru'yah?
A) Because khiyar al-ru'yah is a personal right that expires with possession
B) Because the contract is not yet complete with khiyar al-ru'yah, so returning part would split the contract
C) Because the seller's consent is needed for khiyar al-ru'yah returns
D) Because a defect makes the entire contract void

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, after possession, a defect justifies splitting the bundle sale by returning part; inspection and stipulation options do not, because they prevent the contract from being fully concluded.

The Buyer's Statement is Believed Regarding Seeing the Item
Intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer's claim of not seeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whose statement is accepted in a dispute over seeing the item, apply the principle of al-asl (original presumption) to resolve such disputes, and distinguish it from related scenarios where the seller's statement is accepted.

Core Idea
The buyer's claim of not seeing prevails because the original presumption (al-asl) is non-existence, overriding market custom.

This lesson explores a procedural rule in Hanafi sales law: when a buyer and seller disagree about whether the buyer saw the item before purchase, whose statement is accepted? The text from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the default position and responds to a contrary view from Fath al-Qadir, applying the fundamental principle of al-asl (original presumption) over al-zahir (apparent practice). Understanding this ruling is crucial for khiyar al-ru'ya (option of sight) and similar disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
The buyer claims he did not see the item before purchase (or saw it only after purchase and then consented). The seller claims the buyer did see it. Whose statement is accepted?
This is a dispute about a fact (whether seeing occurred), not about the terms of the contract.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the buyer's statement is accepted. The reasoning: the seller is asserting an incidental fact (knowledge of the item's attributes) which the buyer denies, and the original presumption (al-asl) is that seeing did not occur (عدم الرؤية).
This is the ruling from the authoritative Zahir al-Riwayah works.
3
Understand the opposing view within the school
Fath al-Qadir suggests the seller's statement should be accepted because the common practice (الغالب) in markets is that buyers inspect goods before buying. This is a claim of al-zahir (apparent probability).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; both views are within the school.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The author of Al-Bahr al-Raiq rebuts Fath al-Qadir by invoking the rule 'al-asl al-adam' (the original is non-existence). The original presumption takes precedence over mere probability unless there is a stronger conflicting apparent fact. Market custom is insufficient to override the default.
This illustrates the Hanafi hierarchy: al-asl overrides al-zahir.
5
Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the buyer's statement is accepted, he can rescind the sale under khiyar al-ru'ya. If the seller's statement were accepted, the sale would be binding. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the buyer's statement.
The passage also extends this rule to other scenarios (e.g., possession matters, khiyar al-shart, khiyar al-'ayb), which are detailed in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المشتري ما رأيت أو قال رأيت بعد الشراء ثم رضيت فقال رضيت قبل الرؤية، ولذا أطلق في الكتاب لأن البائع يدعي أمراً عارضاً هو العلم بالصفة والمشتري ينكره فالقول له وما في فتح القديره من أنه ينبغي أن يكون القول للبائع لأن الغالب في البياعات في الأسواق كون المشترين رأوا المبيع، فدعوى البائع رؤية المشتري تمسك بالظاهر لأن الغالب هو الظاهر والمذهب أن القول لمن تمسك بالظاهر لا بالأصل إلا أن يعارضه ظاهر آخر اهـ. مدفوع بما ذكرناه في قاعدة أن الأصل العدم فراجعها إن شئت. وفي المحيط : لو أراد المشتري أن يرده فأنكر البائع كون المردود مبيعاً فالقول للمشتري، وكذلك في خيار الشرط لأنه انفسخ العقد برده وبقي ملك البائع في يده فيكون القول قول القابض في تعيين ملكه أميناً كان أو ضميناً كالمودع والغاصب. فلو اختلفا في الرد بالعيب فالقول للبائع لأن العقد لا ينفسخ بفسخ المشتري حتى يلزمه القاضي فبقي المشتري مدعياً حق الفسخ والبائع ينكر فيكون القول له اهـ. وهذا ما كتبناه في الفوائد أن القول للقابض، إلا في هذه المسألة. وفي الظهيرية في مسألة الإختلاف في التعيين في خيار الشرط للمشتري وكانت السلعة غير مقبوضة فأراد المشتري إجازة العقد في عين في يد البائع فقال البائع ما بعتك هذا وقال المشتري بل بعتني هذا لم يذكر محمد هذه الصورة في شيء من الكتب وقالوا : ينبغي أن يكون القول قول البائع كما لو بيع هذه العين وأنكر البائع البيع أصلاً، وأما إذا كان الخيار للبائع والعين غير مقبوضة فأراد البائع الزام البيع في عين وقال المشتري ما اشتريت هذا ذكر أن القول للمشتري اهـ. والحاصل أن الخلاف إن كان في التعيين مع خيار الشرط والسلعة مقبوضة فالقول للمشتري سواء كان الخيار له أو للبائع وإن لم تكن مقبوضة فإن كان الخيار للمشتري فالقول للبائع وعكسه فالقول للمشتري، وإذا اختلفا في اشتراط الخيار فالقول لمنكره عندهما، وعنده المدعيه كما في المجمع لأن منكره يدعي لزوم العقد ومدعيه ينكر اللزوم فالقول له وتمامه في شرح المجمع وفي القنية إختلفا في شرط الخيار وأقاما الخيار أولى وفي البزازية : أقر بقبض المشتري ثم قال لم أر كله لا أدعي البينة فبينة مدعي يصدق اهـ.
And the buyer said: 'I did not see' or he said 'I saw after the purchase and then I consented' so he said 'I consented before seeing'. Therefore it is absolute in the book because the seller claims an incidental matter which is knowledge of the attribute and the buyer denies it, so the statement is his. And what is in Fath al-Qadir that it should be that the statement is the seller's because the common practice in market sales is that the buyers saw the merchandise, so the seller's claim of the buyer's sight adheres to the apparent (al-zahir) because the common practice is the apparent, and the madhhab is that the statement is for the one who adheres to the apparent not to the original (al-asl) unless another apparent opposes it. It is rebutted by what we mentioned in the rule that the original is non-existence, so refer to it if you wish. And in al-Muhit: If the buyer wanted to return it and the seller denied that the returned item was the sold item, the statement is the buyer's. Likewise in khiyar al-shart because the contract is rescinded by his return and the seller's ownership remains in his hand, so the statement is the holder's (al-qabid) in determining his ownership, whether he is a trustee or a guarantor, like the depositary and the usurper. If they disputed regarding the return due to defect, the statement is the seller's because the contract is not rescinded by the buyer's rescission until the judge enforces it, so the buyer remains claiming the right of rescission and the seller denies, so the statement is his. And this is what we wrote in al-Fawa'id: the statement is for the holder, except in this case. And in al-Zahiriyyah regarding the issue of dispute in specification in khiyar al-shart for the buyer while the commodity was not in possession, so the buyer wanted to execute the contract on a specific item in the seller's hand, the seller said 'I did not sell this to you' and the buyer said 'Rather you sold me this'. Muhammad did not mention this scenario in any book, and they said: It should be that the statement is the seller's, just as if this item had been sold and the seller denied the sale entirely. But if the option was for the seller and the item was not in possession, and the seller wanted to enforce the sale on a specific item and the buyer said 'I did not buy this', it was mentioned that the statement is the buyer's. The conclusion is that the dispute, if it is regarding specification with khiyar al-shart and the commodity is in possession, the statement is the buyer's whether the option is for him or for the seller. If it is not in possession, then if the option is for the buyer, the statement is the seller's, and vice versa, the statement is the buyer's. And if they dispute regarding stipulation of the option, the statement is for the denier according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and according to him (Abu Hanifa) for the claimant, as in al-Majma', because the denier claims the binding of the contract and the claimant denies the binding, so the statement is his. Its completion is in Sharh al-Majma'. And in al-Qunyah: They disputed regarding stipulation of the option and they established that the option is more appropriate. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: He acknowledged the buyer's possession, then said 'I did not see all of it', he does not claim (no evidence), so the evidence of the claimant is believed.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage addresses a procedural dispute in sales law: when a buyer and seller disagree over whether the buyer saw the item before purchase, whose statement is accepted? The matn establishes that if the buyer claims he did not see it, or that he saw it after purchase and then accepted, his statement is taken. The reasoning: the seller is asserting an incidental fact (knowledge of the item's description), which the buyer denies. The default principle (الأصل) is non-seeing (عدم الرؤية). A view from Fath al-Qadir suggests the seller should be believed, as market practice is that buyers usually see the goods. However, the author rebuts this, noting that the original (عدم) takes precedence over mere appearance (الظاهر) unless there is a conflicting apparent fact. The commentary then extends to various scenarios: when the buyer wants to return and the seller denies the item was sold, the buyer's statement is accepted in khiyar al-shart because the contract is rescinded. But in khiyar al-'ayb (defect), the seller's statement is accepted because the contract is not rescinded until a judge rules. There's also a discussion on when the item is not in possession: the rule depends on who holds the option and who has possession. Finally, if they dispute the very stipulation of the option, the denier's statement is accepted (according to the two disciples), while Abu Hanifa gives it to the claimant. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of applying principles (الأصل vs الظاهر) and the importance of possession in determining whose word is taken.

Key Terms
الأصل (al-asl): The foundational presumption that something is absent unless proven otherwise (e.g., عدم الرؤية).
الظاهر (al-zahir): What is apparent based on common practice or probability, not certainty.
القول للمشتري (al-qawl li al-mushtari): The buyer's statement is accepted in the dispute regarding seeing the item before purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute between buyer and seller over whether the buyer saw the item before purchase, whose statement is accepted according to the Hanafi school?
A) The seller's statement, because it is supported by the common practice in markets.
B) The buyer's statement, because the original principle is that he did not see it.
C) Both statements are given equal weight and the judge decides.
D) The statement of the party who has possession of the item.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts the buyer's statement regarding non-seeing because the original presumption (al-asl) overrides the apparent practice (al-zahir).

Returning Part After Disposal: Khiyar al-‘Ayb vs. Khiyar al-Ru’ya
Expansion of a matn ruling on khiyar al-‘ayb vs. khiyar al-ru’ya with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on returning a part of a purchase when another part has been sold or gifted, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the revival of khiyar al-ru’ya after its fall.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru’ya prevents completion, allowing partial return; khiyar al-‘ayb after completion generally prohibits it.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, Ibn Nujaym expands on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq: if a buyer purchases a bundle of garments, then sells or gifts one garment, can he return the remaining part due to a defect? The answer depends on the type of option. The commentary explores the reasoning and a later dispute between Shams al-A’imma and Abu Yusuf/al-Quduri on whether the option of inspection can revive after it has fallen.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central case and ruling
The scenario: buyer purchased a bundle (عدل), sold or gifted one garment. He now wants to return the remainder. The matn rules: he returns for defect (khiyar al-‘ayb) but not for inspection/condition (khiyar al-ru’ya/shart). Identify the precise act and ruling.
The ruling applies after possession (qabd) as per the text.
2
Understand the key distinction
Khiyar al-ru’ya and khiyar al-shart prevent the transaction from being complete (tamam). Therefore, returning the remainder would split an incomplete transaction. Khiyar al-‘ayb arises after the transaction is complete (with possession), so returning part splits a completed deal, which is normally prohibited but here allowed due to the defect right.
The reasoning hinges on the concept of 'completion of the transaction.'
3
Note the condition of possession
Before possession, all khiyars are alike: the transaction is not complete, and returning part is not allowed. After possession, the distinction emerges. The text specifies that the issue is set after possession.
Without possession, the sale of part before possession is invalid.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
If the buyer sold the entire item (so his khiyar al-ru’ya fell), and then the item returns to him via rescission, does the option revive? Shams al-A’imma says yes (the obstacle has been removed). Abu Yusuf and al-Quduri say no (the act indicates consent, option falls permanently). Compare their rationales.
The author distinguishes selling all vs. selling part.
5
Follow the author’s tarjih
Ibn Nujaym prefers Shams al-A’imma’s view for the case of part disposal: selling part does not indicate full consent, and the barrier (splitting) is removed when the part returns.
This tarjih is the mufta bihi for this specific scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو اشترى عدلاً وباع منه ثوباً أو وهب رد بعيب لا بخيار رؤية أو شرط) لأنه تعذر الرد فيما خرج عن ملكه وفي رد ما بقي تفريق الصفقة قبل التمام لأن خيار الرؤية والشرط يمنعان تمامها بخلاف خيار العيب لتمامها معه بعد القبض. وترك المصنف قيد قوله : (أما قبله فالكل سواء أي خيار العيب والرؤية والشرط . في التسليم في الهبة ولا به منه لا يخرج عن ملكه بها إلا معه ولذا قيدها به في الهداية والمفعول كلامه «مقدر» أي رد ما بقي والمسألة موضوعة فيما إذا كان بعد القبض كما قيده به في الجامع الصغير وإلا لم يصح بيع الثوب قبل قبضة؛ كذا في العناية. أما قبله فالكل سواء لا تتم الصفقة معه . نعم يقع بين القبض وعدمه فيما إذا اشترى شيئين ولم يقبضهما ثم اطلع على عيب بأحدهما فإنه لا . يرد المغيب وحده بخلاف ما إذا كان بعد قبضهما، فلو عاد إليه بسبب هو فسخ فهو على خيار الرؤية ؛ كذا ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي. وعن أبي يوسف : لا يعود بعد سقوطه لخيار الشرط وعليه اعتمد القدوري ؛ كذا في الهداية بخلاف ما إذا وهب عبده المدين ممن له الدين أو عبده الجاني من ولي الجناية ثم رجع في الهبة حيث يعودان عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد والعذر لأبي يوسف أن حق خيار الرؤية أضعف منها؛ كذا في الشرح والعدل المثل والمراد هنا الغرارة التي هي . عدل غرارة أخرى على الجمل أو نحوه أي يعادلها وفيها أثواب وفي فتح القدير ما اعتمده القدوري صححه فاضيحان وحقيقة الملحظ تختلف فشمس الأئمة لحظ البيع والهبة مانعاً زال فيعمل المقتضي وهو خيار الرؤية عمله ولحظ على هذه الرواية مسقطاً، وإذا سقط لا يعود بلا سبب وهذا أوجه لأن نفس هذا التصرف يدل على الرضا ويبطل الخيار قبل الرؤية وبعدها .اهـ. والأوجه عندي ما ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي. وقوله لأن نفس هذا التصرف إلى أخره ممنوع وإنما يدل لو تصرف في جميع المبيع. وإنما الكلام هنا فيما إذا تصرف في البعض فحينئذ لو رد الباقي فقط لزم تفرق الصفقة فكان لزوم تفوقها مانعاً من رد الباقي فإذا زال عمل المقتضي عمله وكأنه اختلط عليه بما إذا باع المبيع كله وسقط خياره ثم رد عليه بما هو فسخ فإنه لا يعود خياره كما قدمناه لكن لم يذكروا فيها خلافاً والله تعالى أعلم. تهجم على مقام قوله : (نعم يقع الفرق الخ) لم يظهر فرق فيما ذكره لأن المراد إظهاره قبل القبض ولا رد له فيه تأمل قوله : (وكأنه اختلط عليه أي على صاحب الفتح. قال في النهر وأقول: هذا هذا الإمام مع عدم التدبر في الكلام، وذلك أن جزمهم بعدم عود الخيار فيما إذا باع كله ثم عاد إليه بما هو فسخ من غير ذكر خلاف دليل بين لما اختاره القدوري إذ لو كانت العلة المؤثرة وجود المانع للزم إذا زال أن يعود لكنه لا يعود لأنه سقط وشأن الساقط أن لا يعود، ودعوى أن الكل مسقط بيع البعض مانع تحكم ظاهر وهذا معنى قوله لأن نفس هذا التصرف الخ». فإن قلت: لو كان وبيع كذلك لما احتيج إلى التعليل بأن في الرد تفريق الصفقة قلت: لا مانع من أ يعلل الحكم بعلتين : الرضا بالبيع ولزوم تفريق الصفقة غير أنه ما دام خارجاً عن ملكه فالتعليل به أظهر فلهذا المعنى فتدبر
His statement: (And if he bought a bundle and sold from it a garment or gifted it, he returns due to defect but not due to option of inspection or condition) because returning what has left his ownership is impossible, and in returning the remainder there is splitting of the transaction before completion, because the option of inspection and condition prevent its completion, unlike the option of defect which occurs after its completion with possession. And the author omitted the condition... His statement: (As for before possession, all are the same, i.e., the option of defect, inspection, and condition.) In delivery of the gift, and [the garment] does not leave his ownership except with it, so he restricted it in al-Hidayah. The object of his statement is implied: i.e., return the remainder. The issue is set forth when it is after possession, as he restricted it in al-Jami' al-Saghir; otherwise the sale of the garment before possession is not valid. As for before possession, all are the same: the transaction is not completed with it. Indeed, there is a difference between possession and non-possession in the case where one buys two items and does not take possession of them, then discovers a defect in one of them: he cannot return the defective one alone, unlike when after possession of both. If it returns to him by means of a rescission, then it is subject to the option of inspection; thus mentioned by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. And from Abu Yusuf: it does not return after it has fallen for the option of condition, and upon him (Abu Yusuf) al-Quduri relied. Similarly in al-Hidayah, unlike the case where he gifted his slave who is indebted to the donee, or his slave who committed an offense to the guardian of the offense, then he revoked the gift: there they return according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. The excuse for Abu Yusuf is that the right of the option of inspection is weaker than those. Similarly in al-Sharh. And al-‘adl al-mithl: the intended meaning here is the sack which is a bundle equal to another bundle on a camel or similar, i.e., equivalent to it, and in it are garments. And in Fath al-Qadir: what al-Quduri relied upon was authenticated by Qadi Khan. The reality of the perspective differs: Shams al-A'imma considered the sale and gift as an obstacle that has been removed, so the effective cause (the option of inspection) operates. And he considered, according to this transmission, as a cause of cancellation; when it falls, it does not return without a cause. This is more appropriate because the act itself indicates consent and invalidates the option before and after inspection. But in my view, what Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned is more appropriate. His statement that 'the act itself indicates consent etc.' is denied; it only indicates if he acted regarding the entire sold item. The discussion here is when he acted regarding part, so if he returns only the remainder, splitting of the transaction would have occurred, so the necessity of splitting was an obstacle to returning the remainder. When the obstacle is removed, the effective cause operates. It seems he confused this with the case where he sold the entire sold item and his option fell, then it came back to him by means of a rescission, where his option does not return, as we have mentioned, but they did not mention any difference. And Allah knows best. He jumped to a level. His statement: (Indeed, the difference occurs etc.) The difference in what he mentioned is not apparent because the intended is to show it before possession, and there is no return in it. Contemplate. His statement: (It seems he confused) i.e., the author of Fath al-Qadir. He said in al-Nahr: And I say: This imam lacked reflection in his speech. Their certainty that the option does not return when he sells all and then it returns to him by a rescission, without mentioning any difference, is clear evidence for what al-Quduri chose. If the effective cause was the existence of an obstacle, then when it is removed it should return, but it does not return because it fell, and the nature of the fallen is not to return. The claim that selling all is a cause of cancellation while selling part is an obstacle is pure arbitrariness. This is the meaning of his statement: 'because the act itself indicates consent etc.' If you said: If it were such and such, there would be no need to give the reason that returning splits the transaction. I say: There is no harm in explaining the ruling with two reasons: consent to the sale and the necessity of splitting the transaction, except that as long as it is outside his ownership, the explanation by that (splitting) is more apparent. So for this meaning, reflect.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the matn: if a person buys a bundle (عدل) and then sells or gifts one garment from it, he can return the remainder for a defect only if the khiyar is one of inspection (خيار الرؤية) or condition (خيار الشرط), not if it is the option of defect (خيار العيب). The reasoning: the sold or gifted part has left his ownership, so returning the remainder would split the transaction (تفريق الصفقة) before it is complete. Khiyar al-ru'ya and khiyar al-shart prevent the transaction from becoming complete, so splitting is irrelevant; but khiyar al-'ayb arises after the transaction is complete (after possession), so returning part would break a completed deal. The commentator notes that this applies after possession (قبض); before possession, all khiyars are alike: you cannot return only part because the transaction is entirely incomplete. A nuance: if two items are bought without possession and one has a defect, you cannot return only that one; after possession you can. The passage then discusses whether khiyar al-ru'ya can return after it has fallen. For example, if the buyer sells all the items (so his option falls), and then the item comes back to him through a rescission (فسخ), does the option revive? Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi says yes, because the sale was a barrier that has been removed, so the option operates again. Abu Yusuf and al-Quduri say no, because selling all indicates consent and causes the option to fall permanently. The author (Ibn Nujaym) prefers al-Sarakhsi's view for the case where only part was sold: selling part does not indicate full consent, and the barrier (splitting the transaction) was present, but when the barrier is removed (e.g., by the part returning), the option can revive. He distinguishes this from selling all, where the option falls completely and does not revive. The text concludes with a discussion criticizing Fath al-Qadir's confusion of the two cases.

Key Terms
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting the transaction by returning only part while another part has been disposed of (sold or gifted).
عدل (adl): A bundle or sack of goods, often used as a unit of sale. Here it refers to a bundle of garments equal to another bundle on a camel.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of inspection: the buyer may return an item if he did not see it before purchase.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option of defect: the buyer may return an item if a defect was present at the time of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can a buyer return the remainder of a bundle (عدل) after selling one garment from it in the case of khiyar al-'ayb but not in the case of khiyar al-ru'ya?
A) Because khiyar al-ru'ya is stronger than khiyar al-'ayb.
B) Because khiyar al-ru'ya and khiyar al-shart prevent the transaction from being complete, so returning part does not split a completed transaction; khiyar al-'ayb occurs after the transaction is complete.
C) Because khiyar al-'ayb requires the buyer to return all items, while khiyar al-ru'ya does not.
D) Because the buyer has not yet taken possession in khiyar al-ru'ya.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of returning part of a purchase after disposing of another part depends on whether the option prevents transaction completion: allowed for khiyar al-‘ayb, not for khiyar al-ru’ya/shart; and for the latter, partial disposal is an obstacle that, if removed, can revive the option per Shams al-A'imma’s view.

The Option of Sight After Partial Disposal: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on option of sight after partial disposal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi positions on whether the option of sight returns after partial disposal, and explain the legal basis and thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The option of sight: does it return after partial disposal? Abu Yusuf says no, Sarakhsi says yes if the preventing factor is removed.

The option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) allows a buyer to rescind a purchase upon seeing the item. A key question arises when the buyer has disposed of part of the purchased lot: does the option revive if that disposal is later rescinded? Our passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school, with Abu Yusuf and al-Quduri holding that the option is permanently dropped, while Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi argues that it returns when the preventing factor of tafriq al-safqah is removed. This lesson will trace the arguments and identify the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Note that the case involves a purchase of two items where one is defective after possession, and the buyer disposes of the non-defective part before returning the defective one.
The disposal could be a sale or gift.
2
State the two main positions
Abu Yusuf (followed by al-Quduri) holds that the option does not return after being dropped by the disposal. Muhammad al-Shaybani analogizes to the revocation of a gift. Sarakhsi distinguishes between full and partial disposal.
Sarakhsi's view is that partial disposal does not drop the option; it only temporarily prevents return due to tafriq al-safqah.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Yusuf relies on the disposal indicating satisfaction and the weakness of khiyar al-ru'ya. Muhammad uses qiyas with a gifted slave. Sarakhsi focuses on the removal of the preventing factor.
The qiyas of Muhammad is considered weaker because the option of sight is less established than the right to revoke a gift.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the buyer sells the non-defective item and that sale is rescinded, can he still return the defective one? Abu Yusuf says no; Sarakhsi says yes.
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq prefers Sarakhsi's view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi is disputed: al-Quduri's view (Abu Yusuf) is relied upon in many texts, but the commentator explicitly prefers Sarakhsi.
This reflects a tension between earlier and later Hanafi authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب خيار الرؤية

في التسليم في الهبة ولا به منه لا يخرج عن ملكه بها إلا معه ولذا قيدها به في الهداية والمفعول كلامه «مقدر» أي رد ما بقي والمسألة موضوعة فيما إذا كان بعد القبض كما قيده به في الجامع الصغير وإلا لم يصح بيع الثوب قبل قبضة؛ كذا في العناية. أما قبله فالكل سواء لا تتم الصفقة معه . نعم يقع بين القبض وعدمه فيما إذا اشترى شيئين ولم يقبضهما ثم اطلع على عيب بأحدهما فإنه لا . يرد المغيب وحده بخلاف ما إذا كان بعد قبضهما، فلو عاد إليه بسبب هو فسخ فهو على خيار الرؤية ؛ كذا ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي. وعن أبي يوسف : لا يعود بعد سقوطه لخيار الشرط وعليه اعتمد القدوري ؛ كذا في الهداية بخلاف ما إذا وهب عبده المدين ممن له الدين أو عبده الجاني من ولي الجناية ثم رجع في الهبة حيث يعودان عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد والعذر لأبي يوسف أن حق خيار الرؤية أضعف منها؛ كذا في الشرح والعدل المثل والمراد هنا الغرارة التي هي . عدل غرارة أخرى على الجمل أو نحوه أي يعادلها وفيها أثواب وفي فتح القدير ما اعتمده القدوري صححه فاضيحان وحقيقة الملحظ تختلف فشمس الأئمة لحظ البيع والهبة مانعاً زال فيعمل المقتضي وهو خيار الرؤية عمله ولحظ على هذه الرواية مسقطاً، وإذا سقط لا يعود بلا سبب وهذا أوجه لأن نفس هذا التصرف يدل على الرضا ويبطل الخيار قبل الرؤية وبعدها .اهـ. والأوجه عندي ما ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي. وقوله لأن نفس هذا التصرف إلى أخره ممنوع وإنما يدل لو تصرف في جميع المبيع. وإنما الكلام هنا فيما إذا تصرف في البعض فحينئذ لو رد الباقي فقط لزم تفرق الصفقة فكان لزوم تفوقها مانعاً من رد الباقي فإذا زال عمل المقتضي عمله وكأنه اختلط عليه بما إذا باع المبيع كله وسقط خياره ثم رد عليه بما هو فسخ فإنه لا يعود خياره كما قدمناه لكن لم يذكروا فيها خلافاً والله تعالى أعلم.
تهجم
على مقام
قوله : (نعم يقع الفرق الخ) لم يظهر فرق فيما ذكره لأن المراد إظهاره قبل القبض ولا رد له فيه تأمل قوله : (وكأنه اختلط عليه أي على صاحب الفتح. قال في النهر وأقول: هذا هذا الإمام مع عدم التدبر في الكلام، وذلك أن جزمهم بعدم عود الخيار فيما إذا باع كله ثم عاد إليه بما هو فسخ من غير ذكر خلاف دليل بين لما اختاره القدوري إذ لو كانت العلة المؤثرة وجود المانع للزم إذا زال أن يعود لكنه لا يعود لأنه سقط وشأن الساقط أن لا يعود، ودعوى أن الكل مسقط بيع البعض مانع تحكم ظاهر وهذا معنى قوله لأن نفس هذا التصرف الخ». فإن قلت: لو كان وبيع كذلك لما احتيج إلى التعليل بأن في الرد تفريق الصفقة قلت: لا مانع من أ يعلل الحكم بعلتين : الرضا بالبيع ولزوم تفريق الصفقة غير أنه ما دام خارجاً عن ملكه فالتعليل به أظهر فلهذا المعنى
فتدبر
Book of Sales, Chapter of Option of Sight. Regarding delivery in a gift, and he does not exit from his ownership except with it, therefore it is restricted by it in al-Hidayah. The object of his speech is 'estimated' meaning: return what remains. The issue is posed when it is after possession, as restricted in al-Jami' al-Saghir; otherwise the sale of a garment before its possession is not valid, as in al-Inayah. Before it, everything is equal, the transaction is not completed with it. Yes, a difference occurs between possession and non-possession when he buys two items and does not take possession of them, then finds a defect in one of them: he cannot return the defective one alone, unlike when it is after possession of both. So if it returns to him due to a rescission, it is upon the option of sight. Thus mentioned by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. From Abu Yusuf: it does not return after its fall due to the option of stipulation, and al-Quduri relied upon it. Thus in al-Hidayah. Unlike if he gifted his indebted slave to the creditor or his criminous slave to the guardian of the injury, then revoked the gift: in that case they return according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. The excuse for Abu Yusuf is that the right of the option of sight is weaker than them. Thus in the commentary. 'Al-adl al-mithl' and here it means a sack that is equivalent to another sack on a camel or similar, i.e. equivalent, and in it are garments. In Fath al-Qadir: what al-Quduri relied upon was declared sound by Qadi Khan. The viewpoint differs: Shams al-A'imma considered sale and gift as a preventing factor that has been removed, so the effective cause (the option of sight) operates; while according to this narration he considered it as a dropping factor, and once dropped it does not return without a cause. This is more proper because this very action indicates satisfaction and invalidates the option before and after sight. The end. But in my view, what Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned is more proper. His statement 'because this very action etc.' is rejected; it indicates only if he disposed of the entire sold item. The discussion here is when he disposed of part. Then if he returns only the remainder, tafriq al-safqah (splitting the transaction) would be necessary, so the necessity of splitting was a preventing factor from returning the remainder. When it is removed, the effective cause operates. It seems that he confused this with the case where he sold the entire sold item and his option fell, then it returned to him via a rescission, then his option does not return as we have mentioned, but they did not mention a difference in that; and Allah knows best. Intrusion upon a station. His statement: 'Yes, a difference occurs...' The difference is not apparent in what he mentioned because the intent is to show it before possession, and he has no right to return in it; ponder. His statement: 'It seems he confused it' i.e. the author of al-Fath. He said in al-Nahr: I say: this is this Imam with lack of reflection in speech. That is because their decisive ruling that the option does not return when he sold everything then it returned to him via a rescission, without mentioning a difference, is clear evidence for what al-Quduri chose, because if the effective cause were the existence of a preventing factor, it would follow that when it is removed it would return, but it does not return because it has fallen, and the nature of what has fallen is that it does not return. The claim that 'the whole is a dropper, selling part is a preventer' is an obvious arbitrary judgment, and this is the meaning of his statement 'because this very action etc.' If you say: if it were like that for a sale, then there would be no need to explain by saying that returning involves splitting the transaction. I say: there is no preventing from explaining the ruling by two causes: satisfaction with the sale and the necessity of splitting the transaction, except that as long as it is outside his ownership, the explanation by the latter is more apparent, so for this meaning contemplate.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) and its interaction with returning a defective item when multiple items are purchased. The author begins by clarifying that the ruling applies after possession. Before possession, the transaction is incomplete, so partial return is impossible. After possession, if one of two items is defective, the defective one can be returned alone. The key dispute is about what happens if the buyer later disposes of the non-defective item (e.g., sells or gifts it) and then that disposal is rescinded—does the option of sight return? Abu Yusuf holds that once the option is dropped by the disposal, it does not return, relying on the idea that the disposal indicates satisfaction. This view is adopted by al-Quduri and is considered the relied-upon position in the Hidayah. Muhammad holds that it returns, analogizing to the case of a gifted slave who returns upon revocation of the gift. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi distinguishes: if the disposal was of the entire sale, the option does not return because it is completely dropped; but if only part was disposed of, the remaining part can be returned because the prevention (tafriq al-safqah) is removed. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq prefers Sarakhsi's view, noting that disposing of part does not necessarily indicate full satisfaction; it only indicates satisfaction for that part. The passage ends with a critical note on the commentary of Fath al-Qadir, arguing that the analogy with full disposal is mistaken. The lesson is an intra-Hanafi dispute with a clear preference expressed by the commentator.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of sight, a contractual right to rescind a purchase after seeing the item if it proves unsatisfactory.
تفرق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting a single transaction into parts, which is generally prohibited in sale contracts as it causes ambiguity.
إقالة (iqalah): Rescission of a sale by mutual consent, which returns ownership to the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, what happens to the option of sight if a buyer disposes of only part of the purchased lot and then returns the remaining defective item?
A) The option does not return because it was dropped.
B) The option returns because the preventing factor (tafriq al-safqah) is removed.
C) The option returns only if the disposal was by sale, not gift.
D) The option is void from the beginning.

Key Takeaway
The option of sight after partial disposal is a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute; the strongest position is Sarakhsi's, but the mufta bihi often follows Abu Yusuf.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Definition of 'Ayb: Linguistic and Legal
Opening definitions of 'ayb and its legal scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and legal definitions of 'ayb in the Hanafi school and identify which definition is used in khiyar al-ayb.

Core Idea
A defect ('ayb) is any quality that reduces market value in merchants' estimation.

Option of Defect (khiyar al-ayb) is a buyer's right to return a defective item. But what counts as a defect? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the linguistic meaning of 'ayb and its legal definition, which is based on practical market valuation. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Examine the Linguistic Root
Identify the triliteral root 'ayn-ya-ba and its various forms: 'aaba (to be defective), 'ayyaba (to attribute defect). Understand that linguistically, 'ayb means any imperfection.
Focus on the verb forms and their transitivity as mentioned in al-Misbah.
2
Compare the Two Legal Definitions
Read the definition from Fath al-Qadir (what sound innate nature is free from) and the author's definition (what reduces price according to merchants). Recognize that the latter is the operative definition in fiqh.
The first definition is more philosophical; the second is practical.
3
Understand Why Merchants' Estimation Matters
In Hanafi law, value is determined by market norms, not subjective preferences. A defect is anything that lowers the item's customary price, even if it doesn't affect functionality.
This allows for defects like aesthetic flaws that reduce desirability.

Chapter of Option of Defect. Whoever finds a defect in the sold item takes it with all the price or returns it. And what necessitates a reduction in price according to merchants is (the defect). Chapter of Option of Defect. The arrangement of options and the addition in “option of defect” is the addition of a thing to its cause. As for the defect linguistically, it is said “aab al-mataa’ ‘ayban” from the paradigm “saara”, so he is ‘a’ib, and the owner made it defective so it is ma’yub, transitive and intransitive. The agent from this is ‘a’ib and ‘ayyab for emphasis, and the noun is al-‘ab and al-ma’ab. And ‘ayyabah with tashdid means he attributed it to defect. The word ‘ayb is used as a noun and pluralized as ‘uyub; thus in al-Misbah. In Fath al-Qadir it is defined as “what the sound innate nature is free from”. As for the Shari’ah definition, it is what the author will mention: that which necessitates a reduction in price according to merchants. (Note): Concealing a defect in a commodity is haram. In al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Fatawa: if he sells a defective commodity, he must disclose it; if he does not disclose, some of our scholars said he becomes a transgressor and his testimony is rejected. Al-Sadr said: we do not adopt this. End. Al-Khulasah restricted it to him knowing about it. In al-Zahiriyyah and in the hadith: Chapter of Option of Defect. His statement: (And in Fath al-Qadir it is defined...) al-Ramli said: I say: many have defined it similarly. Benefit: Some Shafi’i scholar – I mean Ibn Hajar al-Haytami in his fatwas – was asked about a man, a baker who kneads bread for sale and sells it to people while he is leprous, scabietic, with itch and black bile; is it permissible for him to handle the mentioned bread while he has these characteristics or not? He answered: it is not permissible to sell what he has handled like his kneading unless he explains the true state of affairs to the buyer, because if the buyer knew that, he would not buy it from him in most cases; and everything that is like that, its concealment is from forbidden deception (ghish), and the Prophet (SAW) said: “Whoever deceives my community is not of me.” Many imams have narrated that it is obligatory upon the ruler or his deputy to expel those who have leprosy or scabies from among the people and to allocate for them a place outside the city and to spend on their poor from the public treasury. Our principles do not reject it. The standard for forbidden deception is that the sold item includes a descriptive deficiency such that if the buyer knew of it he would refrain from purchasing; so everything that is like that is deception, and everything that is not like that is not forbidden deception. This was mentioned in the aforementioned fatwas and there is no preventing from it in our school; ponder. End. His statement: (Al-Sadr said we do not adopt it) He said in al-Nahr: i.e., we do not adopt the view that he becomes a transgressor merely due to this because it is a minor sin. There is no difference in this between the sold item and the price except in two issues: first, a Muslim in dar al-harb who buys something and pays the price with adulterated goods or defective dirhams is permissible if he is free, not if he is a slave; thus in al-Walwaljiyyah. (1) Narrated by Muslim in Kitab al-Iman, Hadith 164; Abu Dawud in Kitab al-Buyu’, Bab 50; al-Tirmidhi in Kitab al-Buyu’, Bab 72; Ibn Majah in Kitab al-Tijarat, Bab 36; Ahmad in his Musnad (2/50, 242, 417) with the wording: “Whoever deceives is not of me.”
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq begins the chapter on the Option of Defect (khiyar al-‘ayb). It opens by quoting the author of Kanz al-Daqa’iq: the buyer may either keep the defective item for the full price or return it. The commentary then clarifies that the chapter heading “option of defect” attaches the ruling to its cause. A linguistic analysis of the word ‘ayb follows, drawing from al-Misbah, indicating its root and forms. The legal definition according to Fath al-Qadir is “what the sound innate nature is free from”, but the standard fiqh definition (what the author will cite) is “what necessitates a reduction in price in the estimation of merchants.” A critical note (تنبيه) states that concealing a defect is haram. The commentary cites al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Fatawa that the seller must disclose; if he does not, some scholars (مشايخنا) say he becomes a fasiq (transgressor) and his testimony is rejected. However, al-Sadr al-Shahid says this view is not adopted, because mere concealment without other aggravating factors is a minor sin and does not render him a fasiq. This is the relied-upon position. The passage then quotes a long fatwa from the Shafi’i scholar Ibn Hajar al-Haytami about a baker with contagious skin diseases, concluding that he must disclose his condition because it would repel buyers and concealing it is deception (ghish), which is haram. The fatwa also discusses the ruler’s duty to isolate such people. The text notes that Hanafi principles do not reject this. The standard for ghish is given: any deficiency that would deter a reasonable buyer. Finally, an exception is mentioned: a Muslim in dar al-harb (hostile territory) may pay with counterfeit goods or defective coins, provided he is free; a slave may not. The hadith “Whoever deceives…” is cited at the end.

Key Terms
عيب ('ayb): Linguistically: a defect in an object. Legally: any quality that reduces the market value of the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi legal definition in this passage, what qualifies as a defect ('ayb)?
A) Any imperfection in the item's innate nature.
B) Anything that reduces the item's price in the estimation of merchants.
C) Any flaw that makes the item unusable.
D) Only flaws that are visible at the time of sale.

Key Takeaway
A defect under khiyar al-ayb is anything that lowers the market value in merchants' estimation, not merely a functional flaw.

Prohibition of Concealing Defects and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on concealment and intra-Hanafi difference on fisq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on concealing a defect, cite the hadith evidence, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding fisq (transgression).

Core Idea
Concealing a defect is haram, but alone it is a minor sin, not causing fisq.

While the buyer has the option of defect, the seller has a duty to disclose known defects. The passage clearly states that concealment is haram. However, there is a nuance within the Hanafi school: does such concealment automatically make the seller a fasiq (transgressor) whose testimony is rejected? Al-Sadr al-Shahid says no, and this is the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling on Concealment
Read the note (تنبيه): 'كتمان عيب السلعة حرام' – concealing a defect is haram. This is the foundational ruling.
No dispute on the haram nature.
2
Examine the Hadith Evidence
The passage cites the hadith 'من غش أمتي فليس مني' (Whoever deceives my community is not of me). Understand that concealment is a form of deception (ghish), which is prohibited.
The hadith is narrated by Muslim and others.
3
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Fisq
Some Hanafi scholars (مشايخنا) hold that the seller who conceals becomes a fasiq and his testimony is rejected. Al-Sadr al-Shahid rejects this view.
Al-Sadr's reasoning: concealment alone is a minor sin (saghirah), not a major one.
4
Note the Mufta Bihi Position
The relied-upon position is that concealment is haram but does not automatically disqualify the seller's testimony. This is supported by al-Nahr.
The tarjih authority is al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-Nahr.

As for the defect linguistically, it is said “aab al-mataa’ ‘ayban” from the paradigm “saara”, so he is ‘a’ib, and the owner made it defective so it is ma’yub, transitive and intransitive. The agent from this is ‘a’ib and ‘ayyab for emphasis, and the noun is al-‘ab and al-ma’ab. And ‘ayyabah with tashdid means he attributed it to defect. The word ‘ayb is used as a noun and pluralized as ‘uyub; thus in al-Misbah. In Fath al-Qadir it is defined as “what the sound innate nature is free from”. As for the Shari’ah definition, it is what the author will mention: that which necessitates a reduction in price according to merchants. (Note): Concealing a defect in a commodity is haram. In al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Fatawa: if he sells a defective commodity, he must disclose it; if he does not disclose, some of our scholars said he becomes a transgressor and his testimony is rejected. Al-Sadr said: we do not adopt this. End. Al-Khulasah restricted it to him knowing about it. In al-Zahiriyyah and in the hadith: Chapter of Option of Defect. His statement: (And in Fath al-Qadir it is defined...) al-Ramli said: I say: many have defined it similarly. Benefit: Some Shafi’i scholar – I mean Ibn Hajar al-Haytami in his fatwas – was asked about a man, a baker who kneads bread for sale and sells it to people while he is leprous, scabietic, with itch and black bile; is it permissible for him to handle the mentioned bread while he has these characteristics or not? He answered: it is not permissible to sell what he has handled like his kneading unless he explains the true state of affairs to the buyer, because if the buyer knew that, he would not buy it from him in most cases; and everything that is like that, its concealment is from forbidden deception (ghish), and the Prophet (SAW) said: “Whoever deceives my community is not of me.” Many imams have narrated that it is obligatory upon the ruler or his deputy to expel those who have leprosy or scabies from among the people and to allocate for them a place outside the city and to spend on their poor from the public treasury. Our principles do not reject it. The standard for forbidden deception is that the sold item includes a descriptive deficiency such that if the buyer knew of it he would refrain from purchasing; so everything that is like that is deception, and everything that is not like that is not forbidden deception. This was mentioned in the aforementioned fatwas and there is no preventing from it in our school; ponder. End. His statement: (Al-Sadr said we do not adopt it) He said in al-Nahr: i.e., we do not adopt the view that he becomes a transgressor merely due to this because it is a minor sin. There is no difference in this between the sold item and the price except in two issues: first, a Muslim in dar al-harb who buys something and pays the price with adulterated goods or defective dirhams is permissible if he is free, not if he is a slave; thus in al-Walwaljiyyah. (1) Narrated by Muslim in Kitab al-Iman, Hadith 164; Abu Dawud in Kitab al-Buyu’, Bab 50; al-Tirmidhi in Kitab al-Buyu’, Bab 72; Ibn Majah in Kitab al-Tijarat, Bab 36; Ahmad in his Musnad (2/50, 242, 417) with the wording: “Whoever deceives is not of me.”
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq begins the chapter on the Option of Defect (khiyar al-‘ayb). It opens by quoting the author of Kanz al-Daqa’iq: the buyer may either keep the defective item for the full price or return it. The commentary then clarifies that the chapter heading “option of defect” attaches the ruling to its cause. A linguistic analysis of the word ‘ayb follows, drawing from al-Misbah, indicating its root and forms. The legal definition according to Fath al-Qadir is “what the sound innate nature is free from”, but the standard fiqh definition (what the author will cite) is “what necessitates a reduction in price in the estimation of merchants.” A critical note (تنبيه) states that concealing a defect is haram. The commentary cites al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Fatawa that the seller must disclose; if he does not, some scholars (مشايخنا) say he becomes a fasiq (transgressor) and his testimony is rejected. However, al-Sadr al-Shahid says this view is not adopted, because mere concealment without other aggravating factors is a minor sin and does not render him a fasiq. This is the relied-upon position. The passage then quotes a long fatwa from the Shafi’i scholar Ibn Hajar al-Haytami about a baker with contagious skin diseases, concluding that he must disclose his condition because it would repel buyers and concealing it is deception (ghish), which is haram. The fatwa also discusses the ruler’s duty to isolate such people. The text notes that Hanafi principles do not reject this. The standard for ghish is given: any deficiency that would deter a reasonable buyer. Finally, an exception is mentioned: a Muslim in dar al-harb (hostile territory) may pay with counterfeit goods or defective coins, provided he is free; a slave may not. The hadith “Whoever deceives…” is cited at the end.

Key Terms
غش (ghish): Deception in a transaction, especially by concealing a defect that would deter the buyer.
فسق (fisq): A legal term for sinfulness that may disqualify one’s testimony; here debated whether concealment alone causes fisq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view in this passage, what is the effect of concealing a defect on the seller's legal testimony?
A) His testimony is automatically rejected.
B) His testimony is not automatically rejected; it is evaluated normally.
C) The sale is void, so testimony is irrelevant.
D) He must pay compensation, but testimony is unaffected.

Key Takeaway
Concealing a defect is haram, but it is a minor sin and does not alone make the seller a fasiq whose testimony is rejected.

Standard for Forbidden Deception (Ghish) and the Baker's Case
Standard for ghish and its application to a baker and dar al-harb exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi standard for forbidden deception (ghish) to a scenario and identify the exception for dar al-harb.

Core Idea
Deception is haram when the deficiency would deter a reasonable buyer; exceptions apply in dar al-harb.

The passage includes a detailed fatwa from a Shafi'i scholar about a baker with contagious skin diseases, which the Hanafi school accepts as consistent with its principles. From this, a general standard for ghish is derived: any deficiency that would cause a buyer to refrain from purchasing if known. There is also an exceptional ruling for Muslims in enemy territory (dar al-harb). This lesson focuses on applying that standard.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Standard for Ghish
Read the line: 'وضابط الغش المحرم أن يشتمل المبيع على وصف نقص لو علم به المشتري امتنع عن شرائه' – the standard is a deficiency that would make the buyer abstain if he knew.
This is a clear, objective test.
2
Apply the Standard to the Baker's Case
The baker has contagious diseases. Buyers would likely abstain if they knew. Therefore, concealing his condition is haram, and he must disclose.
The fatwa also adds a public health duty: isolating such individuals.
3
Understand the Exception for Dar al-Harb
In dar al-harb, a free Muslim may pay with counterfeit goods or defective coins because the transaction is with enemy combatants and normal rules of honesty in trade are relaxed for self-preservation.
The exception does not apply to slaves.

Basic Ruling and Definition of the Option of Defect (Khiyar al-Ayb)
States the basic ruling and defines defect linguistically and legally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to a buyer upon discovering a defect, define 'ayb linguistically and legally, and explain the rational proof (istihsan) for khiyar al-ayb.

Core Idea
A buyer may either keep the defective item with full price or return it; no reduction is allowed.

In this lesson, we explore the basic ruling of khiyar al-ayb (option of defect). The Hanafi school gives the buyer a choice when a defect is found: keep the item with the full price, or return it. We will also examine how the law defines a defect, both linguistically and legally, and the rational proof that establishes this option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the buyer's two options
From the text: 'أخذه بكل الثمن أو رده' (take it with all the price or return it). Note that there is no third option like a price reduction.
2
Define 'ayb linguistically and legally
Linguistically, 'ayb derives from 'عاب' meaning to be defective. Legally, it is defined as 'ما أوجب نقصان الثمن عند التجار' (what obligates a decrease in the price according to traders). This is a customary standard.
The legal definition relies on 'urf (custom) of merchants.
3
Grasp the rational proof (istihsan)
The contract implicitly requires the item to be sound. When that condition is lost, the buyer is given a choice to avoid harm. The text says: 'لأن مطلق العقد يقتضي وصف السلامة فعند فواته يتخير كيلا يتضرر'.
This proof is rational, not based on a specific text, but is widely accepted.
4
Understand the scope of the ruling
The ruling applies whether the defect existed at the time of sale or arose later while in the seller's possession, and whether it is major or minor. The text says: 'أطلقه فشمل ما عند البيع أو حدث بعده في يد البائع وما إذا كان فاحشاً أو يسيراً'.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب خيار العيب من وجد بالمبيع عيباً أخذه بكل الثمن أو رده وما أوجب نقصان الثمن عند التجار فهو. تقدم وجه ترتيب الخيارات والإضافة في خيار العيب إضافة الشيء إلى سببه، وأما العيب فهو في اللغة يقال عاب المتاع عيباً من باب سار فهو عائب وعابه صاحبه فهو معيب يتعدى ولا يتعدى والفاعل من هذا عائب وعياب مبالغة والاسم العاب والمعاب. وعيبه بالتشديد نسبه إلى العيب واستعمل العيب اسماً وجمع على عيوب؛ كذا في المصباح. وفسره في فتح القدير بما تخلو عنه أصل الفطرة السليمة . وأما في الشريعة فما سيذكره المصنف أنه ما أوجب نقصان الثمن عند التجار. قوله : (من وجد بالمبيع عيباً أخذه بكل الثمن أو رده لأن مطلق العقد يقتضي وصف السلامة فعند فواته يتخير كيلا يتضرر بلزوم ما لا يرضى به دل كلامه أنه ليس له إمساكه وأخذ النقصان. لأن الأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن في مجرد العقد ولأنه لم يرض بزواله عن ملكه بأقل من المسمى فيتضرر به ودفع الضرر عن المشتري ممكن بالرد بدون تضرره . أطلقه فشمل ما عند البيع أو حدث بعده في يد البائع وما إذا كان فاحشاً أو يسيراً؛ كذا في به السراج الوهاج.
Chapter of Option of Defect. Whoever finds a defect in the purchased item takes it with all the price or returns it, and what obligates a decrease in the price according to traders is (the defect). The arrangement of options and the addition in 'option of defect' as addition of a thing to its cause has preceded. As for the defect linguistically, it is said 'aaba al-mataa' 'ayban' from the paradigm of 'saara' so it is 'aa'ib', and its owner 'aabahu' so it is 'ma'yub' - it is transitive and intransitive, and the agent from this is 'aa'ib' and 'ayyab' for exaggeration, and the noun is 'al-aab' and 'al-ma'aab'. 'Ayyabahu' with tashdid means to attribute it to defect, and 'al-ayb' is used as a noun and pluralized as 'uyub'; as in al-Misbah. And he interpreted it in Fath al-Qadir as 'what the sound innate disposition is free from'. As for in Shariah, it is what the author will mention: that it is what obligates a decrease in the price according to traders. His saying: 'Whoever finds a defect in the purchased item takes it with all the price or returns it' because an absolute contract requires the description of soundness, so when it is lost he has the choice so as not to be harmed by adhering to what he is not pleased with. His words indicate that he does not have the option to keep it and take the difference, because descriptions are not compensated by any part of the price in the mere contract, and because he did not consent to its leaving his ownership for less than the named price, so he would be harmed, and preventing harm to the buyer is possible by returning without harming him. He (the author) made it absolute, so it includes what (the defect) existed at the time of sale or occurred afterwards in the seller's possession, and whether it is major or minor; as in Bah al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) in the Hanafi school. It states the basic ruling: when a buyer discovers a defect in the purchased item, he has two choices - either keep the item and pay the full price, or return it. The defect is defined as anything that reduces the price according to merchants; this is a customary standard. The commentator then provides both the linguistic definition of 'ayb' and the legal definition. The proof given is rational (istihsan): a sale contract implicitly requires the item to be free of defects. If the item is defective, the buyer would be harmed if forced to keep it with no compensation, and the seller would not be harmed by taking it back. The ruling applies whether the defect existed at the time of sale or arose later while in the seller's possession, and whether it is major or minor. This is the relied-upon position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): Option of defect: the buyer's right to return a defective purchased item.
العيب (al-ayb): Anything that reduces the price according to custom of traders.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what are the options available to a buyer who finds a defect in a purchased item?
A) Keep the item with full price or return it
B) Keep the item and claim a reduction in price
C) Return the item and claim compensation for damages
D) Only return the item, cannot keep it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi khiyar al-ayb gives the buyer only two options: keep with full price or return, based on the implicit contractual guarantee of soundness.

Prohibition of Concealing Defects: Ruling, Proof, Intra-Hanafi Dispute, and the Baker's Case
Expands on the prohibition of concealing defects with proof, intra-madhhab dispute, application, and textual correction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibition of concealing defects, cite the hadith proof, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on declaring the seller fasiq, apply the definition of forbidden cheating to a real case, and identify a textual critical refinement.

Core Idea
Concealing a defect is haram; the seller must disclose, but declaring him fasiq is not the taken position.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq establishes the prohibition of hiding defects in sales, supported by the hadith 'Whoever cheats is not of me.' It then delves into an intra-Hanafi difference: some scholars consider the concealing seller fasiq with testimony rejected, but al-Sadr al-Shahid advises otherwise. The text also presents a concrete fatwa from the Shafi'i scholar Ibn Hajar al-Haytami on a baker with contagious diseases, showing cross-madhhab agreement. Finally, a textual critical refinement resolves a popular misreading of a hadith. This lesson ties together fiqh, intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, and textual criticism.

Guided Path
1
State the core ruling and its evidence
Identify that the ruling is haram and the evidence is the hadith 'من غش فليس مني'.
2
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on tasdiq
Some Hanafi teachers hold the seller becomes fasiq; al-Sadr rejects this because it is a minor sin.
The mufta bihi is not to declare fasiq.
3
Analyze the baker's case as an application
The fatwa from Ibn Hajar applies the same principle: a baker with visible contagious diseases must disclose, otherwise it is ghishsh.
This shows cross-madhhab agreement.
4
Define forbidden cheating (ghishsh)
The definition: any deficiency in the sold item that would deter the buyer if known.
5
Note the textual critical refinement
The correct wording of a hadith regarding the buyer 'Ada' bin Khalid, correcting the common misreading.
