(تنبيه) : كتمان عيب السلعة حرام. وفي البزازية وفي الفتاوى : إذا باع سلعة معيبة عليه البيان وإن لم يبين قال بعض مشايخنا يفسق وترد شهادته . قال الصدر : ولا تأخذ به .اهـ. وقيده في الخلاصة بأن يعلم به وفي الظهيرية وفي الحديث : (من غش فليس مني). قوله : (وفسره في فتح القدير (الخ قال الرملي أقول : فسره بذلك كثير . من فائدة : سئل بعض الشافعية - أقول وهو ابن حجر الهيتمي وهي في فتاواه ـ عن رجل عجان خباز يعجن الخبز للبيع ويبيعه على الناس وهو أبرص أجذم ذو حكة وسوداء، فهل يجوز له أن يباشر الخبز المذكور وهو بتلك الصفات أم لا ؟ فأجاب بقوله : لا يجوز بيع ما باشر نحو عجنه إلا أن يبين للمشتري حقيقة الحال لأن المشتري لو اطلع على ذلك لم يشتره منه في الغالب وكل ما كان كذلك يكون كتمه من الغش المحرم وقد قال صلى الله عليه وسلم من غش فليس مني) وقد نقل غير واحد من الأئمة أنه يجب على السلطان أو نائبه أن يخرج من به نحو جذام أو برص من بين أظهر الناس ويفرد لهم محلاً خارج البلد وينفق على فقرائهم من بيت المال اهـ. وقواعدنا لا تأباه. وضابط الغش المحرم أن يشتمل المبيع على وصف نقص لو علم به المشتري امتنع عن شرائه فكل ما كان كذلك يكون غشاً، وكل ما لا يكون كذلك لا يكون غشاً محرماً ؛ ذكره في الفتاوى المذكورة ولا مانع منه عندنا تأمل اهـ . قوله : (قال الصدر لا نأخذ به قال في النهر : أي لا نأخذ بكونه يفسق بمجرد هذا لأنه صغيرة، ولا فرق في ذلك بين المبيع والثمن إلا في مسألتين: الأولى المسلم في دار الحرب إذا اشترى شيئاً ودفع الثمن عروضاً مغشوشة أو دراهم زيوفاً جاز إن كان حراً لا عبداً؛ كذا في الولوالجية. (۱) رواه مسلم في كتاب الإيمان حديث ١٦٤ أبو داود في كتاب البيوع باب ٥٠. الترمذي في كتاب البيوع باب ۷۲. ابن ماجه في كتاب التجارات باب .٣٦ أحمد في مسنده (٢/ ٥٠، ٢٤٢، ٤١٧) بلفظ من غش فليس مناه. اشترى عداء العداء إذا كان بن خالد بن هوذة - بالذال المعجمة وفتح الهاء وسكون الواو - من رسول الله عبداً لا داء فيه ولا غائلة ولا خبثة وهذه الرواية هي الصحيحة؛ كذا ذكره الطحاوي في شرح مشكل الآثار بإسناده إلى عبد المجيد. قال العداء بن خالد: ألا أقرئك كتاباً كتبه لي رسول الله ؟ قلت: بلى فأخرج إلي كتاباً فإذا فيه بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم هذا ما اشترى محمد رسول الله الخ. وهذا تبين أن المشتري كان العداء لا محمد رسول الله. وفي من عامة كتب الفقه هذا ما اشترى محمد رسول الله من العداء لكن الصحيح ما قلنا اهـ.
(Note): Concealing a defect of the commodity is forbidden. In al-Bazzaziyyah and in al-Fatawa: if he sells a defective commodity, it is upon him to disclose; if he does not disclose, some of our teachers said he becomes a transgressor and his testimony is rejected. al-Sadr said: do not take with that. He restricted it in al-Khulasa by (if) he knew about it. And in al-Zahira and in the hadith: 'Whoever cheats is not of me.' His saying: (and he interpreted it in Fath al-Qadir) etc., al-Ramli said: I say: many interpreted it thus. From a benefit: A Shafi'i scholar - I say he is Ibn Hajar al-Haytami and it is in his fatawa - was asked about a man, a baker who kneads bread for sale and sells it to people while he is leprous, scabious, having itch and blackness, is it permissible for him to handle that bread while he has those descriptions or not? So he answered: It is not permissible to sell what he handled like his kneading unless he explains to the buyer the true state of affairs, because if the buyer were to know that, he would generally not buy it from him, and everything like that, its concealment is from forbidden cheating, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever cheats my ummah is not of me.' And more than one of the imams have narrated that it is obligatory upon the ruler or his deputy to expel those who have leprosy or vitiligo from among the people and set aside for them a place outside the city and spend on their poor from the public treasury. Our principles do not oppose it. The definition of forbidden cheating is that the sold item includes a description of deficiency such that if the buyer knew it he would refrain from buying it; so everything like that is cheating, and everything not like that is not forbidden cheating; mentioned in the aforementioned fatawa, and there is no obstacle to it with us; contemplate. His saying: (al-Sadr said we do not take with it) he said in al-Nahr: meaning we do not take with (the idea) that he becomes a transgressor merely due to this because it is a minor sin, and there is no difference in that between the sold item and the price except in two issues: the first: a Muslim in dar al-harb if he buys something and pays the price with adulterated goods or counterfeit dirhams, it is permissible if he is free, not a slave; as in al-Walwaljiyah. Narrated by Muslim, Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, Ahmad. 'Ada' bin Khalid - with the dotted dhal, fatha on ha, sukun on waw - bought from the Messenger of Allah a slave without disease, deceit or defect, and this narration is the correct one; as mentioned by al-Tahawi. 'Ada' said: Shall I not read to you a document written for me by the Messenger of Allah? I said: yes, so he brought out a document and in it: 'This is what Muhammad the Messenger of Allah bought...' etc. And this shows that the buyer was 'Ada' not the Messenger of Allah. In most fiqh books it is 'This is what Muhammad the Messenger of Allah bought from 'Ada'' but the correct is what we said.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a critical note (tanbih) on the prohibition of concealing defects in sales. The basic ruling is that hiding a defect is haram. The text reports a difference among Hanafi scholars regarding whether the seller who conceals becomes a transgressor (fasiq) and his testimony rejected. Al-Sadr al-Shahid advises not to act on this. The condition is that the seller must know about the defect. A hadith is cited: 'Whoever cheats is not of me.' A fatwa from the Shafi'i scholar Ibn Hajar al-Haytami is then quoted concerning a baker with leprosy and other diseases. The fatwa states that such a person must disclose his condition to buyers, otherwise it is concealment of a defect and thus forbidden cheating. The Hanafi commentator affirms that this ruling does not contradict Hanafi principles. The text then discusses a textual variation in a hadith about a purchase from 'Ada' bin Khalid, clarifying that the buyer was 'Ada', not the Prophet. This is a textual critical refinement.

Key Terms
الغش (al-ghishsh): Cheating: selling an item with a hidden defect that would deter the buyer if known.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a baker with leprosy who sells bread without disclosing his condition?
A) Permissible because the bread itself is not defective
B) Haram because it involves cheating and concealment of a defect
C) Makruh only if he touches the bread directly
D) Permissible if he is a slave

Key Takeaway
Concealing a defect is haram; the seller must disclose known defects, but declaring him fasiq is not the relied-upon position; cross-madhhab agreement exists on the prohibition of ghishsh.

Major vs Minor Defects and Substitution Without Weighing
Classifies defects and substitution rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between major and minor defects according to the Hanafi school and apply the substitution rule for defective goods without precise weighing.

Core Idea
A major defect permits return in all contracts; a minor defect only in ordinary sales, not in dower, khul', or sulh.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies two interconnected issues: the classification of defects as major or minor, and the permissibility of substituting a defective item with a sound one without exact weighing. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for practical sales and marriage contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Determine whether the contract is an ordinary sale, dower (mahr), khul' compensation, or sulh for blood. The ruling on returning for a defect varies by contract.
In ordinary sales, both major and minor defects allow return. In dower, khul', and sulh, only major defects do.
2
Classify the defect as major or minor
Use the Hanafi definition: a major defect is one on which all valuators agree that it reduces the price; a minor defect is one on which valuators differ.
The text gives an example: a major defect in dower lowers the quality from good to medium or medium to low.
3
Apply the default rule for return
If the contract is ordinary sale, return is allowed for both major and minor defects. If it is dower, khul', or sulh, return is allowed only for major defects.
Exception: if the dower item is of the measured or weighed category, even a minor defect allows return.
4
Analyze substitution without weighing
When the seller replaces a defective part of a commodity, check whether that quantity is customarily weighed. If not, substitution by estimation (mujadhafah) is permissible.
Example: one or two pieces of fanid candy can be replaced without weighing; three pieces cannot because they enter the realm of weight.
5
Verify conditions for return
Ensure the defect existed with the seller before sale and that the buyer was unaware of it at the time of contract.
The text mentions these conditions briefly; they are standard in all defect-based returns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي جامع الفصولين والمهر وبدل الخلع وبدل الصلح عن دم بفاحش العيب لا بيسيره وفي غيرها يرد بهما والفاحش في المهر ما يخرجه من الجيد إلى الوسط ومن الوسط إلى الرديء، وإنما لا يرد في المهر بيسيره إذا لم يكن كيلياً أو وزنياً وأما هما فيرد بيسيره أيضاً اهـ. ولم يتكلم الشارحون على ما إذا رد البعض هل له أن يعطي مثله سليماً؟ قال في القنية وفي الذخيرة: اشترى مناً من الفانيد فوجد واحدة أو اثنتين منها أسود فأبدله البائع أبيض بغير وزن جاز، وفي الثلاث لا يجوز لأنها تدخل تحت الوزن ولذا لو اشترى الخبز ووجد خبزاً واحداً محترقاً فأبدله الخباز لم يجز إلا بالوزن لأنه مما يدخل تحت الوزن، فإن خمسة أساتير وعشرة وزن حجر فلا تجوز فيه المجاذفة. قال رضي الله تعالى عنه : وعرف به كثير من المسائل وهو أن استبدال شيء بمثله في الرد بالعيب إنما يجوز مجازفة إذا لم يكن لذلك المقدار من ذلك الجنس حجر يوزن به، وإن كان له من جنس آخر حجر ترى أنه جعل الثلاثة من الفانيد موزونة وإن لم يكن ذلك القدر من الخبز موزوناً اهـ. ولا بد من قيود الأول أن يكون العيب عند البائع. الثاني أن لا يعلم به المشتري عند للمسألة العمد یرد فلا ألا الثانية يجوز إعطاء الزيوف والناقص في الجبايات .اهـ. وأقول : قوله إذا اشترى شيئاً» صوابه أسيراً بدل قول شيئاً» كما رأيته في الولوالجية. وعلل الفرق بين الحر والعبد بأن شراء الأحرار ليس بشراء ليجب إعطاء المسمى قوله : هذا ما اشترى قال الرملي : في نسخة ما اشتراه». قوله : (فاحشاً أو يسيراً الخ) في البزازية : اشترى كرماً فبان أن شربه من ناوق على ظهر نهر له الرد لأنه عيب فاحش والعيب اليسير ما يدخل تحت تقويم المقومين، وتفسيره أن يقوم سليماً بألف ومع العيب بأقل وقومه آخر مع العيب بألف أيضاً والفاحش ما لو قوم سليماً بألف وكل قوموه مع العيب بأقل. قوله : (على
And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: (in cases of) dower, substitute of khul', substitute of sulh for blood, (the return is) for a major defect not a minor one, while in others it is returned for both. The major (defect) in dower is what takes it from good to medium or from medium to inferior. And it is not returned in dower for a minor defect if (the dower) is not of the measured or weighed category; as for these, it is returned for a minor defect as well. The commentators did not discuss if he returns part of it, whether he may give its sound equivalent? He said in al-Qunyah and al-Dhakhirah: He bought a mann of fanid, found one or two black pieces, so the seller replaced them with white ones without weighing; it is permissible. In three, it is not permissible because it falls under weight. Similarly, if he bought bread and found one burnt loaf and the baker replaced it, it is not permissible except by weight because it falls under weight. For five ustat and ten, weight stone, so throwing (by estimation) is not permissible. He (the author) said: Many issues are known by this: that substitution of something with its like in return for defect is only permissible by estimation if there is no stone to weigh that quantity of that genus; and if there is a stone of another genus, you see that he considered the three pieces of fanid as weighed even if that quantity of bread is not weighed. Conditions are necessary: first, the defect must be with the seller; second, the buyer must not know of it at the time of... intentional return... no... except the second: it is permissible to give counterfeit and deficient (coins) in collections. I say: his saying 'if he buys something' is correct as 'a captive' instead of 'something' as I saw in al-Walwaljiyyah. And the difference between free and slave is explained: because the purchase of free men is not a purchase so that the named (price) must be given. His saying: 'this is what he bought' al-Ramli said: in a copy 'what he bought'. His saying: (major or minor etc.) in al-Bazzaziyyah: He bought a vineyard and it appeared that its drink is from a watering channel on the river, he has the option to return because it is a major defect. The minor defect is what is subject to assessment of valuators, and its interpretation: it is valued sound at a thousand and with the defect less, and another values it with defect also at a thousand; the major is if valued sound at a thousand and every one values it with defect less. His saying: (upon).
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage deals with the classification of defects as major (fahish) or minor (yasir) and the rules of substitution when returning defective goods. In certain contracts like dower (mahr), khul' compensation, and diyat settlement, only major defects allow return; minor defects do not. The major defect in dower is defined as one that degrades the quality from good to medium or medium to inferior. For measured (makil) or weighed (mawzun) items, even a minor defect allows return. The text then discusses a practical issue: when the buyer returns part of a defective commodity, can the seller replace it with sound ones? The answer is: yes, without weighing if the quantity is small and not customarily weighed (like one or two pieces of fanid/candy). For three pieces, it becomes weight-based and requires exact weight. This is based on the principle that substitution by estimation is only allowed when there is no standard weight for that specific quantity and genus. The text also corrects a textual error in al-Walwaljiyyah, changing 'something' to 'a captive'. Finally, definitions of major and minor defects from al-Bazzaziyyah are given: a minor defect is where there is difference of opinion among valuators; a major defect is where all agree on reduction.

Key Terms
عيب فاحش (ayb fahish): A major defect that all valuators agree reduces the price.
عيب يسير (ayb yasir): A minor defect where valuators differ; some say it reduces price, others say no.
مجازفة (mujadhafah): Estimation without precise weighing or measuring.
كيلي (kili): Measured by volume (e.g., grains, liquids).
وزني (wazni): Weighed by scale (e.g., gold, silver, certain foods).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, in which contract is return allowed only for major defects?
A) Ordinary sale
B) Marriage (dower)
C) Lease
D) Sale of real estate

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes defects by the consensus of valuators: major defects allow return in all contracts, minor defects only in ordinary sales and not in dower, khul', or sulh unless the item is measured or weighed.

The Hanafi Ruling on Khiyar al-‘Ayb: Buyer’s Options and Conditions
Commentary on the ruling of khiyar al-'ayb with conditions and seven exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two permissible options for a buyer discovering a defect, identify the six conditions for khiyar al-‘ayb, and explain the seven exceptional cases where the option does not apply.

Core Idea
The buyer must choose between keeping the item with full price or returning it—he cannot claim compensation for the defect.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on the option of defect (khiyar al-‘ayb) in sales. Based on the prophetic precedent and detailed juristic reasoning, we will see why the buyer is given a choice between two remedies only, and under what conditions this option is available or barred. The discussion includes important nuances such as the distinction between gross and minor defects in the context of dower (mahr), and the replacement of defective parts by estimation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Core Ruling from the Hadith Precedent
Examine the hadith about the Prophet's purchase from 'Ada' bin Khalid. Identify that the ruling of khiyar al-‘ayb is derived from this practice and that it establishes the buyer's right to return a defective item.
The hadith is cited to show the origin of the option.
2
Identify the Buyer’s Two Permissible Options and the Prohibition of Compensation
According to the text, the buyer may either keep the defective item and pay the full agreed price, or return it for a full refund. The buyer cannot keep the item and claim a reduction in price because the defect is not considered a separate part of the price.
This is a clear distinction from the Shafi'i school which allows compensation.
3
List the Six Conditions for the Option to Apply
The text enumerates conditions: the defect existed in the seller's possession, the buyer was unaware at contract and at receipt, the defect cannot be removed without hardship, the seller did not stipulate immunity, and the defect has not disappeared before rescission.
These conditions must all be satisfied for the option to be valid.
4
Recognize the Seven Exceptional Cases Where the Option is Suspended
The passage lists seven scenarios where the buyer cannot return the defective item, e.g., if the buyer becomes muhrim after purchase of game, or if the buyer is a dhimmi who later converts to Islam after buying wine.
These exceptions are detailed in the 'Fawa'id' section of the text.
5
The Distinction Between Gross and Minor Defects in Mahr
In the context of dower (mahr), only gross defects (fahish) allow return, not minor ones, unless the dower consists of measured or weighed items. This contrasts with sales where either defect triggers the option.
This nuance shows the application of the same principle in different contracts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب خيار العيب. اشترى العداء بن خالد بن هوذة من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عبداً لا داء فيه ولا غائلة ولا خبثة وهذه الرواية هي الصحيحة؛ كذا ذكره الطحاوي في شرح مشكل الآثار بإسناده إلى عبد المجيد. قال العداء بن خالد: ألا أقرئك كتاباً كتبه لي رسول الله؟ قلت: بلى، فأخرج إلي كتاباً فإذا فيه: بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم، هذا ما اشترى محمد رسول الله من العداء... وهذا تبين أن المشتري كان العداء لا محمد المشتري ممكن بالرد بدون تضرره. أطلقه فشمل ما عند البيع أو حدث بعده في يد البائع وما إذا كان فاحشاً أو يسيراً؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج. وفي جامع الفصولين والمهر وبدل الخلع وبدل الصلح عن دم بفاحش العيب لا ولذا لو اشترى الخبز ووجد خبزاً واحداً محترقاً فأبدله الخباز لم يجز إلا بالوزن لأنه مما يدخل تحت الوزن، فإن خمسة أساتير وعشرة وزن حجر فلا تجوز فيه المجاذفة. قال رضي الله عنه: وعرف به كثير من المسائل موزوناً. ولا بد من قيود: الأول أن يكون العيب عند البائع. الثاني أن لا يعلم به المشتري عند البيع. الثالث أن لا يعلم به عند القبض وهي في الهداية. الرابع أن لا يتمكن من إزالته بلا مشقة فإن تمكن فلا كإحرام الجارية فإنه بسبيل من تحليلها ونجاسة الثوب، وينبغي ثوب لا يفسد بالغسل ولا ينقص؛ كذا في فتح القدير ولا حاجة إلى قوله ينبغي مع التصريح. قال في الولوالجية: اشترى ثوباً فوجد فيه دماً إن كان إذا غسله من الدم ينقص الثوب كان عيباً لوجود حده وإلا لا يكون عيباً. ولو اشترى جبة فوجد فيها فأرة ميتة فهو عيب لوجود حده فإن لبسها حتى نقصها رجع بنقصان العيب لتعذر الرد. وقيدها في البزازية بأن يضرها الفتق فإن ضرها يردها وإن لم يضرها لا ترد. الخامس أن لا يشترط البراءة منه خصوصاً أو من العيوب عموماً وسيأتي آخر الباب. السادس أن لا يزول قبل الفسخ فإن زال ليس له الرد مثل بياض العين إذا انجلى والحمى إذا زالت كذا في السراج الوهاج. ويستثنى من إطلاقهم مسائل ذكرناها في الفوائد: الأولى بيع صيد بين حلالين ثم أحرما أو أحدهما فوجد به عيباً امتنع رده وإنما يرجع بالنقصان كما صرحوا به في جنايات الإحرام. الثانية قال في البغية والقنية: لو كان في الدار باب في الطريق الأعظم وبابه في سكة غير نافذة أقام أهلها بينة أنهم أعاروا البائع هذا الطريق فأمر القاضي بسده يخير المشتري إن شاء رده وإن شاء رجع بنقصان ذلك الطريق والتخيير هنا بخلاف سائر العيوب. الثالثة اشترى الذمي خمراً وقبضها وبه ثم أسلم سقط خيار الرد؛ كذا في فتح القدير. الرابعة اشترى كفناً للميت ووجد به عيباً لا يرد ولا يرجع بالنقصان إن تبرع به أجنبي ولو وارثاً رجع بالنقص إن كان من التركة. الخامسة اشترى من عبده المأذون المديون المستغرق فوجد به عيباً لا يرده عليه ولا على بائعه إن كان الثمن منقوداً وإن لم ينقده المولى وقبض المبيع أولاً ووجد به عيباً يرده إن كان الثمن من النقود أو كيلياً أو وزنياً بغير عينه لأنه يدفع بالرد مطالبة من نفسه وإن كان عرضاً لا يمكن الرد. وفي المحيط: لو اشترى المولى من مكاتبه فوجد به عيباً لا يرده ولا يرجع ولا يخاصم بائعه لكونه عبده. السادسة باع نفس العبد من العبد بجارية ثم وجد بها عيباً رد الجارية وأخذ من العبد قيمة نفسه عندهما، وعند محمد يرجع بقيمة الجارية. السابعة باع الوارث من مورثه فمات المشتري وورثه البائع ووجد به عيباً رد إلى الوارث الآخر إن كان وإن لم يكن له سواه لا يرد ولا يرجع بالنقصان، وكذا إذا من ابنه الصغير شيئاً وقبضه وأشهد ثم وجد به عيباً يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي.
Chapter of Option of Defect. 'Ada' bin Khalid bin Hudha bought from the Messenger of Allah a slave with no disease, no hidden defect, and no treachery, and this narration is the correct one... (then the commentary) He said: (Whoever finds a defect in the sold item takes it with the full price or returns it.) Because the absolute contract requires the description of soundness, so when it is lost he is given a choice to avoid harm from being bound to what he is not pleased with. His statement indicates that he does not have the right to keep it and take compensation for the defect, because descriptions are not met by any portion of the price in the mere contract, and because he did not consent to its departure from his ownership for less than the named price, so he would be harmed, and preventing harm to the buyer is possible by return without harming him. (He gave it absolutely, so it includes what was at the time of sale or occurred afterward in the seller's possession, and whether it is gross or minor.) ... (Then conditions and exceptions...)
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the chapter of Option of Defect. It begins by citing a hadith about the Prophet's purchase from 'Ada' bin Khalid, establishing the precedent for khiyar al-'ayb. The core ruling is that when a buyer discovers a defect in the purchased item, he has two options: accept the item with full payment of the agreed price, or return it for a full refund. The text explains that the buyer cannot keep the item and claim a reduction in price because the defect is not a separate part of the price—the contract implicitly guarantees soundness. The ruling applies whether the defect existed at the time of sale or arose afterward while in the seller's possession, and whether the defect is gross (fahish) or minor (yasir). However, in the context of dower (mahr), the rule differs: only gross defects allow return, not minor ones, unless the dower consists of measured or weighed items. The commentator then addresses a specific issue: if the buyer finds a defect in only part of the goods, can he replace the defective part with a sound one without weighing? The text cites from al-Qunyah and al-Dhakhirah that if someone bought a mann of candy and found one or two pieces black, replacing them with white pieces without weighing is permissible, but three pieces require weighing because that amount becomes weight-based. The same applies to bread: replacing a burnt loaf requires weighing. The general principle is that replacement by estimation is allowed only if there is no standard weight measure for that quantity of that type. The passage then lists six conditions for the option: (1) the defect must have existed while the item was in the seller's possession; (2) the buyer was unaware of it at the time of the contract; (3) the buyer was unaware of it at the time of receipt; (4) the defect cannot be removed without hardship (e.g., a slave girl's ihram can be lifted by her owner, so it is not a defect; a cloth that can be washed and regain its value is not defective); (5) the seller did not stipulate immunity from that defect specifically or from all defects generally; (6) the defect has not disappeared before rescission (e.g., whiteness of the eye that clears, or fever that subsides). Finally, seven exceptions are given where the option of return is not available: (1) if two purchasers of game hunt become muhrim and find a defect; (2) if a door is ordered closed by a judge due to borrowed right of way; (3) if a dhimmi buys wine and then accepts Islam; (4) if a shroud is bought for a deceased and a defect is found; (5) if a slave buys from his authorized slave who is in debt; (6) if a slave sells himself and the defect is found; (7) if an heir buys from the testator and then inherits the item.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-‘ayb): The option a buyer has to either keep a defective item with full price or return it for a refund.
عيب فاحش (‘ayb fāhish): A gross defect that reduces the value significantly; in the context of mahr, it is defined as one that changes the item from good to medium or from medium to poor.
عيب يسير (‘ayb yasīr): A minor defect that does not significantly affect value; in sales, both types allow option, but in mahr, only fahish defects matter unless the mahr is measured or weighed.
المجاذفة (al-mujādhaqah): Replacement by estimation without precise weighing or measuring.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a condition for the buyer to exercise the option of defect (khiyar al-‘ayb)?
A) The defect must be gross (fahish), not minor.
B) The buyer must have stipulated immunity from defects.
C) The defect must have existed in the seller's possession.
D) The buyer must have paid the full price before discovering the defect.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts the buyer's remedy for a defective item to either full acceptance with full price or full return, without the possibility of claiming a reduction for the defect.

Third and Fourth Conditions: Knowledge and Removability
Listing and explaining the third and fourth conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the third and fourth conditions for khiyar al-ayb and apply them to determine whether a defect gives the right to return.

Core Idea
Knowledge of defect at receipt bars return; easily removable defects are not defects.

The third condition for khiyar al-ayb concerns the buyer's knowledge at the time of receipt, while the fourth concerns the ability to remove the defect without hardship. These two conditions together distinguish between defects that are legally actionable and those that are merely temporary or trivial. The commentator explains the nuances and gives practical examples.

Guided Path
1
State the third condition
The buyer must not have known of the defect at the time of taking possession (qabd). Knowledge after contract but before receipt is irrelevant; the condition is tied to receipt.
Ibn Nujaym notes that mere sight of the defect does not automatically imply consent; explicit acceptance is needed.
2
Analyze the ikhtilaf on sight as consent
Some scholars argue that seeing the defect and still taking possession implies acceptance, but the relied-upon Hanafi position (as per al-Zayla'i and others) is that acceptance must be explicit, not inferred from silence or mere sight.
This ikhtilaf affects whether a buyer who saw a defect but said nothing can later return the item.
3
State the fourth condition
The buyer must be unable to remove the defect without hardship (mashaqqa). If the defect can be easily removed (e.g., washing blood from a garment without damage), it is not a legally recognized defect.
The text gives examples: a slave-girl in ihram (can be deconsecrated) and a garment with blood (if washing damages it, it is a defect; otherwise not).
4
Apply the examples
Blood on a garment that decreases value when washed is a defect; a dead mouse in a cloak is a defect; a tear that harms the garment if repaired is a defect; if no harm, no defect.
The ruling from al-Bazzaziyya adds that if the tear harms the garment (by repair), return is allowed; otherwise not.
5
Note the nuance of 'decrease'
A defect must cause a decrease in value or utility from the merchants' perspective. If removal does not cause decrease, it is not a defect.
This aligns with the Hanafi custom-based definition of defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Third: that he does not know of it at the time of receipt, and it is in al-Hidayah. Fourth: that he is not able to remove it without hardship; if he is able, then no, like the consecration of a slave-girl, for he has a way to make her permissible, and the impurity of a garment. And it should be a garment that is not spoiled by washing and does not decrease; like that is in Fath al-Qadir, and there is no need for his statement 'it should be' with explicit mention. He said in al-Walwaljiyyah: He bought a garment and found blood in it; if when he washes it of blood the garment decreases, it is a defect due to the presence of its limit; otherwise it is not a defect. And if he bought a cloak and found a dead mouse in it, it is a defect due to the presence of its limit; if he wore it until it decreased, he claims the decrease of the defect due to the impossibility of return. And it was restricted in al-Bazzaziyyah by the tear harming it; if it harms, he returns it; if it does not harm, then no. Fifth: that he does not stipulate exemption from it specifically or from defects generally, and it will come at the end of the chapter. Sixth: that it does not disappear before rescission; if it disappears, he has no right to return, like the white of the eye if it clears and fever if it goes away; like that in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. His statement: (Third) that he does not know of it at the time of receipt. He said in al-Sharnabulaliyyah: it implies that mere sight is consent, and the statement of al-Zayla'i opposes it: there was not found from the buyer anything indicating consent to it after knowledge of the defect. And likewise what is in Sharh al-Majma': and he did not consent after seeing it. His statement: (and likewise the option of stipulation) I say: it preceded in its chapter when mentioning the fruit of the difference between the Imam and his two companions regarding the entry of the sold item into

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement from the sharh explaining the conditions (shurut) for exercising the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb). It covers the third through sixth conditions as listed in the matn. The third condition is that the buyer must not have known of the defect at the time of taking possession. The commentator notes a difference of opinion: mere sight of the defect may imply consent according to some, but the relied-upon view is that it does not unless there is explicit acceptance. The fourth condition is that the buyer cannot remove the defect without hardship; if he can easily remove it (like washing a garment or deconsecrating a slave-girl), it is not a defect. Examples are given: a garment that decreases when washed is defective; a dead mouse is a defect; a tear that harms the garment gives the right to return. The fifth condition is that the seller did not stipulate exemption from that specific defect or from all defects (which will be discussed later). The sixth condition is that the defect must be permanent and not disappear on its own before rescission; if it disappears (like temporary fever), the right to return is lost. The marginal comments further clarify the third condition.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The option of the buyer to return a defective item or claim compensation for the decrease in value.
عيب (ayb): A defect that decreases the value or utility of the item from the perspective of merchants.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases a garment and later finds a bloodstain that can be removed by washing without causing damage to the garment, does he have the right to return the garment due to defect?
A) Yes, because blood is a defect regardless of removability.
B) No, because he can remove it without hardship, so it is not considered a defect.
C) Yes, but only if the blood is fresh.
D) No, because he must have stipulated exemption.

Key Takeaway
The third and fourth conditions of khiyar al-ayb ensure that the option is only available for defects that are both unknown to the buyer at receipt and not easily removable.

Fifth and Sixth Conditions: Exemption and Permanence
Listing and explaining the fifth and sixth conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fifth and sixth conditions for khiyar al-ayb and apply them to scenarios involving seller's exemption and temporary defects.

Core Idea
Seller's stipulation of exemption bars return; temporary defects that self-cure before rescission forfeit the option.

The fifth condition concerns the seller's right to stipulate exemption from defects (bara'a), and the sixth condition deals with the defect's permanence. These conditions protect the seller from unpredictable returns and ensure the defect is genuine at the time of rescission.

Guided Path
1
State the fifth condition
The seller must not have stipulated exemption from that specific defect or from all defects generally. If the seller explicitly says 'I sell this item with no liability for defects', the buyer waives his right to khiyar al-ayb.
The text says 'sa yati akhir al-bab' (will come at the end of the chapter), indicating a detailed discussion later.
2
State the sixth condition
The defect must not disappear before the buyer rescinds the contract. If the defect heals or vanishes on its own (e.g., temporary fever, white film in eye that clears), the option is lost.
Examples from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: white of the eye (bayad al-ayn) clearing, fever subsiding.
3
Understand the rationale for the fifth condition
The seller may lower the price due to known defects or accept the risk of unknown ones. A stipulation of bara'a is valid in Hanafi fiqh and is binding on the buyer, preventing return unless the defect was deliberately concealed.
Hanafis permit such stipulations because the buyer is free to accept or reject the term.
4
Understand the rationale for the sixth condition
The option exists to remedy a genuine deficiency. If the defect disappears naturally, the basis for the option ceases. This prevents the buyer from cancelling a contract for a temporary issue.
The condition assumes the defect is not caused by the buyer and the disappearance is not due to the buyer's action.
5
Apply the examples
If a horse has a minor limp that heals within a day, the buyer cannot return it. But if the seller had exempted himself from all defects, a permanent limp would still be non-returnable (unless the seller knew and hid it).
Note: Deliberate concealment (tadlis) by the seller overrides the exemption.

Source Text

الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission of the contract; annulment by mutual agreement or by exercise of an option.

Key Takeaway
The fifth and sixth conditions of khiyar al-ayb protect the seller from returns when an exemption is agreed upon or when the defect is temporary, ensuring that the option is only exercised for genuine, permanent defects not waived by contract.

Three Exceptions to Khiyar al-Ayb
Listing three exceptional cases to khiyar al-ayb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three exceptional cases where the right to return a defective item is lost or replaced, and explain the legal reasoning behind each exception.

Core Idea
The right to return a defective item is forfeited when returning would cause a greater legal harm.

The general rule in Hanafi fiqh is that a buyer discovering a defect may return the item (khiyar al-ayb). However, this right is not absolute. In three exceptional cases, the buyer loses the option to return and can only claim compensation for the decrease in value. Each exception is based on a distinct legal rationale: prohibitions of ihram, judicial intervention, and change of legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Default Ruling
Start by remembering that in a sale, if the buyer finds a defect that existed at the time of contract or delivery, the default remedy is to return the item and demand a full refund (khiyar al-ayb). This is the standard rule.
The default is return (radd), not compensation (nugsan).
2
Analyze Exception 1: Game and Ihram
Two persons buy a game animal while both are halal (not in ihram). Later, one or both enter ihram and discover a defect. They cannot return the game because doing so would involve hunting or killing it, which is prohibited in ihram. Instead, they claim compensation for the decrease in value.
This is analogous to jinayat al-ihram (penalties for ihram violations).
3
Analyze Exception 2: Closed Road by Judicial Order
A house has a door on a public road but its main door is in a dead-end alley. The residents prove they lent the road to the seller; the judge orders it closed. The buyer of the house may either return the house (full refund) or claim compensation for the decreased value. This choice is specific to this defect.
Here, the buyer still has the option to return, unlike the other exceptions.
4
Analyze Exception 3: Wine Purchased by Dhimmi Who Later Converts
A dhimmi buys wine, takes possession, and finds a defect. He then converts to Islam. Since a Muslim cannot lawfully own or return wine, the right to return drops. He can only claim compensation for the decrease.
The change of legal capacity renders return impossible.
5
Synthesize the Common Principle
In all three cases, returning the item would violate a higher legal principle: prohibition of ihram, judicial authority, or Islamic purity law. Therefore, the law substitutes compensation for return. This shows that khiyar al-ayb is not absolute but yields to greater legal objectives.
Understand that exceptions are limited and have clear legal causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى بيع صيد بين حلالين ثم أحرما أو أحدهما فوجد به عيباً امتنع رده وإنما يرجع بالنقصان كما صرحوا به في جنايات الإحرام . الثانية قال في البغية والقنية : لو كان في الدار باب في الطريق الأعظم وبابه في سكة غير نافذة أقام أهلها بينة أنهم أعاروا البائع هذا الطريق فأمر القاضي بسده يخير المشتري إن شاء رده وإن شاء رجع بنقصان ذلك الطريق والتخيير هنا بخلاف سائر العيوب اهـ. الثالثة اشترى الذمي خمراً وقبضها وبه ثم أسلم سقط خيار الرد ؛ كذا في مهر فتح القدير.
First: The sale of game between two permissible (non-muhrim) persons, then both or one of them enter ihram, and he finds a defect in it – he is prevented from returning it, and he only claims the decrease, as they clearly stated in the injuries of ihram. Second: He said in al-Bughyah and al-Qunyah: If there is in a house a door on the great road and its door is in a non-thoroughfare alley, its inhabitants establish a proof that they lent this road to the seller, and the judge orders its closure – the buyer is given a choice: if he wishes, he returns it; if he wishes, he claims the decrease of that road, and the choice here is contrary to other defects. Third: A dhimmi bought wine and received it, and it had a defect, then he converted to Islam – the option of return drops; like that in the dower of Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement listing three exceptions (mustathniyat) to the general rule that a buyer may return a defective item. First exception: If two non-muhrim hunters buy game (hunted animal), then both or one enter ihram, and later find a defect in the game, they cannot return it. They only claim compensation for the decrease in value because returning the game would involve violating ihram prohibitions (e.g., hunting or killing game while in ihram). Second exception: If a house has a door opening onto a public thoroughfare, but its main entrance is in a dead-end alley, and the residents prove they have lent the path to the seller, and the judge orders it closed – then the buyer of the house has a choice: either return the house (due to the defect of the road being closed) or claim compensation for the decrease. This choice is specific to this case. Third exception: If a dhimmi buys wine and takes possession, and the wine has a defect, then the dhimmi converts to Islam, his right to return the wine is forfeited because after conversion he cannot lawfully deal with wine; he can only claim the decrease.

Key Terms
مستثنيات (mustathniyat): Exceptional cases where the general ruling on khiyar al-ayb does not apply.
جنايات الإحرام (jinayat al-ihram): Acts that are prohibited in the state of ihram, such as hunting or cutting trees.
نقصان (nugsan): Compensation for the decrease in value due to the defect, as opposed to full refund.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A dhimmi buys wine, takes possession, finds a defect, and then converts to Islam. What is his remedy?
A) He can return the wine and get a full refund.
B) He can only claim compensation for the decrease in value.
C) He has no right to return or claim compensation because he converted.
D) He can return it only if he converts back.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ayb can be replaced by compensation when returning the item would violate a higher legal principle, as demonstrated by the three exceptions in this lesson.

Exception 4: Shroud for the Deceased – No Return of Defective Item
Exception to the right of return for shrouds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning a defective shroud bought for the deceased and distinguish when a claim for the decrease is possible.

Core Idea
A shroud consumed in burial cannot be returned, but a donor may claim the decrease from the estate.

In Hanafi sales law, the buyer's right to return a defective item (khiyar al-ayb) is suspended in certain cases. One such case is when the item is a shroud for the deceased. Since the shroud is consumed in burial, it cannot be returned. However, compensation may be available if the shroud was purchased with estate funds.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the general rule
Normally, a buyer can return a defective item. This exception is specific.
2
Identify the shroud exception
The shroud is consumed in burial; return is impossible physically and legally.
3
Distinguish donation vs. personal purchase
If an unrelated person (ajnabi) donated the shroud, they can claim the decrease from the estate. If the buyer paid from his own money, no claim.
The text says 'إن تبرع به أجنبي' – if an ajnabi donated it.
4
Apply to a scenario
If a son buys a shroud for his father using his own money and finds a defect after burial, he cannot return it and cannot claim compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة اشترى كفناً للميت ووجد به عيباً لا يرد ولا يرجع بالنقصان إن تبرع به أجنبي ولو وارثاً رجع بالنقص إن كان من التركة .اهـ. الخامسة اشترى من عبده المأذون المديون المستغرق فوجد به عيباً لا يرده عليه ولا على بائعه إن كان الثمن منقوداً وإن لم ينقده المولى وقبض المبيع أولاً ووجد به عيباً يرده إن كان الثمن من النقود أو كيلياً أو وزنياً بغير عينه لأنه يدفع بالرد مطالبة من نفسه وإن كان عرضاً لا يمكن الرد. وفي المحيط : لو اشترى المولى من مكاتبه فوجد به عيباً لا يرده ولا يرجع ولا يخاصم بائعه لكونه عبده .اهـ.
Fourth: He bought a shroud for the deceased and found a defect in it – he does not return it and does not claim the decrease. If an unrelated person donated it, even if an heir, he claims the decrease if it was from the estate. Fifth: He bought from his ma'dhun slave, who is in debt and whose assets are fully encumbered, and found a defect in it – he does not return it to him nor to his seller if the price was cash, and if the master did not pay the price, and he took possession of the sold item first, and found a defect, he returns it if the price was cash or a measured or weighed item not identified by its specific identity, because by returning he pushes the claim from himself; but if it was a specific item (non-fungible), he cannot return it. And in al-Muhit: If the master bought from his mukatab and found a defect, he does not return it, does not claim the decrease, and does not litigate with the seller because he is his slave.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement presenting two exceptions. Fourth exception: If someone buys a shroud for a deceased person (i.e., for the purpose of burial) and finds a defect, he does not return the shroud and does not claim the decrease (since the shroud is consumed in burial). However, if the shroud was donated by a non-heir (ajnabi), even if that donor is an heir, he can claim the decrease from the estate because the estate remains liable. Fifth exception: If a master buys something from his own ma'dhun slave (who is permitted to trade but is in debt and fully occupied by debts), and finds a defect, the ruling depends on the type of price. If the price was cash (naqdan) and the master did not pay it, he cannot return the defective item to the slave or to the slave's seller because he would be claiming from someone who has no assets (the slave). But if the price was cash but he first took possession and then found a defect, he can return it because he is essentially suing himself. If the price was a specific item (ard) that is non-fungible, he cannot return. The note from al-Muhit adds: if the master buys from his mukatab (slave who has contracted for manumission), he cannot return a defective item because the mukatab is still his slave and cannot be sued.

Key Terms
كفن (kafan): A shroud for the deceased; a specific item of clothing for burial.
أجنبي (ajnabi): An unrelated person; someone not directly paying from the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a shroud is bought for the deceased, buried, and later found defective, and the buyer used his own money, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The buyer can return it to the seller.
B) The buyer can claim the decrease from the seller.
C) The buyer cannot return it and cannot claim the decrease.
D) The buyer can only claim the decrease from the estate.

Key Takeaway
A shroud consumed in burial cannot be returned; only an ajnabi donor may claim the decrease from the estate.

Exception 5: Purchase from One's Ma'dhun Slave – Restrictions on Returning Defective Items
Exception to the right of return for purchases from one's own ma'dhun slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on returning a defective item purchased from one's own ma'dhun slave and differentiate based on the type of price and payment status.

Core Idea
A master's purchase from his debt-encumbered ma'dhun slave bars return of defective items when the price is cash and unpaid.

In Hanafi sales law, a master buying from his own ma'dhun slave faces a unique restriction on returning defective items. This is because the slave's assets are effectively the master's property, and suing the slave would be suing oneself. The ruling depends on whether the price is cash or a specific item, and whether payment has been made.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ma'dhun slave
A ma'dhun slave is permitted to trade, but his assets are fully encumbered by debts (mustaghraq). He is like a separate legal entity but owned by the master.
2
Apply the exception for cash price (unpaid)
If the price is cash (naqdan) and the master has not yet paid, he cannot return the defective item to the slave or the slave's seller, because he would be claiming from his own property.
The text says 'لا يرده عليه ولا على بائعه'.
3
Distinguish when cash price is paid
If the master has paid the price and taken possession, then found a defect, he can return it because he is essentially reclaiming his money from the slave's estate.
The text says 'يقبض المبيع أولاً ووجد به عيباً يرده'.
4
Distinguish specific items (ard)
If the price was a specific non-fungible item (ard), return is impossible regardless of payment status, because the slave cannot repossess a specific item from the master.
The text says 'وإن كان عرضاً لا يمكن الرد'.
5
Extend to mukatab
The same prohibition applies to a mukatab slave who has contracted for manumission: the master cannot return a defective item or litigate.
From al-Muhit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة اشترى كفناً للميت ووجد به عيباً لا يرد ولا يرجع بالنقصان إن تبرع به أجنبي ولو وارثاً رجع بالنقص إن كان من التركة .اهـ. الخامسة اشترى من عبده المأذون المديون المستغرق فوجد به عيباً لا يرده عليه ولا على بائعه إن كان الثمن منقوداً وإن لم ينقده المولى وقبض المبيع أولاً ووجد به عيباً يرده إن كان الثمن من النقود أو كيلياً أو وزنياً بغير عينه لأنه يدفع بالرد مطالبة من نفسه وإن كان عرضاً لا يمكن الرد. وفي المحيط : لو اشترى المولى من مكاتبه فوجد به عيباً لا يرده ولا يرجع ولا يخاصم بائعه لكونه عبده .اهـ.
Fourth: He bought a shroud for the deceased and found a defect in it – he does not return it and does not claim the decrease. If an unrelated person donated it, even if an heir, he claims the decrease if it was from the estate. Fifth: He bought from his ma'dhun slave, who is in debt and whose assets are fully encumbered, and found a defect in it – he does not return it to him nor to his seller if the price was cash, and if the master did not pay the price, and he took possession of the sold item first, and found a defect, he returns it if the price was cash or a measured or weighed item not identified by its specific identity, because by returning he pushes the claim from himself; but if it was a specific item (non-fungible), he cannot return it. And in al-Muhit: If the master bought from his mukatab and found a defect, he does not return it, does not claim the decrease, and does not litigate with the seller because he is his slave.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement presenting two exceptions. Fourth exception: If someone buys a shroud for a deceased person (i.e., for the purpose of burial) and finds a defect, he does not return the shroud and does not claim the decrease (since the shroud is consumed in burial). However, if the shroud was donated by a non-heir (ajnabi), even if that donor is an heir, he can claim the decrease from the estate because the estate remains liable. Fifth exception: If a master buys something from his own ma'dhun slave (who is permitted to trade but is in debt and fully occupied by debts), and finds a defect, the ruling depends on the type of price. If the price was cash (naqdan) and the master did not pay it, he cannot return the defective item to the slave or to the slave's seller because he would be claiming from someone who has no assets (the slave). But if the price was cash but he first took possession and then found a defect, he can return it because he is essentially suing himself. If the price was a specific item (ard) that is non-fungible, he cannot return. The note from al-Muhit adds: if the master buys from his mukatab (slave who has contracted for manumission), he cannot return a defective item because the mukatab is still his slave and cannot be sued.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave granted permission by his master to engage in trade.
مستغرق (mustaghraq): Fully encumbered by debts; the slave's assets are entirely consumed by his liabilities.
عرض ('ard): A specific, non-fungible item (as opposed to cash or generic goods).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master buys a defective item from his ma'dhun slave with a price of a specific goat (ard), and has not paid, can he return the item?
A) Yes, because it is defective.
B) No, because the price is not cash.
C) Yes, because he hasn't paid.
D) No, because the slave is his property.

Key Takeaway
A master's purchase from his debt-encumbered ma'dhun slave restricts the right to return defective items based on the type of price and whether it has been paid.

Three Exceptions in Khiyar al-Ayb: Slave Self-Sale, Inheritance, and Ma'dhun Release
Three exceptions in khiyar al-ayb with conditions and rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for each of the three exceptions to khiyar al-ayb and identify the conditions that trigger each exceptional ruling.

Core Idea
A seller who becomes owner through inheritance cannot return to himself; a ma'dhun slave released from debt cannot return the item.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq details three exceptional cases where the standard option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) is modified. These involve a slave buying himself, inheritance merging the buyer and seller, and a ma'dhun slave's debt forgiveness. Understanding these exceptions prevents contradictions in the general rules of khiyar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and list the three exceptions: slave self-sale (sixth), inheritance scenarios (seventh), and ma'dhun release (eighth).
Each exception is introduced by a number: السادسة, السابعة, الثامنة.
2
For the sixth exception, determine the ruling and the intra-Hanafi difference
Note that a slave buys his own self from another slave for a slave-girl, then finds a defect. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), he returns the slave-girl and takes the value of himself. Muhammad alone says he takes the value of the slave-girl.
The term 'عندهما' refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; 'عند محمد' refers to Muhammad's separate view.
3
For the seventh exception, analyze the inheritance scenarios
This exception covers several cases: an heir sells to his testator and then inherits the item; a father gives to his minor son; a father sells to his son; and a sale to an heir who later dies. In each, the seller becomes the owner, but the right to return depends on the presence of other heirs. If there are other heirs, return is possible via a judge-appointed opponent. If the seller is the sole heir, no return is allowed.
The text mentions 'رفع الأمر إلى القاضي حتى ينصب عن ابنه خصماً' – the judge appoints a representative for the minor or absent heir.
4
For the eighth exception, understand the effect of release from price
A ma'dhun slave buys something and the seller forgives the debt. If the forgiveness occurs after receipt, the slave cannot return the item for defect. If before receipt, he can return. The same applies to a free buyer: if the price is forgiven after receipt, return is barred; if before, return is allowed. This also applies to khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation).
The logic: release from price means the buyer no longer suffers a loss; thus the right to return is lost.
5
Synthesize the common principle
These exceptions show that khiyar al-ayb requires distinct parties and a genuine loss. When ownership merges (inheritance) or when the price is forgiven, the right to return is replaced by compensation or lost entirely.
The principle is that khiyar al-ayb is designed to protect the buyer from loss due to a hidden defect. If the buyer no longer bears a loss (e.g., he inherits the item and owns it fully) or does not actually pay (e.g., debt forgiven), the right is modified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة باع نفس العبد من العبد بجارية ثم وجد بها عيباً رد الجارية وأخذ من العبد قيمة نفسه عندهما، وعند محمد يرجع بقيمة الجارية السابعة باع الوارث من مورثه فمات المشتري وورثه البائع ووجد به عيباً رد إلى الوارث الآخر إن كان وإن لم يكن له سواه لا يرد ولا يرجع بالنقصان، وكذا إذا من ابنه الصغير شيئاً وقبضه وأشهد ثم وجد به عيباً يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي حتى ينصب عن ابنه خصماً يرده عليه ثم يرد الأب لابنه على بائعه، وكذا لو باع الأب من ابنه، وكذا لو باع من وارثه فورثه المشتري ووجد به عيباً يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي فينصب خصماً فيرده المشتري إليه ويرده القيم إلى الوارث نقده الثمن أو لا في الصحيح. الثامنة اشترى العبد المأذون شيئاً وأبرأه البائع عن الثمن لا يرده بالعيب وأن المشتري حراً لو بعد القبض فكذلك، وإن قبله فله الرد لأنه امتناع عن القبول وكذا خيار الشرط.
Sixth: A slave sold his own self (purchased itself from another slave) for a slave-girl, then found a defect in her – he returns the slave-girl and takes from the slave the value of himself according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); according to Muhammad, he claims the value of the slave-girl. Seventh: An heir sold to his testator (heir of someone), then the buyer died and the seller inherited him, and he found a defect – he returns to the other heir if there is one; if there is no other heir, he does not return and does not claim the decrease. Likewise, if he (the father) gave something to his minor son, took possession and had witnesses, then found a defect – he brings the matter to the judge so that he appoints for his son an opponent to return it to him, then the father returns it to his son's seller. Similarly, if a father sold to his son, and similarly if he sold to his heir and the buyer died and the seller inherited him, and he found a defect – he brings the matter to the judge who appoints an opponent for him, then the buyer (the inheritor) returns it to him, and the agent returns it to the heir, whether he paid the price or not, in the correct view. Eighth: A ma'dhun slave bought something and the seller released him from the price – he does not return it due to defect, and if the buyer were free, it is likewise after receipt; if (the release) was before receipt, he has the right to return because it is refusal of acceptance. And likewise the option of stipulation.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement covering three more exceptions to khiyar al-ayb. Sixth exception: A slave buys his own self from another slave using a slave-girl as the price. If he later finds a defect in the slave-girl, he can return the slave-girl and claim from the seller-slave the value of himself (i.e., the purchase price agreed upon). According to Imam Muhammad, he claims the value of the slave-girl (not his own value). Seventh exception: This deals with situations where the buyer and seller become the same person through inheritance or where the buyer is a minor child or heir. If an heir sells something to his testator (who dies later and the seller inherits the item), and a defect is found, the seller (now owner) can return the defective item to the other heirs if any; if he is the sole heir, he cannot return it to himself. Similarly, if a father gives something to his minor son and takes possession, then finds a defect, the father must go to the judge to have an opponent appointed for his son, then the father returns the item to the original seller on behalf of the son. The same applies if the father sold to his son or to an heir who later dies. Eighth exception: A ma'dhun slave buys something and the seller forgives the price. The slave cannot return the item due to defect because he no longer has to pay. If the buyer were free, the same applies if the release was after receipt; but if the release was before receipt, he can return because it is considered a refusal of ownership. This also applies to option of stipulation.

Key Terms
قيمة (qimah): Value or price of a commodity, often used in cases of return where compensation is paid instead of the specific item.
وارث (warith): Heir, someone who inherits from the deceased.
عبد مأذون (abd ma'dhun): A slave who has been granted permission by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a father sells an item to his son, and then the son finds a defect, what procedure must the son follow to return it?
A) He can directly return it to his father because they are related.
B) He must go to the judge who will appoint an opponent for him, then he returns it to the father's seller.
C) He cannot return it because it is a sale between father and son.
D) He can only claim compensation, not return.

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-ayb, the right to return is limited when seller and buyer become one through inheritance, when a slave buys himself, or when a ma'dhun is released from price.

Three Exceptions in Sales: Settlement, Quality, and Guardian Purchase
Three exceptions with conditional rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for three exceptional sale cases: settlement after defect, poor quality silver, and guardian purchases, and explain the Hanafi rationale for each.

Core Idea
Not all dissatisfaction with a purchased item gives a right to return; the law balances fairness with preventing harm and riba.

Building on the prior exceptions, this passage presents three more scenarios where the general rule of returning a defective item is restricted. The first involves settlement after a defect claim, the second concerns poor quality in silver vessels and ugliness in slave-girls, and the third deals with guardians and agents who buy at a bargain. Understanding these exceptions sharpens your grasp of the Hanafi approach to defects and the protection of vulnerable parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exceptions
Locate the three distinct cases: the ninth (settlement after defect), tenth (poor quality silver and slave-girl), and eleventh (guardian/agent buying cheaply).
The numbers (التاسعة, العاشرة, الحادي عشر) mark each exception.
2
Analyze the ninth exception: settlement validity
Determine why one form of settlement (seller pays something, buyer keeps item) is valid, while the other (buyer pays something, seller keeps item) is invalid because it resembles riba.
The key is that the buyer cannot receive both the item and a payment, as that would be similar to selling on credit with excess.
3
Analyze the tenth exception: scope of defect
Understand that poor quality without breakage or adulteration in a silver vessel is not a defect; similarly, ugliness in a slave-girl is not a defect unless there is a physical flaw.
This reflects the Hanafi reliance on custom (urf) in defining defects.
4
Analyze the eleventh exception: guardian protection
See why a guardian, agent, or ma'dhun slave cannot return a favorable purchase (bought cheap, worth more) because returning would harm the orphan, principal, or master by losing the bargain.
The exception does not apply if the option of stipulation or sight exists, as the transaction is not complete.
5
Synthesize the common principle
Connect the three exceptions: they all restrict the right of return to prevent harm (riba, loss of bargain, or overextending defects) and protect the integrity of contracts.
These rules are specific applications of the Hanafi maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by their objectives).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسعة لو اصطلحا على أن يدفع البائع شيئاً والمبيع للمشتري جاز بخلاف ما لو اصطلحا على أن يدفع المشتري شيئاً والجارية للبائع لا لأنه ربا والمسائل المذكورة من الرابعة إلى الثامنة في البزازية . العاشرة اشترى إناء فضة مشاراً إليها فوجده رديئاً ليس له الرد إلا إذا كان به كسر أو غش، وكذا إذا اشترى جارية فوجدها سوداء تام الخلقة ليس له الرد لأن القبح في الجواري ليس بعيب. الحادي عشر قال في المحيط وصي أو وكيل أو عبد مأذون اشترى شيئاً بألف وقيمته ثلاثة آلاف درهم فليس له أن يرده بالعيب لما فيه من الإضرار باليتيم والموكل والمولى، ولو كان في خيار الشرط والرؤية فله الرد لعدم تمام الصفقة اهـ.
Ninth: If they settle that the seller pays something and the sold item remains for the buyer, it is permissible, unlike if they settle that the buyer pays something and the slave-girl remains for the seller, no, because it is riba. And the cases mentioned from the fourth to the eighth are in al-Bazzaziyyah. Tenth: He bought a silver vessel, pointed out, and found it to be of poor quality – he has no right to return unless there is breakage or adulteration. Likewise, if he bought a slave-girl and found her black but complete in physique, he has no right to return because ugliness in slave-girls is not a defect. Eleventh: He said in al-Muhit: A guardian (wasi), agent (wakil), or ma'dhun slave bought something for one thousand dirhams while its value is three thousand dirhams – he may not return it due to defect because of the harm to the orphan, principal, or master; but if it was under option of stipulation or option of sight, he may return because the transaction is not complete.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement covering three more exceptions. Ninth exception: If the buyer and seller settle after a defect claim such that the seller gives some compensation and the buyer keeps the item, it is valid. However, if the settlement involves the buyer giving something and the seller keeping the item (i.e., the buyer pays to give back the item), it is invalid because it resembles riba (the seller gets extra payment without giving back the price). Tenth exception: A silver vessel that is of poor quality but has no breakage or adulteration is not considered a defect for return. Similarly, if a buyer purchases a slave-girl and finds her black-skinned but otherwise full-formed, he cannot return because ugliness in slave-girls is not a recognized defect (customarily). Eleventh exception: A guardian (wasi), agent (wakil), or ma'dhun slave who buys something at a price of 1000 dirhams while its market value is 3000 dirhams (i.e., a good deal) cannot return it due to a defect because that would harm the interests of the orphan, principal, or master (by losing the good bargain). However, if the option was khiyar al-shart or khiyar al-ru'yah, he can return because the transaction was not finalized.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): Settlement or compromise between parties. In this context, it is used to resolve a dispute over a defective item.
غش (ghish): Adulteration or fraud, e.g., silver mixed with base metal.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): Option of stipulation – a condition in the sale that allows one or both parties to rescind within a specified period.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): Option of sight – the right to rescind after seeing the item if it does not match the description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer and seller settle after the buyer claims a defect. Which of the following settlements is valid according to the ninth exception?
A) The buyer pays the seller an extra 10% and keeps the item.
B) The seller pays the buyer 10% of the price and the buyer keeps the item.
C) The buyer pays the seller 10% and returns the item to the seller.
D) The seller pays the buyer 10% and the buyer returns the item to the seller.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, the right to return for defect is restricted in three specific scenarios: to prevent riba in settlements, to adhere to custom in defining defects, and to protect the interests of dependents in bargain purchases.

Three Important Notes on Khiyar al-Ayb
Three rulings on cost of return, third-party guarantee, and settlement after claim of defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the expense of returning a defective item, the validity of third-party defect guarantees, and the effect of a settlement after a defect claim.

Core Idea
The buyer bears the cost of returning heavy defective items; third-party defect guarantees are void unless for specific defects; settlement money can be reclaimed if no defect existed.

Khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) gives the buyer the right to return a defective item. But what about the cost of returning a heavy item? Can a third party guarantee against defects? And if the buyer settles with the seller and later finds no defect, can the seller get his money back? These three practical questions are answered in this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first note: expense of return
Read the first note carefully. Note the condition 'al-ladhi lahu haml wa mu'nah' (that has carrying and expense). The ruling is: the buyer bears the expense of returning (mu'nat al-radd).
This applies to heavy items like furniture, not to small items that can be carried easily.
2
Analyze the second note: third-party guarantees
The second note covers multiple scenarios. First, a general guarantee of defects (daman al-uyub) by a third party: according to Abu Hanifa, it is void like daman al-uhdah. Then, specific guarantees for theft, freedom, insanity, or blindness: valid, and the guarantor pays the full price. Then, guarantee of a share of the defect: valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, with different recoveries depending on whether the item is returned or retained and damaged.
Pay attention to the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn.
3
Understand the third note: settlement and reclaim
The third note deals with a settlement (sulh) between buyer and seller on a defect claim. The buyer accepts some money in lieu of the defect. If later it appears there was no defect or the defect was exempted (e.g., the seller was not liable), the seller can recover the settlement money.
This ruling prevents the buyer from unjust enrichment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (تنبيهات مهمة الأولى وجد بالمبيع الذي له حمل ومؤنة عيباً ورده فمؤنة الرد على المشتري الثاني اشترى عبداً وتقابضا وضمن رجل له عيوبه فاطلع على عيب ورده لا ضمان عليه على قياس قول الإمام لأنه باطل كضمان العهدة، ولو ضمن له ضمان السرقة أو الحرية فوجده مسروقاً أو حراً أو الجنون أو العمى فوجده كذلك رجع على الضامن بالثمن، ولو مات عنده وقضى بالنقص رجع به علی ضامن الثمن، ولو ضمن له حصة ما يجده فيه من العيب جاز عند الإمامين إن رد رجع بالثمن كله، وإن تعيب عنده رجع بحصة العيب على الضامن كما يرجع على البائع، وإن ضمن ما لحقه كان الثمن من عهده هذا من البيع كذلك عند الإمام إن استحق رجع بالثمن الثالث ادعى عليه عيباً في المبيع فاصطلحا على أن يبذل البائع للمشتري مالاً ثم بان أنه لا عيب أو كان لكنه بريء استرد بدل الصلح اهـ.
His statement: (Important notes: First) If a defect is found in a sold item that has a heavy burden and expense, and he returns it, the expense of the return is upon the buyer. (Second) He bought a slave, they exchanged, and a man guaranteed the defects for him; then he discovered a defect and returned it – there is no liability upon the guarantor according to the analogy of the Imam's statement, because it is void like the guarantee of responsibility (daman al-uhdah). But if he guaranteed him the guarantee of theft or freedom, and he found it stolen or free, or insanity or blindness, and he found it so, he recovers the price from the guarantor. And if it died in his possession and he was awarded the decrease, he recovers it from the guarantor of the price. And if he guaranteed him the share of what he finds in it of the defect, it is permissible according to the two Imams: if he returns, he recovers all the price; if it became defective in his possession, he recovers the share of the defect from the guarantor as he recovers from the seller. And if he guaranteed what afflicts him, the price is from his responsibility – this from the sale is likewise according to the Imam: if it is reclaimed, he recovers the price. (Third) He claimed a defect in the sold item against him, and they settled that the seller pays some money to the buyer; then it became clear that there was no defect, or there was but it was exempt, he (the seller) gets back the settlement money.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement presenting three important notes (tanbihat muhimma) related to khiyar al-ayb. First note: If the sold item is heavy and has carrying expenses (like furniture), and the buyer returns it due to defect, the cost of returning is borne by the buyer, not the seller. Second note: This deals with third-party guarantees. If someone guarantees defects for a sold slave, and the buyer finds a defect and returns the slave, the guarantor is not liable for the price according to Abu Hanifa (by analogy to a void guarantee like daman al-uhdah). However, if the guarantee is specifically for theft or freedom (i.e., if the slave is found to be stolen or free), or for insanity or blindness, the guarantor is liable for the full price. If the slave dies and the buyer is awarded the decrease, he recovers that from the guarantor. If the guarantee is for a share of the defect found, it is valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: if the buyer returns the item, he recovers the full price from the guarantor; if the item becomes defective in his possession, he recovers only the share of the defect. If the guarantee is for any harm that occurs, then the price is on the guarantor; and according to Abu Hanifa, if the item is reclaimed (e.g., by a rightful owner), the buyer recovers the price from the guarantor. Third note: If the buyer claims a defect and they settle that the seller pays some money to the buyer, but later it is discovered that there was no defect or the defect was exempted, the seller can get back the settlement money.

Key Terms
مؤنة الرد (mu'nat al-radd): The expense incurred in returning a sold item, such as transportation costs.
ضمان العهدة (daman al-uhdah): A guarantee given by the seller or a third party to indemnify the buyer against defects or claims of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third note, if a buyer claims a defect and settles with the seller for a payment, but later it is discovered that there was no defect, what happens to the settlement money?
A) The buyer keeps it as a penalty.
B) The seller can reclaim it.
C) It is donated to charity.
D) The court decides.

Key Takeaway
Three key rules: buyer pays return cost for heavy items; third-party defect guarantees are void unless specific; settlement money is recoverable if defect claim is false.

Core Rulings on the Right to Return Defective Goods
First three observations and a slave buyer dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules for defect guarantees and settlement after a false claim, and explain why Abu Hanifa and the two companions differ on the authorized slave's option.

Core Idea
A defect guarantee is void; only specific guarantees for theft or freedom are valid, and slave buyers lose option upon price release.

This lesson unpacks the first three observations from the text, covering fundamental rules: who bears return costs, which guarantees are enforceable, and what happens when a defect claim is settled then disproven. We also delve into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an authorized slave's right to return after the seller releases the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cost of return
Find the first observation: if the sold item has carriage and expense, the buyer bears the cost of return. Note that this applies regardless of the seller's location.
This is a default rule; parties can agree otherwise.
2
Distinguish valid and void guarantees
In the second observation, three guarantee scenarios are given: a general defect guarantee is void per Abu Hanifa; a guarantee for specific defects (theft, slavery, blindness) is valid and the guarantor pays the price; a guarantee for a share of the defect is permissible according to the two companions.
The void guarantee (ضمان العهدة) is considered uncertain.
3
Analyze the settlement after a false claim
The third observation: if the seller pays the buyer to settle a defect claim, but the defect is later disproven or shown to be innocent, the seller can recover the settlement payment.
This prevents unjust enrichment.
4
Understand the slave buyer dispute
The text presents a case: an authorized slave buys an item, then the seller releases the price within the option period. Abu Hanifa holds the option remains; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it becomes void. Trace their reasoning: return is refusal to own vs. giving ownership without compensation.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon view) is not explicitly stated here but the fourth observation later favors deferment, which may affect this.
5
Extract the hukm for each scenario
Compile a table: first observation → buyer pays return cost; second observation → void guarantee no liability, valid guarantee full liability; third observation → seller recovers settlement if no defect; slave dispute → option remains per Abu Hanifa, void per Sahibayn.
These rulings are all from the Hanafi school, with internal difference on the slave case.

العهدة، ولو ضمن له ضمان السرقة أو الحرية فوجده مسروقاً أو حراً أو الجنون أو العمى فوجده كذلك رجع على الضامن بالثمن، ولو مات عنده وقضى بالنقص رجع به علی ضامن الثمن، ولو ضمن له حصة ما يجده فيه من العيب جاز عند الإمامين إن رد رجع بالثمن كله، وإن تعيب عنده رجع بحصة العيب على الضامن كما يرجع على البائع، وإن ضمن ما لحقه كان الثمن من عهده هذا من البيع كذلك عند الإمام إن استحق رجع بالثمن الثالث ادعى عليه
