مأذوناً فأبرأه البائع عن الثمن في المدة بقي خياره عنده لأن الرد امتناع عن التملك والمأذون له يليه. وعندهما بطل خياره لأنه لما ملكه كان الرد منه تمليكاً بغير عوض وهو ليس من أهله اهـ . فتأمل. قوله : (الحادي عشر ) قال في المحيط وقع في بعض النسخ التعبير عنها بالعاشرة فذكر العاشرة مرتين، وبعد هذه العاشرة وقد ذكر الحادية عشر والثانية عشر إلى الخامسة عشر الآتية في التنبيهات. وظاهر كلام الرملي أن نسخته كذلك وهي غلط من الكاتب لأن الكلام في المسائل المستثناة من إطلاقهم التخيير بين المعيب بكل الثمن أو رذه والمسائل الخمس الآتية ليست . ذلك ما في ذكر العاشر مرتين كما أخذت علمته، فالصواب ذكرها بعد العاشر من التنبيهات المهمة كما في هذه النسخة الموافقة لأغلب النسخ الرابع اطلع على عيب بالغلام أو الدابة فلم يجد المالك فأطعمه وأمسكه ولم يتصرف فيه بما يدل على الرضا يرده لو حضر ويرجع بالنقصان إن هلك. وفي الحاوي القدسي : إنه إذا بعد الاطلاع على العيب مع قدرته على الرد كان رضا وهو غريب، والمعتمد أنه على التراخي. الخامس اطلع على عيب فأعلم القاضي وبرهن على الشراء والعيب فوضعها القاضي عند عدل وماتت عنده ثم حضر البائع؛ إن كان لم يقض بالرد على الغائب لم يرجع عليه بالثمن، وإن كان قضى رجع لأن للقضاء نفاذاً في الأظهر عن أصحابنا. وفي السير : اشترى دابة في دار الإسلام وخرج عليها غازياً واطلع على عيب بغيبة البائع لا يركبها وإن في دار الحرب لأنه رضا وإن أمره الإمام لكن إذا قضى بأن الركوب ليس برضا نفذ وأمضاه القاضي الثاني. السادس خاصم البائع في العيب ثم ترك الخصوصة زمناً وزعم أن الترك كان لينظر هو عيب أم لا له الرد السابع أقر المشتري بعدما اطلع على عيب أو قبله أن المبيع لفلان غير البائع وكذبه فلان له الرد على البائع، وتمام مسائل الإقرار للغير بالمبيع مذكورة في الولوالجية. الثامن عثر على عيب فقال للبائع إن لم أرد إليك اليوم رضيت به قال محمد: القول باطل وله الرد التاسع قال البائع ركبتها بعد العثور على العيب في حاجتك وقال المشتري بل ركبتها لأردها عليك فالقول قول المشتري العاشر اطلع على عيب قبل القبض فقال المشتري للبائع رددته عليك بطل البيع قبل البائع أولاً، والكل من البزازية. وفي السراج الوهاج وإن قال ذلك بعد القبض لم يكن ذلك رداً ما لم يقل البائع قبلت أو رضيت ثم إذا رده برضا البائع كان فسخاً في حقهما بيعاً في حق غيرهما اهـ. وإن رده بحكم فهو فسخ عام. وكذا كل عقد ينفسخ، بالرد ويكون المردود مضموناً بما يقابله ؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين. وفي القنية : اشترى حماراً ووجد به عيباً قديماً فأراد الرد فصولح بينهما بدينار وأخذه ثم وجد به عيباً قديماً آخر فله رده مع الدينار. ثم رقم لآخر أنه يرجع بنقصان العيب وعنه أنه يرده اهـ. الحادي عشر باع بعيراً فوجده المشتري معيباً فرده فقال له البائع اذهب فتعهده إلى عشرة أيام فإن برئ فلك البعير، وإن هلك فمن مالي لا يكون رداً؛ كذا في القنية الثاني عشر المشتري إذا رد المبيع بالعيب فإنه يرجع بالثمن على بائعه إلا في مسألة في القنية : باع عبداً وسلمه ثم وكل وكيلاً بقبض الثمن
Important observations: First: If a defect is found in the sold item that has carriage and expense, and he returns it, the cost of return is on the buyer. Second: He bought a slave, they took possession, and a man guaranteed its defects; he found a defect and returned it; there is no liability on him according to the analogy of Imam's statement because it is void like a guarantee of warranty. And if he guaranteed him against theft or slavery, and he found it stolen or free, or insanity or blindness, and he found it so, he recovers the price from the guarantor. And if it died with him and he was awarded the deficiency, he recovers it from the guarantor of the price. And if he guaranteed him a share of the defect he finds in it, it is permissible according to the two Imams; if he returns, he recovers the whole price; and if it becomes defective with him, he recovers the share of the defect from the guarantor just as he recovers from the seller. And if he guaranteed what overtook him as the price from his warranty, this from the sale is likewise according to the Imam; if it is claimed, he recovers the price. Third: He claimed a defect in the sold item against him, and they settled that the seller would pay the buyer some money, then it became clear that there was no defect, or there was but it is innocent, he recovers the substitute for the settlement. End. The ownership of the buyer and its absence concerning if he had the option; he mentioned among the issues: If the buyer was an authorized slave, and the seller released him from the price during the period, his option remains according to him because the return is a refusal to own, and the authorized one may undertake it. And according to them, his option becomes void because when he owned it, the return from him is giving ownership without compensation, and he is not qualified for it. End. So consider. His statement: (The eleventh) He said in al-Muhit: In some copies, the expression for it is the tenth, so the tenth is mentioned twice, and after this tenth, the eleventh and twelfth up to the fifteenth have been mentioned in the observations. And the apparent meaning of al-Ramli's statement is that his copy was like that, and it is a copyist's error because the discussion is about the excepted issues from their general statement of choosing between the defective for the full price or its return, and the five issues that follow are not that. That is why the tenth was mentioned twice, as you have learned. So the correct is to mention them after the tenth of the important observations as in this copy agreeing with most copies. Fourth: He discovered a defect in the boy or the riding beast, and did not find the owner, so he fed it and held it, and did not act with it in a way indicating consent; he may return it when the owner appears, and he recovers the deficiency if it perishes. And in al-Hawi al-Qudsi: If after discovering the defect while able to return, it is consent, which is strange, and the relied-upon opinion is that it is on deferment. Fifth: He discovered a defect, informed the qadi, proved the purchase and the defect, so the qadi placed it with a trustworthy person, and it died with him, then the seller appeared; if he had not judged the return against the absent one, he does not recover the price from him; if he judged, he recovers because the judgment has effect in the most apparent [view] from our companions. And in al-Siyar: He bought a riding beast in Dar al-Islam and went out on it as a warrior, and discovered a defect while the seller was absent; he should not ride it, even if in Dar al-Harb because it is consent, even if the Imam commanded him; but if the qadi judged that riding is not consent, it takes effect and the second qadi ratifies it. Sixth: He disputed with the seller about the defect, then left the dispute for some time, and claimed that the leave was to see if it is a defect or not; he may return it. Seventh: The buyer admitted, after discovering the defect or before it, that the sold item belongs to someone other than the seller, and that person denied him; he may return it to the seller. And the complete issues of admission of the sold item to another are mentioned in al-Walwaljiyya. Eighth: He came upon a defect and said to the seller, 'If I do not return it to you today, I accept it.' Muhammad said: The statement is void, and he may return it. Ninth: The seller said, 'You rode it after finding the defect for your need,' and the buyer said, 'Rather I rode it to return it to you'; the statement is that of the buyer. Tenth: He discovered a defect before taking possession, and the buyer said to the seller, 'I have returned it to you'; the sale is void whether the seller first [accepted] or not; all from al-Bazzaziyya. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If he said that after taking possession, it is not a return unless the seller says 'I accept' or 'I am pleased'; then if he returns it with the seller's consent, it is a rescission in their right and a sale in the right of others. And if he returns it by a judgment, it is a general rescission. And likewise every contract is rescinded by return, and the returned item is guaranteed by what corresponds to it; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And in al-Qunyah: He bought a donkey and found an old defect in it, so he desired to return it; they settled between them for a dinar, and he took it, then he found another old defect; he may return it with the dinar. Then it is annotated for another that he recovers the deficiency of the defect, and from him that he returns it. End. Eleventh: He sold a camel, and the buyer found it defective and returned it; then the seller said to him, 'Go and take care of it for ten days; if it recovers, it is your camel; if it perishes, it is from my wealth'; that is not a return; as in al-Qunyah. Twelfth: If the buyer returns the sold item due to a defect, he recovers the price from his seller except in one issue in al-Qunyah: He sold a slave and delivered him, then appointed an agent to receive the price.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of important observations (تنبيهات مهمة) from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the option of defect (خيار العيب) in sales. The author presents a series of rulings, each addressing a specific scenario where the right to return a defective item is either affirmed, restricted, or negated. The first observation states that if the sold item has associated carrying charges (حمل ومؤنة), the buyer bears the cost of returning it. The second observation deals with guarantees: if someone guarantees the defects of a slave and the buyer returns it, the guarantor is not liable according to Abu Hanifa because a defect guarantee (ضمان العهدة) is void; however, if the guarantee is for specific issues like theft or slavery, the guarantor pays the price. The third observation covers settlement after a defect claim: if the seller pays the buyer to settle and later it turns out there was no defect, the seller can recover the payment. Following these, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the buyer is an authorized slave and the seller releases him from the price within the option period, Abu Hanifa says his option remains because return is a refusal to own, which the slave can do; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is void because the slave owns the item and return would be giving ownership without compensation, which he cannot do. The author then notes a textual error in some copies where the numbering is confused, clarifying the correct order. The fourth observation rules that if a buyer discovers a defect but cannot find the seller and feeds the animal without indicating satisfaction, he can still return it and recover the deficiency if it perishes; the relied-upon view is that the option remains for a reasonable time. The fifth observation involves the qadi: if the buyer informs the qadi and the item dies in trust, the seller is only liable for the price if the qadi had already ruled on the return; the ruling has effect against absent parties in the most apparent view. The sixth observation says that if the buyer sues for defect then drops the case to investigate, he retains the right to return. The seventh observation: if the buyer admits the item belongs to someone else (not the seller) and that person denies it, the buyer can still return it to the seller. The eighth observation: Muhammad rules that if the buyer says 'if I don't return it today, I accept it,' the statement is void and he can still return it. The ninth observation: if there is a dispute over riding the animal after the defect is found, the buyer's word is taken. The tenth observation: if the buyer says 'I return it' before taking possession, the sale is void; after possession, it's not a return unless the seller accepts. The eleventh observation: if the seller says 'take care of it for ten days' after return, it is not a valid return; the buyer can still return it later if he finds another defect. The twelfth observation: the general rule is that the buyer recovers the full price upon return, but there is an exception in al-Qunyah involving agency for price collection. Throughout, the author emphasizes conditions and exceptions, demonstrating the Hanafi school's careful balancing of the buyer's right to return and the seller's interest in finality.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The buyer's right to return a defective item or demand a price reduction.
ضمان العهدة (daman al-'uhdah): A guarantee by a third party for defects in the sold item; considered void by Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second observation, if a third party guarantees the buyer against 'theft' of the slave, and the slave turns out to be stolen, what is the guarantor's liability?
A) No liability, because all guarantees are void.
B) The guarantor must pay the full price.
C) The guarantor must pay only half the price.
D) The guarantor must compensate the buyer for time wasted.

Key Takeaway
General defect guarantees are void; only specific guarantees are enforceable, and the authorized slave's option is contested.

Actions and Conditions Affecting the Right to Return Defects
Observations 4-12 on timing, qadi, and acts affecting the option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions void the right to return and apply the rules concerning judicial involvement and timing.

Core Idea
The buyer's right to return is forfeited by acts indicating satisfaction; the qadi's ruling can bind absent parties.

This lesson covers observations 4 through 12, which focus on practical scenarios: feeding and holding an animal, involvement of the qadi, dropping a suit, admission of ownership to another, rider disputes, conditional acceptance, return timing, and specific exceptions. You will learn the delicate balance between protecting the buyer and respecting the finality of sales.

Guided Path
1
Understand deferment vs. immediate return
The fourth observation: if the buyer cannot find the seller, he may feed and hold the animal without losing the right. The relied-upon view is that the option is on deferment (على التراخي), meaning the buyer can return later even if able to return immediately.
Contrast with the opinion in al-Hawi al-Qudsi that immediate ability to return constitutes consent.
2
Analyze the effect of a qadi's ruling
The fifth observation: if the buyer informs the qadi and the item dies in trust, the seller is only liable for the price if the qadi had already issued a judgment of return. The ruling is effective against absent parties in the most apparent view.
This highlights the authority of the qadi in protecting rights.
3
Identify actions that forfeit the right
Observations 6-9: dropping the dispute to investigate (does not forfeit), admitting the item belongs to another (does not forfeit if that person denies), making a conditional acceptance (void, right remains), and riding the animal for personal need (buyer's word taken as to purpose).
The general rule: any act indicating acceptance voids the right; but these exceptions show nuance.
4
Distinguish return before and after possession
The tenth observation: if the buyer says 'I return it' before taking possession, the sale is void immediately. After possession, it is not a return unless the seller accepts; if accepted, it is a rescission between them but a sale in the eyes of third parties.
This shows the importance of possession in determining legal effects.
5
Apply the exception in price recovery
The twelfth observation: the general rule is that the buyer recovers the full price upon return, except in one case in al-Qunyah involving agency for price collection.
The exact exception is not detailed here, but it signals that the rule is not absolute.

Important observations: First: If a defect is found in the sold item that has carriage and expense, and he returns it, the cost of return is on the buyer. Second: He bought a slave, they took possession, and a man guaranteed its defects; he found a defect and returned it; there is no liability on him according to the analogy of Imam's statement because it is void like a guarantee of warranty. And if he guaranteed him against theft or slavery, and he found it stolen or free, or insanity or blindness, and he found it so, he recovers the price from the guarantor. And if it died with him and he was awarded the deficiency, he recovers it from the guarantor of the price. And if he guaranteed him a share of the defect he finds in it, it is permissible according to the two Imams; if he returns, he recovers the whole price; and if it becomes defective with him, he recovers the share of the defect from the guarantor just as he recovers from the seller. And if he guaranteed what overtook him as the price from his warranty, this from the sale is likewise according to the Imam; if it is claimed, he recovers the price. Third: He claimed a defect in the sold item against him, and they settled that the seller would pay the buyer some money, then it became clear that there was no defect, or there was but it is innocent, he recovers the substitute for the settlement. End. The ownership of the buyer and its absence concerning if he had the option; he mentioned among the issues: If the buyer was an authorized slave, and the seller released him from the price during the period, his option remains according to him because the return is a refusal to own, and the authorized one may undertake it. And according to them, his option becomes void because when he owned it, the return from him is giving ownership without compensation, and he is not qualified for it. End. So consider. His statement: (The eleventh) He said in al-Muhit: In some copies, the expression for it is the tenth, so the tenth is mentioned twice, and after this tenth, the eleventh and twelfth up to the fifteenth have been mentioned in the observations. And the apparent meaning of al-Ramli's statement is that his copy was like that, and it is a copyist's error because the discussion is about the excepted issues from their general statement of choosing between the defective for the full price or its return, and the five issues that follow are not that. That is why the tenth was mentioned twice, as you have learned. So the correct is to mention them after the tenth of the important observations as in this copy agreeing with most copies. Fourth: He discovered a defect in the boy or the riding beast, and did not find the owner, so he fed it and held it, and did not act with it in a way indicating consent; he may return it when the owner appears, and he recovers the deficiency if it perishes. And in al-Hawi al-Qudsi: If after discovering the defect while able to return, it is consent, which is strange, and the relied-upon opinion is that it is on deferment. Fifth: He discovered a defect, informed the qadi, proved the purchase and the defect, so the qadi placed it with a trustworthy person, and it died with him, then the seller appeared; if he had not judged the return against the absent one, he does not recover the price from him; if he judged, he recovers because the judgment has effect in the most apparent [view] from our companions. And in al-Siyar: He bought a riding beast in Dar al-Islam and went out on it as a warrior, and discovered a defect while the seller was absent; he should not ride it, even if in Dar al-Harb because it is consent, even if the Imam commanded him; but if the qadi judged that riding is not consent, it takes effect and the second qadi ratifies it. Sixth: He disputed with the seller about the defect, then left the dispute for some time, and claimed that the leave was to see if it is a defect or not; he may return it. Seventh: The buyer admitted, after discovering the defect or before it, that the sold item belongs to someone other than the seller, and that person denied him; he may return it to the seller. And the complete issues of admission of the sold item to another are mentioned in al-Walwaljiyya. Eighth: He came upon a defect and said to the seller, 'If I do not return it to you today, I accept it.' Muhammad said: The statement is void, and he may return it. Ninth: The seller said, 'You rode it after finding the defect for your need,' and the buyer said, 'Rather I rode it to return it to you'; the statement is that of the buyer. Tenth: He discovered a defect before taking possession, and the buyer said to the seller, 'I have returned it to you'; the sale is void whether the seller first [accepted] or not; all from al-Bazzaziyya. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If he said that after taking possession, it is not a return unless the seller says 'I accept' or 'I am pleased'; then if he returns it with the seller's consent, it is a rescission in their right and a sale in the right of others. And if he returns it by a judgment, it is a general rescission. And likewise every contract is rescinded by return, and the returned item is guaranteed by what corresponds to it; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And in al-Qunyah: He bought a donkey and found an old defect in it, so he desired to return it; they settled between them for a dinar, and he took it, then he found another old defect; he may return it with the dinar. Then it is annotated for another that he recovers the deficiency of the defect, and from him that he returns it. End. Eleventh: He sold a camel, and the buyer found it defective and returned it; then the seller said to him, 'Go and take care of it for ten days; if it recovers, it is your camel; if it perishes, it is from my wealth'; that is not a return; as in al-Qunyah. Twelfth: If the buyer returns the sold item due to a defect, he recovers the price from his seller except in one issue in al-Qunyah: He sold a slave and delivered him, then appointed an agent to receive the price.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of important observations (تنبيهات مهمة) from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the option of defect (خيار العيب) in sales. The author presents a series of rulings, each addressing a specific scenario where the right to return a defective item is either affirmed, restricted, or negated. The first observation states that if the sold item has associated carrying charges (حمل ومؤنة), the buyer bears the cost of returning it. The second observation deals with guarantees: if someone guarantees the defects of a slave and the buyer returns it, the guarantor is not liable according to Abu Hanifa because a defect guarantee (ضمان العهدة) is void; however, if the guarantee is for specific issues like theft or slavery, the guarantor pays the price. The third observation covers settlement after a defect claim: if the seller pays the buyer to settle and later it turns out there was no defect, the seller can recover the payment. Following these, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the buyer is an authorized slave and the seller releases him from the price within the option period, Abu Hanifa says his option remains because return is a refusal to own, which the slave can do; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is void because the slave owns the item and return would be giving ownership without compensation, which he cannot do. The author then notes a textual error in some copies where the numbering is confused, clarifying the correct order. The fourth observation rules that if a buyer discovers a defect but cannot find the seller and feeds the animal without indicating satisfaction, he can still return it and recover the deficiency if it perishes; the relied-upon view is that the option remains for a reasonable time. The fifth observation involves the qadi: if the buyer informs the qadi and the item dies in trust, the seller is only liable for the price if the qadi had already ruled on the return; the ruling has effect against absent parties in the most apparent view. The sixth observation says that if the buyer sues for defect then drops the case to investigate, he retains the right to return. The seventh observation: if the buyer admits the item belongs to someone else (not the seller) and that person denies it, the buyer can still return it to the seller. The eighth observation: Muhammad rules that if the buyer says 'if I don't return it today, I accept it,' the statement is void and he can still return it. The ninth observation: if there is a dispute over riding the animal after the defect is found, the buyer's word is taken. The tenth observation: if the buyer says 'I return it' before taking possession, the sale is void; after possession, it's not a return unless the seller accepts. The eleventh observation: if the seller says 'take care of it for ten days' after return, it is not a valid return; the buyer can still return it later if he finds another defect. The twelfth observation: the general rule is that the buyer recovers the full price upon return, but there is an exception in al-Qunyah involving agency for price collection. Throughout, the author emphasizes conditions and exceptions, demonstrating the Hanafi school's careful balancing of the buyer's right to return and the seller's interest in finality.

Key Terms
على التراخي ('ala al-tarakhi): On deferment; the option to return is not lost by mere passage of time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fifth observation, if a buyer discovers a defect and informs the qadi, who places the item in trust, and it dies before the qadi issues a judgment, what is the buyer's recourse?
A) He can recover the price because the qadi was notified.
B) He cannot recover the price because no judgment was given.
C) He can recover half the price.
D) He can recover only if the seller was present.

Key Takeaway
The right to return is preserved by deferment, but acts of satisfaction forfeit it; judicial involvement requires a prior ruling to bind the absent seller.

Buyer's Option After Feeding Defective Item Without Acceptance
Ruling on buyer keeping defective item without acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that feeding and keeping a defective item without an act of acceptance preserves the option to return.

Core Idea
Feeding a defective item without acceptance does not forfeit the buyer's right to return.

When a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased slave or animal, and the seller is unavailable, the buyer may be tempted to care for the item until the seller returns. This lesson clarifies that merely feeding and keeping the item does not imply acceptance, and the buyer retains the option to return it and claim compensation for any depreciation or loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Focus on the buyer's conduct: he discovers the defect, cannot find the seller, then feeds and keeps the item without any action that indicates acceptance (e.g., using it for work).
The item is a slave or animal, but the principle applies to any defective good.
2
State the ruling
The buyer may return the item when the seller appears and claim compensation (the difference in value due to the defect) if the item perishes in the meantime.
The right to return is preserved.
3
Identify the condition for forfeiture
An act that indicates acceptance (e.g., using the item for personal benefit) would forfeit the option. Feeding alone does not qualify.
The Hanafi standard: only clear signs of contentment (rida) forfeit the option.
4
Analyze the dispute
Al-Hawi al-Qudsi held that the mere ability to return after discovery constitutes acceptance, but this view is deemed strange (gharib) and not relied upon.
The mu'tamad (relied-upon) position is that the option is on delay (tarakhi); the buyer has a reasonable time.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
For fatwa, the Hanafi position is that the buyer does not lose the option by simply feeding the item while awaiting the seller.
The buyer must avoid any action that manifests acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع اطلع على عيب بالغلام أو الدابة فلم يجد المالك فأطعمه وأمسكه ولم يتصرف فيه بما يدل على الرضا يرده لو حضر ويرجع بالنقصان إن هلك. وفي الحاوي القدسي : إنه إذا بعد الاطلاع على العيب مع قدرته على الرد كان رضا وهو غريب، والمعتمد أنه على التراخي.
The fourth: He discovered a defect in the slave or beast and did not find the owner, so he fed it and kept it and did not act with it in a manner indicating acceptance; he may return it when the owner appears and claim compensation for the defect if it perishes. And in al-Hawi al-Qudsi: If he discovers the defect while able to return it, that is considered acceptance, but it is strange; and the relied-upon opinion is that it is on delayed basis.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on option of defect. The case: a buyer finds a defect in a slave or animal, cannot find the seller, but feeds and keeps the item without any act that implies acceptance (like using it for work). The Hanafi position is that he may still return it when the seller appears, and if it perishes, he claims the difference in value due to the defect. The text notes a strange view from al-Hawi al-Qudsi that the mere ability to return after discovering the defect constitutes acceptance, but the reliable (mu'tamad) position is that the option is not lost immediately; rather the buyer has time (tarakhi) to decide.

Key Terms
على التراخي (ala al-tarakhi): The option is not forfeited immediately; the buyer has a reasonable time to decide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fourth case, if a buyer finds a defect in a horse and feeds it while the seller is absent, can he still return the horse later?
A) No, because feeding indicates acceptance.
B) Yes, unless he also rode it for his own benefit.
C) Yes, as long as he did not act in a manner that implies acceptance.
D) No, the option is lost immediately upon discovery.

Key Takeaway
Feeding a defective item without acceptance does not forfeit the option; the buyer retains the right to return.

Buyer's Right to Return After Dropping a Defect Lawsuit
Ruling on a buyer who litigates then drops the case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a buyer who initiates a lawsuit over a defect then drops it, and identify the condition that preserves the right of return.

Core Idea
Dropping a lawsuit does not forfeit the right to return if the buyer was investigating the defect.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in sales: a buyer files a lawsuit over a defect but then drops it. Does this action forfeit the right to return the item? The Hanafi school provides a clear rule that protects the buyer's right under a specific condition. We will explore the ruling and its underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the buyer's action
The buyer initially litigated over a defect, then dropped the lawsuit.
The sequence matters: litigation followed by a drop.
2
Identify the claim
The buyer claims the purpose of dropping the lawsuit was to investigate whether the flaw is actually a defect.
The claim must be about investigation, not other reasons.
3
State the ruling
The buyer retains the right to return (al-رد) if his claim is plausible.
The right is not automatically lost by the act of litigating.
4
Understand the basis
The Hanafi school considers the buyer's stated intent; mere litigation does not imply acceptance.
Intent matters in this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس خاصم البائع في العيب ثم ترك الخصوصة زمناً وزعم أن الترك كان لينظر هو عيب أم لا له الرد
The sixth: He litigated with the seller over the defect, then left the lawsuit for a time and claimed that the leaving was in order to see whether it is a defect or not; he has the right to return.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that initiating a lawsuit and then dropping it does not necessarily imply acceptance. If the buyer claims the purpose of the delay was to investigate whether the flaw is actually a defect, his right to return remains intact. The Hanafi school gives weight to the buyer's stated intent as long as it is plausible.

Key Terms
الخصوصة (al-khususah): Lawsuit or legal dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In case 6, if a buyer files a lawsuit over a defect but then drops it, can he still return the item?
A) No, because filing a lawsuit shows acceptance.
B) Yes, if he can prove the defect existed at sale.
C) Yes, if he dropped the lawsuit to investigate the defect.
D) No, because he had his chance in court.

Key Takeaway
A buyer who drops a lawsuit over a defect can still return the item if the purpose was to investigate the defect.

Buyer's Acknowledgment and Right to Return for Defect
Ruling on buyer's acknowledgment that seller is not true owner and its effect on defect return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a buyer's acknowledgment that the seller is not the true owner and explain why the right to return for a defect remains when the alleged owner denies it.

Core Idea
A buyer's admission that the seller is not the owner does not void the right to return for defect if the alleged owner denies it.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a subtle case in sales: the buyer admits the sold item belongs to someone else, but that person denies it. The Hanafi ruling protects the buyer’s option (khiyar al-‘ayb) despite this admission. Understanding this helps you see how acknowledgments (iqrar) interact with contractual rights in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The buyer acknowledges (after discovering a defect or before) that the sold item belongs to a person other than the seller.
The acknowledgment can occur at any time – before or after discovery of the defect.
2
Check the alleged owner's response
The alleged true owner denies the buyer's claim – i.e., he says the item is not his.
This denial is crucial; it prevents the acknowledgment from becoming conclusive.
3
State the ruling
The buyer retains the right to return the item to the seller for the defect (khiyar al-‘ayb).
The return is against the seller, not the alleged owner.
4
Understand the legal reasoning
The acknowledgment is not binding because the alleged owner denies it. Therefore, it does not affect the buyer's option.
The principle: iqrar lil-ghayr (acknowledgment for another) requires confirmation by that other to be effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع أقر المشتري بعدما اطلع على عيب أو قبله أن المبيع لفلان غير البائع وكذبه فلان له الرد على البائع، وتمام مسائل الإقرار للغير بالمبيع مذكورة في الولوالجية.
The seventh: The buyer acknowledged after discovering a defect or before it that the sold item belongs to someone other than the seller, and that person (the true owner) denied it; he has the right to return against the seller. The complete issues of acknowledgment for the item belonging to another are mentioned in al-Walwaljiyya.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This case addresses a situation where the buyer admits that the seller is not the true owner, but the alleged true owner denies this. The Hanafi ruling: this acknowledgment does not forfeit the buyer's right to return the item to the seller for a defect. The reasoning: the acknowledgment is not conclusive because the alleged owner denies title, so the buyer's option remains. The text refers to al-Walwaljiyya for further details on similar acknowledgments.

Key Terms
الإقرار للغير (al-iqrar lil-ghayr): Acknowledgment that the property belongs to someone else.
الرد (al-radd): Return of the sold item to the seller due to a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because he has admitted the seller has no title.
B) Yes, because the alleged owner denied it, so the admission is ineffective.
C) No, only the true owner can return the item.
D) Yes, but only if the defect was discovered before the acknowledgment.

Key Takeaway
A buyer's acknowledgment that the seller is not the true owner does not affect the right to return for a defect if the alleged owner denies it.

Case 8: Condition to Return by End of Day
Ruling on buyer's conditional statement: 'If I don't return today, I accept'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a buyer's conditional statement that appears to waive his right of return, and identify the legal effect of such a statement.

Core Idea
A conditional promise to accept a defective good is void; the buyer's right to return remains intact.

In this case, the buyer discovers a defect in the purchased item and says to the seller: 'If I do not return it to you today, I accept it.' Does this statement forfeit his right to return? Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani rules that the statement is void and the buyer retains his right of return. This lesson examines the Hanafi principle behind this ruling and its implications for sales contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the buyer's statement
Read the buyer's conditional statement: 'If I do not return it to you today, I accept it.' Note that it is a promise conditioned on inaction (failure to return by the end of the day).
This is not an explicit and unconditional waiver of the option.
2
State the ruling of Imam Muhammad
Imam Muhammad declares that this statement (القول) is batil (void). Consequently, the buyer retains the right to return the defective item (وله الرد).
The condition does not create any legal effect.
3
Understand the legal effect
Because the statement is void, it does not constitute a valid waiver of the option of defect. The buyer's right to return is preserved unchanged.
The underlying contract remains valid, and the seller must accept the return if the defect is proven.
4
Extract the Hanafi principle
A conditional promise that attempts to restrict a God-given right (like the option of defect) is ineffective. A valid waiver must be explicit, unconditional, and made with full knowledge.
This protects buyers from inadvertently forfeiting their rights through informal statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن عثر على عيب فقال للبائع إن لم أرد إليك اليوم رضيت به قال محمد: القول باطل وله الرد
The eighth: He stumbled upon a defect and said to the seller, 'If I do not return it to you today, I am satisfied with it.' Muhammad said: 'The statement is void, and he has the right to return.'
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This case presents a statement by Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani). If the buyer conditions his option on returning by the end of the day, saying that failure to return implies acceptance, this condition is null and void. The buyer's right to return for the defect is not forfeited. The Hanafi principle: one cannot unilaterally impose a binding condition that restricts the God-given right of option (khiyar), and such a statement is treated as a vain promise.

Key Terms
باطل (batil): Void and legally ineffective; a condition that produces no legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Abu Hanifa
B) Imam Muhammad
C) Abu Yusuf
D) The judge in the case

Key Takeaway
A buyer's conditional promise to accept a defective item does not forfeit his right of return; the condition is void.

Dispute on Purpose of Riding After Defect Discovery
Ruling on disagreement whether riding was for return or personal use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for a dispute between buyer and seller regarding the purpose of using a defective item after discovery and apply it to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
The buyer's word is trusted when claiming the use was for return, not acceptance.

When a defective item is returned, the buyer's actions after discovering the defect can indicate acceptance or rejection. This lesson examines a specific dispute: the seller claims the buyer used the item (riding) for personal benefit, which would forfeit the right of return. The Hanafi school gives priority to the buyer's explanation, provided it is plausible, because the buyer is the one exercising the right of return and his intention is given weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
The seller claims the buyer rode the animal after discovering the defect for personal need. The buyer claims the riding was solely to return it to the seller. The legal question is: whose statement prevails?
The act of riding is ambiguous—it could indicate acceptance or preparation for return.
2
Apply the Hanafi principle
The principle 'al-qawl qawl al-mushtari' (the buyer's statement is given precedence) applies. The buyer is trusted because he is the one performing the return and his explanation is plausible (riding to return is common).
This is not an absolute rule; the buyer's claim must be reasonable under the circumstances.
3
Consider the practical consequence
If the buyer's word is accepted, he can return the item and is not deemed to have accepted it by riding. The seller cannot object based solely on the act of riding.
This protects the buyer's option (khiyar al-ayb) from being forfeited by ambiguous actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع قال البائع ركبتها بعد العثور على العيب في حاجتك وقال المشتري بل ركبتها لأردها عليك فالقول قول المشتري
The ninth: The seller said, 'You rode it after discovering the defect for your need,' and the buyer said, 'Rather I rode it to return it to you.' So the statement is the buyer's statement.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This case resolves a dispute between buyer and seller after a defect is discovered. The seller claims the buyer rode the animal for his own benefit (which would imply acceptance and forfeit the option). The buyer claims he rode it solely to return it to the seller. The Hanafi rule: the buyer's word is accepted, because the buyer is the one returning the item and his claim of intention is given priority, provided it is plausible. This is based on the principle that the person exercising a right (here the return) is trusted in his explanation.

Key Terms
القول قول المشتري (al-qawl qawl al-mushtari): The buyer's statement is given precedence in this dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school trusts the buyer's explanation for using a defective item after discovery, as long as the claim is plausible, preserving the right of return.

Defect Discovery, Judicial Ruling, and Custodial Death
Ruling on buyer informing judge of defect and animal dying in custody

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a buyer discovers a defect, obtains a judge's ruling, and the item dies in custody, distinguishing the effect of a prior judicial order for return.

Core Idea
Full price recovery hinges on a prior judicial order for return; without it, only defect compensation is due.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in sales (buyu) where a buyer discovers a defect after purchase and involves the judge. The key is whether the judge has already ruled for the return of the item. You will learn how the Hanafi principle of judicial enforceability (nafadh) determines the outcome, and how this applies even in cross-border contexts.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two scenarios
Identify whether the judge ruled for return (radd) before the animal died or not. This is the decisive factor.
The ruling is based on this distinction.
2
Apply the principle of nafadh
Understand that a judge's ruling is enforceable even on an absent party according to the Hanafi school.
This principle is the proof for the difference in outcome.
3
Extend to the siyar sub-case
Apply the same logic to riding the beast: without a ruling, riding implies acceptance; with a ruling, the judge's decision overrides.
The same principle of nafadh applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس اطلع على عيب فأعلم القاضي وبرهن على الشراء والعيب فوضعها القاضي عند عدل وماتت عنده ثم حضر البائع؛ إن كان لم يقض بالرد على الغائب لم يرجع عليه بالثمن، وإن كان قضى رجع لأن للقضاء نفاذاً في الأظهر عن أصحابنا. وفي السير : اشترى دابة في دار الإسلام وخرج عليها غازياً واطلع على عيب بغيبة البائع لا يركبها وإن في دار الحرب لأنه رضا وإن أمره الإمام لكن إذا قضى بأن الركوب ليس برضا نفذ وأمضاه القاضي الثاني.
The fifth: He discovered a defect, informed the judge, proved the purchase and defect, so the judge placed it with a trustworthy person, and it died in his custody. Then the seller came. If the judge had not ruled for return on the absent party, the buyer cannot claim the price back. If he had ruled, he can, because the ruling is enforceable (nafidh) according to the apparent view of our companions. And in al-Siyar: He bought a beast in Dar al-Islam and went out on it as a warrior and discovered a defect in the seller's absence; he should not ride it even in Dar al-Harb because that would be acceptance. Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This case deals with a buyer who discovers a defect, gets a judge's order, and the item dies in the custody of a trustee. The key distinction: if the judge had already ruled for the return (radd) while the seller was absent, the buyer recovers the full price. If no such ruling was made, the buyer only claims the defect compensation. The proof given: a judge's ruling is enforceable (nafidh) even on absent parties per the Hanafi school. The sub-case from al-Siyar adds that riding a defective beast after discovery is normally acceptance, but if a judge rules otherwise, that ruling must be followed.

Key Terms
نفاذ (nafadh): Enforceability of a judicial ruling even on an absent party.
ردّ (radd): Return of the defective item to the seller for a refund.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Buyer's Return of Defective Item: Before vs After Possession
Ruling on effect of buyer's statement of return before and after possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal effect of a buyer saying 'I return it' upon discovering a defect, distinguishing between before and after taking possession, and apply the ruling to cases of settlement and multiple defects.

Core Idea
Saying 'I return it' voids the sale automatically before possession, but requires the seller's acceptance after possession.

This lesson examines a crucial distinction in the law of sale regarding the buyer's right to return a defective item (khiyar al-ayb). The timing of the buyer's statement relative to taking physical possession (qabd) determines whether the return is automatic or requires the seller's consent. We will also explore a nuanced sub-case involving a settlement for one defect followed by discovery of another.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between a defect discovered before repossession (qabl al-qabd) and after repossession (ba'd al-qabd). Note that the buyer's statement 'I return it' has different effects in each scenario.
Possession (qabd) is the key dividing line.
2
Apply the rule for pre-possession defect
If the buyer discovers a defect before taking possession and says 'I have returned it to you,' the sale becomes void immediately, regardless of whether the seller accepts or not. This is because the buyer's right of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) is still in effect.
The sale is invalidated (batal) by the buyer's unilateral statement.
3
Apply the rule for post-possession defect
If the buyer discovers the defect after taking possession and says the same words, it is not considered a valid return until the seller expressly accepts by saying 'I accept' or 'I am satisfied.' The buyer's statement alone is insufficient.
After qabd, the return is a mutual rescission (faskh) that requires the seller's consent.
4
Analyze the effects of a valid return
When the return is validated (either by seller's consent or by judicial order), it is a rescission (faskh) between the buyer and seller, but in relation to third parties (e.g., creditors) it is treated as a new sale (bay') from the seller to the buyer. If a judge orders the return, it is a general rescission binding on all.
This distinction protects third-party rights.
5
Examine the sub-case from al-Ghunya
If a buyer discovers one old defect, settles with the seller for compensation (a dinar), takes the item, then discovers another old defect, he may still return the item along with the compensation. There is a difference of opinion: some say he only claims compensation for the second defect, others say he can return the item.
The acceptance of compensation does not waive the right to return for a different defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر اطلع على عيب قبل القبض فقال المشتري للبائع رددته عليك بطل البيع قبل البائع أولاً، والكل من البزازية. وفي السراج الوهاج وإن قال ذلك بعد القبض لم يكن ذلك رداً ما لم يقل البائع قبلت أو رضيت ثم إذا رده برضا البائع كان فسخاً في حقهما بيعاً في حق غيرهما اهـ. وإن رده بحكم فهو فسخ عام. وكذا كل عقد ينفسخ، بالرد ويكون المردود مضموناً بما يقابله ؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين. وفي القنية : اشترى حماراً ووجد به عيباً قديماً فأراد الرد فصولح بينهما بدينار وأخذه ثم وجد به عيباً قديماً آخر فله رده مع الدينار. ثم رقم لآخر أنه يرجع بنقصان العيب وعنه أنه يرده اهـ.
The tenth: He discovered a defect before repossession and said to the seller, 'I have returned it to you.' The sale is void, whether the seller accepts first or not. All from al-Bazzaziyyah. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If he said that after repossession, it is not a return unless the seller says 'I accept' or 'I am satisfied.' Then if he returns it with the seller's consent, it is a rescission (faskh) between them, but a sale (bay') in the right of third parties. And if he returns it by judicial ruling, it is a general rescission. Similarly, every contract is rescinded by return, and the returned item is guaranteed by the equivalent. So in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And in al-Ghunya: He bought a donkey and found an old defect, wanted to return, and they settled for a dinar and he took it; then he found another old defect; he may return it along with the dinar. Then he numbered for another that he claims compensation for the defect, but it is said he returns it.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This lengthy case differentiates between return before and after physical possession (qabd). If the buyer discovers a defect and says 'I return it' before taking possession, the sale is automatically void, regardless of the seller's response. After possession, however, the same statement is not a valid return without the seller's express acceptance. If the seller accepts, it is a rescission (faskh) between the parties, but for third parties, it is considered a new sale. If a judge orders the return, it is a general rescission. The sub-case from al-Ghunya: a buyer who settled for compensation (a dinar) for one old defect may still return the item if he discovers another old defect, and the compensation is also returned. There is a difference of opinion on whether he claims defect compensation or returns the item.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of the contract; annulment by mutual consent or judicial ruling.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession or control of the item bought.
رد (radd): Return of the defective item by the buyer to the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer must physically return the item first.
B) The seller must say 'I accept' or 'I am satisfied.'
C) A judge must issue an order.
D) The buyer must also cancel the contract in writing.

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of a buyer's statement 'I return it' for a defect depends crucially on whether possession has occurred: before possession it voids the sale automatically; after possession it requires the seller's acceptance for a mutual rescission, which is treated as a sale vis-à-vis third parties.

Seller's Conditional Instruction Does Not Constitute a Valid Return
Ruling on seller's instruction to buyer to care for camel for ten days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a seller's conditional instruction to care for a defective item does not count as a valid legal return (radd) and identify the conditions required for a valid return.

Core Idea
A return requires explicit rescission; a care order does not undo the sale.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from the Hanafi fiqh of sales (buyu'): a buyer attempts to return a defective camel, but the seller tells the buyer to care for it for ten days. Does this arrangement constitute a valid return? The text provides a clear ruling: no. Understanding this helps clarify the strict requirements for a valid rescission of a sale contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key legal act
Determine whether the seller's instruction 'go and take care of it for ten days' is being considered as a valid return (radd) or something else. The text states it is not a return.
2
Understand the implication for liability
If the camel perishes during the ten days, the seller said 'it is from my wealth', meaning the seller bears the loss. However, the text rules that this does not constitute a return; thus the buyer is not relieved of liability. Clarify that the seller's offer does not change the legal status.
The buyer remains responsible unless a valid rescission occurs.
3
Connect to conditions of valid return
Identify that a valid return requires mutual consent or judicial rescission, not a conditional care arrangement. The condition (if it recovers, the camel is yours) shows the seller intends to keep the sale pending, not cancel it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادي عشر باع بعيراً فوجده المشتري معيباً فرده فقال له البائع اذهب فتعهده إلى عشرة أيام فإن برئ فلك البعير، وإن هلك فمن مالي لا يكون رداً؛ كذا في القنية
The eleventh: He sold a camel, and the buyer found it defective and returned it. The seller said to him: 'Go and take care of it for ten days; if it recovers, the camel is yours; if it perishes, it is from my wealth.' This is not a valid return. So in al-Ghunya.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This case shows that a seller's conditional suggestion to care for the item does not constitute a return. The buyer had attempted to return the defective camel, but the seller instead proposed a trial period. The Hanafi position: such an arrangement does not constitute a rescission of the contract. The buyer is not relieved of liability; if the camel dies during this period, it is considered under the buyer's responsibility. The ruling emphasizes that a valid return requires clear and explicit rescission, either by mutual consent or judicial decision.

Key Terms
لا يكون رداً (la yakunu raddan): The action is not considered a valid legal return (radd).

Exception: Buyer Returning Defective Item When Agent Acknowledged Receipt
Ruling on buyer returning defective item when seller's agent acknowledged receipt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exceptional case where a buyer who returns a defective item cannot recover the price from either the seller or the agent, and identify the conditions that trigger this exception.

Core Idea
If the seller's agent acknowledges receipt of the price but the seller denies, the buyer cannot recover the price upon return of a defective item.

Generally, a buyer who returns a defective item recovers the full price from the seller. However, there is an exception when a seller's agent appointed to collect the price acknowledges receipt (even falsely) and the seller denies receipt. In this case, the buyer is absolved from paying again but later cannot recover the price from either party. This lesson explores the conditions and reasoning behind this exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State the general Hanafi rule that a buyer returning a defective item recovers the price from the seller.
This is the default position.
2
Identify the exceptional case
In the exceptional case, the seller appointed an agent; the agent acknowledged receipt of the price but the seller denied receiving it.
The agent's acknowledgment is central.
3
Determine the legal consequences
The buyer is absolved of paying again, but if a defect is found later, he cannot recover the price from the seller (due to agent's admission) nor from the agent (who is an amin, a trustee).
The buyer is protected from double payment but loses the right to recovery.
4
Understand the rationale
The agent is an amin (trustee) and not a contracting party; the seller's denial coupled with the agent's acknowledgment creates a conflict that bars the buyer's claim against both.
The ruling preserves the seller's denial and the agent's trust status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني عشر المشتري إذا رد المبيع بالعيب فإنه يرجع بالثمن على بائعه إلا في مسألة في القنية : باع عبداً وسلمه ثم وكل وكيلاً بقبض الثمن فأقر الوكيل بقبضه وهلاكه وجحد البائع الموكل برئ المشتري ولا ضمان على الوكيل، فإن وجد المشتري به عيباً رده ولا يرجع بالثمن على البائع لإقرار الوكيل ولا على الوكيل لكونه أميناً وليس بعاقد والثانية في الفوائد
The twelfth: When the buyer returns the sold item due to defect, he recovers the price from his seller, except in a case in al-Ghunya: He sold a slave and handed him over, then appointed an agent to collect the price. The agent acknowledged receipt and destruction (of the price), but the seller principal denied. The buyer is absolved, and there is no liability on the agent. Then if the buyer finds a defect in the slave and returns him, he does not recover the price from the seller due to the agent's acknowledgment, nor from the agent because he is an amin (trustee) and not a party to the contract. The second (case) is in al-Fawa'id.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This case outlines an exception to the general rule that a buyer who returns a defective item recovers the full price from the seller. The exception: if the seller appointed an agent to collect the price, and the agent (falsely) admitted to receiving and losing the price (while the seller denies), then the buyer is absolved from paying again, but if he later discovers a defect and returns the slave, he cannot recover the price from the seller (because the seller claims he never got the price) nor from the agent (who is merely a trustee, not a contracting party). The ruling protects both the buyer (who is not required to pay twice) and the seller (who did not actually receive payment). The reference to al-Fawa'id indicates a second similar case.

Key Terms
أمين (amin): Trustee; the agent is not liable for the price unless he breached trust.
وكيل (wakil): Agent; a person appointed to act on behalf of another, specifically here to collect the price.
عيب (‘ayb): Defect; a flaw in the sold item that gives the buyer the right to return it (khiyar al-‘ayb).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the exceptional case in case 12, why can't the buyer recover the price from the seller after returning a defective slave?
A) Because the slave is not actually defective.
B) Because the seller's agent acknowledged receipt, so the seller claims he never got the price.
C) Because the buyer waited too long.
D) Because the slave was returned by judicial order.

Key Takeaway
The agent's pending acknowledgment can block the buyer's right to recover the price from the seller upon return of a defective item, even if the acknowledgment is false.

Legatee's Right to Return a Defective Bequest
Ruling on khiyar al-'ayb in bequest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a legatee's right to return a defective bequeathed item and identify the condition that determines this right.

Core Idea
A legatee's right to return a defective bequest depends on whether he is an heir.

In this brief ruling from a classical Hanafi text, we encounter an important exception in the law of bequest. While a buyer normally has the right to return a defective purchased item, the legatee (musa lahu) does not automatically have this right. The key factor is the legatee's status as an heir or non-heir. Let us examine the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and legal capacity
The ruling concerns the legatee's right to return a defective item (khiyar al-'ayb) after taking possession. Note that the legatee is a recipient of a bequest, not a buyer.
Sale contracts are governed by khiyar al-'ayb; bequest is a gratuitous transfer.
2
State the default rule
The text states: 'The legatee does not have the right to return for defect.' Understand that this is the default position.
Contrast with a buyer's right.
3
Identify the exception
The exception is: 'unless he is not an heir.' If the legatee is not an heir, he does have the right to return.
This exception is based on the principle that a bequest to an heir is invalid without the consent of other heirs, so the heir's acceptance implies acceptance of defects.
4
Apply to scenarios
Practice applying the rule: For an heir legatee, no khiyar; for a non-heir legatee, khiyar applies like a sale.
Consider also the effect of the testator's consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس عشر الموصى له لا يملك الرد بالعيب إلا إذا لم يكن وراث؛ كذا في الصغرى
The fifteenth: The legatee (musa lahu) does not have the right to return for defect unless he is not an heir; so in al-Sughra.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This case addresses a bequest (wasiyyah). The recipient of a bequeathed item (musa lahu) does not have the option of return for defect unless that person is not an heir. If the legatee is also an heir, accepting the bequest implies acceptance of any defects. If he is not an heir, he has the same rights as a buyer, including khiyar al-'ayb. The reference 'al-Sughra' likely refers to a work on fiqh.

Key Terms
الموصى له (al-musa lahu): The person to whom a bequest is made (legatee).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to case 15, when can a legatee return a defective bequeathed item?
A) Always, just like a buyer.
B) Never, because it is a gift.
C) Only if he is not an heir.
D) Only if the defect was hidden.

Key Takeaway
A legatee's right to return a defective bequest hinges on whether he is an heir; if an heir, acceptance is final; if not, he retains the option.

What Constitutes a Legal Defect ('Ayb) in Sales
Definition of legal defect ('ayb) in Hanafi commercial law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'ayb and apply it to concrete examples by referencing trade custom.

Core Idea
A defect is whatever reduces market value according to merchants, even if purely cosmetic.

In Hanafi sales law, the concept of a defect ('ayb) is crucial for buyer protections like the right of return (khiyar al-'ayb). But what exactly counts as a defect? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq establishes the core criterion: any attribute that decreases the price according to custom. This definition is broader than it first seems, and includes subtle intra-madhhab discussions on specific cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Criterion
The passage states clearly: 'Whatever causes a decrease in price according to merchants is a defect.' Note that the measure is the custom of merchants (or craftsmen for manufactured goods), not personal opinion.
2
Understand the Rationale
The reason given is that the aim of the defect rule is 'decrease of monetary value.' Thus, any attribute that reduces the item's value in the market qualifies, even if it does not harm the item's substance or utility.
3
Recognize Generality: Cosmetic Flaws Count
The passage explicitly expands the definition to include attributes that 'do not decrease the substance nor its benefits, but merely the appearance.' Example: a black toenail on a healthy slave can be a defect if it reduces price.
4
Note Intra-Madhhab Nuances
The text records disagreements among Hanafi scholars: e.g., black nail considered a defect only among Turks (al-Mi'raj), and lack of language skill is a defect only if experts deem it so (al-Bazzaziyyah). Al-Qadi ruled a home-born slave (muwallad) is not defective for lacking language.
5
Apply to a New Scenario
Now test your understanding: a used car with a small dent that does not affect driving but reduces its resale value by 10% among car dealers — is it a defect? Yes, according to the Hanafi definition, because it decreases price by custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أوجب نقصان الثمن عند التجار فهو عيب لأن المقصود نقصان المالية وذلك بانتقاص القيمة والمرجع في معرفته عرف أهله وهم التجار أو أرباب الصنائع إن كان المبيع من المصنوعات ؛ كذا في فتح القدير فلا يقتصر الحكم على التجار. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان ينقص العين أو لا ينقصها ولا ينقص منافعها بل مجرد النظر إليها كالظفر الأسود الصحيح القوي على العمل، وكما في جارية تركية لا تعرف لسان الترك كما في فتح القدير . وقيد في المعراج الظفر الأسود لكونه عيباً بالأتراك أما في الحبش فلا. وقيد في البزازية عدم معرفة اللسان بأن يعده أهل الخبرة عيباً وقال القاضي في المولد لا يكون عيباً. والتجار بضم تاجر وبكسرها مع التخفيف ولا يكاد يوجد تاء بعدها جيم؛
And whatever causes a decrease in price according to merchants is a defect, because the aim is decrease of monetary value, and that is by decrease of value. The reference in knowing it is the custom of its people, who are the merchants or experts in crafts if the sold item is manufactured. So in Fath al-Qadir. So the ruling is not restricted to merchants. He made it general, so it includes whether it decreases the substance or does not decrease it nor decrease its benefits, but merely the appearance, like the black nail that is healthy and strong for work, and like a Turkish slave girl who does not know the Turkish language, as in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Mi'raj, the black nail was restricted as being a defect among Turks, but not among Abyssinians. And in al-Bazzaziyyah, the lack of language knowledge is restricted to when experts consider it a defect. And al-Qadi said regarding a slave born in the master's house (muwallad) that it is not a defect. And al-tujjar (merchants): with damm of ta' (tujjār) and with kasr and lightening, and a ta' followed by jim is hardly found.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This is a critical definitional passage. It establishes the Hanafi criterion for what constitutes a legal defect ('ayb) in sales: any characteristic that customarily reduces the market price. The reference is trade custom (urf ahl al-suq). The text from Fath al-Qadir broadens this to include defects that are purely cosmetic (like a black nail) or lack of a skill (like a Turkish slave not knowing Turkish) if it affects value. However, restrictions are mentioned: black nail is only a defect among Turks, not among Abyssinians where it may be normal. Similarly, ignorance of language is only a defect if experts deem it so. A muwallad (home-born slave) is not considered defective for lacking language skills. The text also corrects the pronunciation of 'tujjar'.

Key Terms
عيب (ayb): Any attribute that decreases the price of an item according to the custom of merchants.
مولّد (muwallad): A slave born in the master's house (home-born).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, which of the following is a defect?
A) A healthy slave with a minor scar that does not affect work but reduces price by custom.
B) A slave who is missing a hand but works just as well as before.
C) A slave whose hair color is different from the buyer's preference but does not affect price.
D) A slave who is slightly taller than average.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'ayb is entirely custom-based: any attribute that diminishes market value according to merchants is a defect, regardless of its effect on functionality.

Exception: Returning Defective Goods Purchased from a Judge or His Agent
Ruling on buyer's right to return defective item purchased from a judge or his agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exception to the right of return for defect (khiyar al-'ayb) when the seller is a judge or his agent acting for an orphan, and explain the rationale based on guardianship.

Core Idea
The buyer cannot return a defective item to a judge who sold an orphan's property; the remedy lies against the estate, not the judge.

The general principle of khiyar al-'ayb gives a buyer the right to return a defective purchase to the seller. However, this right is not absolute. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a specific exception: when the seller is a judge (or his trustee) selling the property of a minor orphan, the buyer loses the right to litigate for return against the judge. The reason lies in the judge's role as a guardian; his actions are subject to oversight, and the buyer's claim should be directed at the orphan's estate rather than the judge personally. This illustrates how the status of the seller can modify contractual remedies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recall that in Hanafi fiqh, khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) allows a buyer to return a defective item to the seller, provided the defect existed at the time of sale and the buyer was unaware.
2
State the exception
From the passage: if a judge sells the property of a minor (orphan) to a man, and after delivery the buyer discovers a defect, he has no right to litigate against the judge for return. The same applies if the judge's agent (amin) made the sale.
This exception is additional to those previously listed in the commentary.
3
Understand the rationale
The judge acts as a guardian (wali) for the orphan. His sale is a fiduciary act. The buyer's remedy for defect is against the orphan's estate, not the judge personally. The judge is immune from direct lawsuit because he is not the beneficial owner.
The principal (al-manuub) is the judge, and the agent's ruling follows the principal's.
4
Trace the source and commentary
The ruling is from al-Tatarkhaniyya, quoted by al-Ramli. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq adds that this exception was omitted by an earlier commentator, so the student should take note.
This is an expansion (ziyada) to the list of exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الرملي : وقد نقل بعضهم عن التتارخانية أن القاضي لو باع مال الصغير من رجل وسلمه إلى المشتري ثم وجد المشتري عيباً فليس له أن يخاصم القاضي في الرد بالعيب، وكذلك إذا باع بعض أمناء القاضي مال الصغير لا سبيل للمشتري في الخصومة في الرد على البائع فإنه نائب عن القاضي وحكمه حكم المنوب .اهـ. فهذا مما استثني أيضاً ولم يذكره هذا الشارح فتأمله .اهـ. وهذه المسألة التي
Al-Ramli said: And some have transmitted from al-Tatarkhaniyya that if the judge sells the property of a minor to a man and delivers it to the buyer, then the buyer finds a defect, he cannot litigate against the judge for return due to defect. Similarly, if one of the judge's trustees sells the property of a minor, the buyer has no way to litigate for return against the seller, because he is the deputy of the judge, and his ruling is the ruling of the principal. So this is also among the exceptions, and this commentator did not mention it, so ponder. And this case which...
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This is an additional exception to the general right of return for defect, quoted by al-Ramli from al-Tatarkhaniyya. If a judge (or his agent) sells property of an orphan (minor) and the buyer later discovers a defect, the buyer cannot sue the judge or the agent for return. The reason: the judge acts as a guardian and his sale is subject to supervision; the remedy lies elsewhere (perhaps against the estate). The commentator of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq notes that this exception was not included in the earlier list of cases, so the student should note it.

Key Terms
المنوب (al-manuub): The person for whom one acts as deputy (the judge).
الأمين (al-amin): Trustee, agent of the judge entrusted with selling the orphan's property.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to return a defective item due to a hidden defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason the buyer cannot sue the judge for return of a defective item?
A) The judge is immune from all lawsuits.
B) The buyer assumed the risk by purchasing from a judge.
C) The judge's sale is final and cannot be challenged.
D) The judge is a guardian and the buyer's remedy is against the orphan's estate.

Key Takeaway
The right of return for defect does not apply against a judge or his agent when they sell an orphan's property; the buyer must claim against the estate.

Agent's Acknowledgment and Buyer's Recourse for Defect
Ruling on buyer's recourse when agent acknowledges receipt and seller denies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal consequences when an agent acknowledges receipt and destruction of goods while the seller denies, and explain why the buyer cannot recover the price from either the seller or the agent.

Core Idea
An agent's acknowledgment binds the buyer but not the seller; the buyer's recourse is limited due to the agent's trustee status.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a scenario in sales where an agent acts on behalf of a seller. When the agent admits receiving and losing the item, but the seller denies this, the buyer is free from liability. However, if the buyer later discovers a defect, he cannot reclaim the price from the seller because of the agent's conflicting statement, nor from the agent because the agent is a trustee, not a party to the contract. This lesson explores the legal reasoning behind these outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the agent's acknowledgment
The agent (wakil) admits receipt and destruction of the item; the seller (principal) denies. The buyer is then released from any obligation.
2
Determine the liability of the agent
The agent is an amin (trustee) and bears no liability for the loss unless negligence is proven. The text explicitly says 'لا ضمان على الوكيل'.
3
Analyze the defect return scenario
If the buyer later finds a defect, he may return the item. But the text says he cannot claim the price from the seller because the agent's acknowledgment contradicts the seller's denial.
4
Understand why the buyer cannot claim from the agent
The agent is not the contracting party; the contract is between buyer and seller. The agent's role is strictly representational, and as an amin, he is not liable for the price.
5
Formulate the practical consequence
The buyer bears the loss, despite the defect, due to the procedural impasse created by the contradictory statements of agent and seller.
This ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle that an agent does not guarantee the transaction outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأقر الوكيل بقبضه وهلاكه وجحد البائع الموكل برئ المشتري ولا ضمان على الوكيل، فإن وجد المشتري به عيباً رده ولا يرجع بالثمن على البائع لإقرار الوكيل ولا على الوكيل لكونه أميناً وليس بعاقد
If the agent acknowledges its receipt and destruction, and the seller denies, the buyer is acquitted and there is no liability upon the agent. If the buyer finds a defect in it, he returns it, and he does not claim the price from the seller due to the agent's acknowledgment, nor from the agent because he is a trustee and is not the contracting party.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling in the chapter of option of defect. The scenario involves an agent who acknowledged receiving the item and its destruction, while the seller (principal) denies the agent's authority or the receipt. The buyer is freed from any obligation, and the agent bears no liability. However, if the buyer later discovers a defect and wishes to return the item, he cannot reclaim the price from the seller (due to the agent's conflicting acknowledgment) nor from the agent (since the agent is an amin, not a party to the sale). The ruling restricts the buyer's recourse, emphasizing the agent's role as a trustee.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakīl): A person authorized by another to act on his behalf in a transaction.
أمين (amīn): A trustee who is not liable for loss of the item in his custody without negligence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the given ruling, why can the buyer not recover the price from the agent after returning a defective item?
A) Because the agent is the actual seller
B) Because the agent is a trustee (amin) and not the contracting party
C) Because the buyer waived his right
D) Because the seller admitted liability

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an agent's acknowledgment does not bind the seller, and the agent's trustee status protects him from liability for the price, leaving the buyer without recourse against either party in this scenario.
