The Defect Dispute: Buyer’s Word vs. Seller’s Word
Ruling on whose word is taken when seller claims sold with defect and buyer claims soundness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default rule in a defect dispute and identify the exception based on the significance of the price.

Core Idea
Buyer’s word prevails unless the price is trivial.

When a buyer claims an item was bought sound and the seller insists it was sold with a disclosed defect, whose statement carries legal weight? The Hanafi school generally favors the buyer, but with a nuanced exception: if the price is so low that it suggests the buyer must have known, the seller’s word is accepted. This lesson unpacks the rule and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize the conflicting claims: seller asserts he disclosed the defect, buyer denies knowledge. Both agree on the presence of the defect but disagree on whether it was disclosed at sale.
This is a dispute over a past event – no witnesses.
2
State the general rule
The default is that the buyer’s word is taken. The buyer denies liability (the defect was hidden), so the burden of proof is on the seller, but the buyer’s oath is credited.
This aligns with the principle that the one who denies (the buyer) is generally believed.
3
Identify the exception
If the price is 'insignificant' (yasir), the seller’s word is taken instead. A low price indicates the buyer likely knew of the defect and accepted it.
The text says: 'if the price is insignificant, the word is given to the seller; otherwise, to the buyer.'
4
Apply to examples
Consider: an item worth 100 dirhams sold for 10 dirhams – the price is insignificant, so seller’s word. If sold for 90 dirhams – price is significant, buyer’s word.
What constitutes 'insignificant' is determined by custom (urf) and the specific market.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث عشر قال البائع بعته لك معيباً بهذا العيب وقال المشتري اشتريته سليماً فالقول للمشتري ثم رقم أنه ينبغي أن يحكم الثمن يعني إن كان الثمن يسيراً فالقول للبائع وإلا فللمشتري .اهـ.
The thirteenth: The seller says, 'I sold it to you defective with this defect,' and the buyer says, 'I bought it sound.' The word is given to the buyer. Then he numbered that the price should be considered, meaning if the price is insignificant, the word is given to the seller; otherwise, to the buyer.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute about the condition at the time of sale. When the seller claims to have disclosed a defect and the buyer denies knowledge, the general rule is that the buyer's statement is credited. However, a refinement is introduced: if the price is very low (implying the buyer likely knew), the seller's word is taken. This nuance prevents exploitation of trivial prices.

Key Terms
القول (al-qawl): The legal weight given to a party's statement in a dispute.
الثمن اليسير (al-thaman al-yasir): An insignificant or trivial price that indicates the buyer likely knew of the defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the seller's word prevail in a dispute over defect disclosure?
A) When the buyer has no witnesses
B) When the price is insignificant
C) When the defect is obvious
D) When the seller is a merchant

Key Takeaway
The buyer’s word is default in a defect dispute, but if the price is so low that it reveals the buyer’s knowledge, the seller’s word is accepted.

Return After Currency Fluctuation: Buyer's Right to Original Gold
Ruling on buyer's right to demand original gold when returning defective item after currency devaluation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a buyer's right to demand the original currency when returning a defective item after the substitute currency has depreciated.

Core Idea
When returning a defective purchase, the buyer may demand the original gold, not the depreciated substitute.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq illustrates a scenario where financial loss due to currency depreciation is borne by the seller when a sale is rescinded. It highlights the Hanafi principle of giving back the specific item paid, protecting the buyer from losses caused by the seller's faulty goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction sequence
The buyer purchases a donkey for three gold dinars, then later gives dirhams as a substitute for the gold dinars. This substitution is like an advance or exchange of currencies.
The original obligation was gold; the dirhams are a substitute.
2
Identify the return event
The buyer returns the donkey after a month due to a defect. This is a valid return (radd bi al-ayb) in a sale.
The return is because of a defect, not a general rescission.
3
Note the currency change
By the time of return, the dirhams have lost purchasing power (sunk in price). The dirhams are now worth less than when given.
Currency depreciation affects the value of the substitute.
4
State the ruling
The buyer may demand the original gold dinars (ayn al-dhahab) from the seller, not the depreciated dirhams.
The seller bears the loss of depreciation.
5
Note the extension and exception
The same rule applies in iqalah (mutual rescission of a sale). However, if the buyer had paid wheat instead of gold, the rule changes (the buyer cannot demand wheat back; the iqalah proceeds differently).
Al-Qunyah is the source reference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع عشر اشترى حماراً بثلاثة دنانير ذهب ثم أعطاه عوضها دراهم ثم رده بعد شهر بعيب وقد انتقص سعر الدراهم فله أن يطلب من البائع عين الذهب، وبمثله أجاب في الإقالة إلا إذا دفع مكان الذهب حنطة، وهي وما قبلها في القنية.
The fourteenth: He bought a donkey for three gold dinars, then gave him dirhams in exchange for them, then returned it after a month due to a defect, and the price of dirhams had decreased. So he may demand the gold itself from the seller. The like response was given in revocation (iqalah), unless he paid wheat instead of gold. These and preceding are in al-Qunyah.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage describes a case where a buyer paid for a donkey in gold dinars but later substituted dirhams (perhaps as an advance). When the item is returned due to a defect, the dirhams have lost value. The buyer can demand the original gold dinars from the seller, not just the depreciated dirhams. This rule also applies to rescission (iqalah), except if the payment was in kind (wheat). The reference is to the work al-Qunyah.

Key Terms
عين الذهب (ʿayn al-dhahab): The specific gold coins (not their value or substitute).
إقالة (iqālah): Mutual rescission of a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the buyer have the right to demand in the case of returning a donkey after paying in dirhams that have lost value?
A) The current market value in dirhams
B) The original gold dinars
C) A refund of the price paid
D) Nothing, as the defect was not the seller's fault

Key Takeaway
In a return due to defect, the buyer may claim the original payment item, not its depreciated substitute.

6 min
Legatee's Right to Return a Defective Bequest
Ruling on legatee's right to return defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a legatee can return a defective bequest and apply the exception concerning a qadi's sale of a minor's property.

Core Idea
A legatee cannot return a defective bequest unless he is not an heir.

This lesson addresses a specific limitation on a legatee's right to return a defective item received through a bequest. The Hanafi position restricts this right—it depends on whether the legatee is also an heir. The passage also extends the logic to a qadi's sale of a minor's property, where the buyer similarly cannot return the item for defect. Understanding these exceptions ensures proper application in inheritance and guardianship transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling
The passage states that a legatee (al-mūṣā lahu) does not have the right to return a defective bequest. This is the default rule.
Remember that the right of return (khiyar al-'ayb) is typically available to buyers, but legatees have a different status.
2
Note the Condition for Exception
The exception is: the legatee may return the defective item only if he is not an heir. If he is an heir, he loses this right.
Why? Possibly because the bequest is merged with inheritance rights, making return impractical.
3
Recognize the Extended Exception from Tatar Khaniyyah
The passage adds that if a qadi sells the property of a minor, and the buyer later finds a defect, the buyer cannot sue the qadi for return. The same applies if a trustee sells. This is because the qadi acts as a deputy, immune from such litigation.
This is an exception to the usual rule that a seller can be sued for defect.
4
Reflect on the Commentator's Omission
The author notes that the commentator (the sharh) did not mention this exception, so the reader should ponder why and how it fits into the broader framework.
This highlights that not all exceptions are always enumerated in a single commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس عشر الموصى له لا يملك الرد بالعيب إلا إذا لم يكن وراث؛ كذا في الصغرى قوله : (الخامس عشر الموصى له لا يملك الرد بالعيب إلا إذا لم يكن (وارث في بعض النسخ وارثاً بالنصب تأمل. قال الرملي : وقد نقل بعضهم عن التتارخانية أن القاضي لو باع مال الصغير من رجل وسلمه إلى المشتري ثم وجد المشتري عيباً فليس له أن يخاصم القاضي في الرد بالعيب، وكذلك إذا باع بعض أمناء القاضي مال الصغير لا سبيل للمشتري في الخصومة في الرد على البائع فإنه نائب عن القاضي وحكمه حكم المنوب .اهـ. فهذا مما استثني أيضاً ولم يذكره هذا الشارح فتأمله .اهـ.
The fifteenth: The legatee does not have the right to return due to a defect unless he is not an heir; thus in al-Sughra. He said: (The fifteenth: The legatee does not have the right to return due to a defect unless he is not an heir—in some copies 'heir' in the accusative, ponder. Al-Ramli said: Some have transmitted from al-Tatar Khaniyyah that if the qadi sold the property of a minor to a man and handed it over to the buyer, then the buyer found a defect, he may not litigate against the qadi for return due to the defect. Similarly, if some trustee of the qadi sold the property of a minor, the buyer has no way to litigate against the seller for return, for he is a deputy of the qadi and his ruling is like the ruling of the principal. Thus this is also excepted, and this commentator did not mention it, so ponder.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage discusses the right of a legatee (one who receives a bequest) to return a defective item. The ruling states that a legatee cannot return the item unless he is not an heir. The commentator adds that when a qadi sells the property of a minor, the buyer cannot sue the qadi for defect, even though normally a seller can be sued. This is an exception to the general rule, and the author notes that the commentator (the sharh) did not mention it, hence the reader should reflect.

Key Terms
الموصى له (al-mūṣā lahu): The person to whom a bequest is made; a legatee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can a legatee return a defective item?
A) Always
B) Only if he is also an heir
C) Only if he is not an heir
D) Never

Key Takeaway
A legatee's right to return a defective bequest is conditional on not being an heir, and a buyer cannot sue a qadi for defect in a sale of a minor's property.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

The Hanafi Definition of Defect: Custom as the Reference
Definition of defect with custom-based criterion and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a defect (al-ʿayb) in sales and apply it to examples, distinguishing it from the Shafiʿi purpose-based criterion.

Core Idea
A defect is not about utility but about market value: custom decides.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raʾiq anchors the definition of a defect (al-ʿayb) to the custom of merchants and craftsmen. The Hanafi school emphasizes that any decrease in price according to trade custom qualifies as a defect, even if the item's function is intact. The commentary expands with examples like a black nail or a slave girl's language deficiency, and contrasts with the Shafiʿi condition of frustration of a valid purpose. The discussion of ibāq (running away) illustrates how custom determines whether a condition is a defect.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core definition
Identify the statement from Kanz al-Daqāʾiq: 'مَا أَوْجَبَ نُقْصَانَ الثَّمَنِ عِنْدَ التُّجَّارِ فَهُوَ عَيْبٌ'. Understand that the criterion is decrease in price according to merchants.
2
Identify the reference for knowing a defect
The text says 'وَالْمَرْجِعُ فِي مَعْرِفَتِهِ عُرْفُ أَهْلِهِ وَهُمُ التُّجَّارُ أَوْ أَرْبَابُ الصَّنَائِعِ'. So custom (ʿurf) of merchants or craftsmen is the evidential source.
3
Analyze the expansion examples
Look at the examples: black nail (visual defect) and Turkish slave girl lacking language. Note that these do not affect function but reduce market value. Note the regional differences (Turks vs. Ethiopians) and expert opinion condition.
4
Compare with Shafiʿi rule
The Shafiʿi definition: return for any deficiency frustrating a valid purpose and generally absent. Identify the thamarah al-khilāf: e.g., black nail is a defect in Hanafi if it reduces price, but not in Shafiʿi if it does not frustrate a valid purpose.
5
Synthesize the muftā bihī definition
From Khizānat al-Fiqh: defect = decrease in object or usufruct, or what merchants consider a defect. The commentator prefers this over the author's wording.

٦٤ كتاب البيع باب خيار عيب كالإباق والبول في الفراش من العيوب والسرقة من العيوب في العبد والجارية والجنون أذن الشاة ما مع المصباح. والضابط عند الشافعية أنه يرد بكل ما في المعقود عليه من منقص القيمة أو نقصان يفوت به غرض صحيح بشرط أن يكون الغالب في أمثال المبيع عدمه. قالوا: وإنما شرطنا فوات غرض صحيح لأنه لو بان فوات تقطعة. يسيرة من فخذه أو ساقه لا رد، ولو قطع من يمنع التضحية ردها وإلا فلا. وشرطنا الغالب لأنه لا ترد الأمة إذا كانت ثيباً أن الثيابة معنى ينقص القيمة لكن ليس الغالب عدم الثيابة؛ كذا كما في شرح وجيزهم في المعراج وقواعدنا لا تأباه للمتأمل. وفي خزانة الفقه : العيب ما نقص العين أو المنفعة وإلا فإن أعده التجار عيباً كان عيباً وإلا فلا وهو أحسن مما في الكتاب، وذكرها في التلخيص من باب الإقرار بالعيب من البيوع وحاصلها أنه أربع لا يرده في مسألتين وتمامه في شرحه للفارسي قوله : (كالإباق) من أبق العبد أبقاً من باب تعب وقتل في لغة، والأكثر من باب ضرب إذا هرب من سيده من غير خوف ولا كد. والإباق ـ بالكسر - He said: (And what necessitates a decrease in price according to merchants is a defect, because the objective is decrease in monetary value, and that is by reduction of value. The reference in knowing it is the custom of its people, and they are the merchants or craftsmen if the sold item is manufactured; thus in Fath al-Qadir, so the ruling is not restricted to merchants. He applied it generally so it includes what decreases the object or does not decrease it nor decrease its usufruct, but merely looking at it, like a black nail that is sound and strong for work, and like a Turkish slave girl who does not know the Turkish language, as in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Mi'raj it was restricted that the black nail is a defect among Turks, but among Ethiopians it is not. And in al-Bazzaziyyah it was restricted that lack of knowledge of language is a defect if experts consider it so, and al-Qadi said in al-Mawlid it is not a defect. 'Merchants' with damm on the ta of tajir and with kasr [of the jim] with tashdid, and there is hardly a ta after jim; the ta with tashdid is plural... He said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: And if he returns it after taking possession, the contract is not rescinded except with the seller's consent or by court order. So his statement 'except with the seller's consent' indicates that if consent is found through action, like the seller taking it from the buyer when he demands return, the sale is rescinded. And it was presented in bay' al-ta'ati that if he returns it with option of defect and the seller is certain it is not his, but he takes it and is pleased, then it is a sale by mutual consent (bay' al-ta'ati) as in Fath al-Qadir. And it also states that meaning stands in place of speech in sales and the like. And it is established among them that consent is sometimes proven by speech and sometimes by other than speech. He said: (He sold) a camel (etc.) Al-Ramli said: It is common in our country that when a buyer discovers a defect or appears to be overcharged in an animal, he brings the sold item to the seller and enters it into his house and says 'Here is your animal, I do not want it,' and returns, and it perishes. There is no doubt that it perishes on the buyer because this is not a return, even if the seller undertakes it, as long as no rescission of the sale has occurred between them explicitly through speech or action or indication.

Page 64: The book of sale, chapter of option of defect: Like running away (iba), and bedwetting are defects, and theft is a defect in a slave or slave girl, and insanity, the sheep's ear... with al-Misbah. And the rule according to the Shafi'is is that one returns due to everything in the contracted item that reduces the value or a deficiency that frustrates a valid purpose, provided that such thing is generally absent in similar items. They said: We conditioned the frustration of a valid purpose because if it appears that the loss is of a small piece of his thigh or leg, no return; and if something is cut that prevents sacrifice, he returns, otherwise not. And we conditioned 'generally absent' because a female slave who is a thayyib (non-virgin) is not returned, for thayyibah is a meaning that reduces value, but non-thayyibah is not generally absent; thus in the commentary of their brief in al-Mi'raj, and our principles do not prohibit it for the contemplative. In Khizanat al-Fiqh: A defect is what decreases the object or the usufruct; otherwise, if merchants consider it a defect, it is a defect, otherwise not. It is better than what is in the book. He mentioned it in al-Talkhis in the chapter of acknowledgment of defect from sales, and its summary is that there are four, he does not return in two issues, and its completion is in his commentary on al-Farisi. He said: (Like running away) from 'abaqa al-'abdu yabiqu with fath [of the ba] from the pattern of ta'iba and qatala in one language, and most commonly from the pattern of daraba, when he runs away from his master without fear or hardship. Al-Ibaq (with kasr) is a verbal noun, so he is 'abiq, plural 'ubbaq like kafir and kuffar; thus in al-Misbah. In al-Jawharah in its chapter, al-Tha'alibi said: The 'abiq is one who flees without oppression from the master; if he flees from oppression, he is not called 'abiq but rather harib (fugitive). Thus ibaq is a defect and fleeing is not a defect. In Khizanat al-Fiqh: Ibaq is hiding from the master in rebellion. In al-Qamus, it is from the pattern of daraba and mana'a and sami'a. Thus it has four forms: the three and qatala as in al-Misbah. He interpreted in al-Qamus as going away without fear or hardship, or he hid then went. He applied it generally so it includes when he runs from the master or from another (e.g., lessee, borrower, depositee) except from a usurper to the master or another if he does not know his residence or cannot return. And it is refuted on their absolute when he runs from the buyer to the seller and does not hide with him, for it is not a defect as in al-Qunyah. And it includes whether the distance is a journey or less, and whether he left the town or not, but the more correct is that the town... He said: (And that by reduction of value) indicates that the intended meaning of price is value. This is supported by what he quoted from al-Muhit just before the alerts in the eleventh issue. He said: (And like a Turkish slave girl who does not know Turkish); i.e., he may return because that is a defect. If he buys an Indian slave girl and finds she does not know Hindi, if people consider it a defect, he may return, otherwise not; thus in al-Nahr from al-Muhit. Al-Bazzaziyyah equated between them, saying: If he buys a Turkish or Indian slave girl who does not know the language, if experts consider it a defect, then likewise, otherwise not. He said: And in al-Mi'raj the black nail was restricted... He said in al-Nahr: The apparent is the absoluteness of what is in al-Fath. He said: (And it is better than what is in the book) He said in al-Nahr: Its reasoning is that the decrease in price due to decrease in the object or the usufruct is known to everyone, not restricted to merchants as the author's words suggest. He said: (And it is refuted on their absolute when he runs away) He said in al-Nahr: It can be answered that we are speaking of ibaq that necessitates a decrease in price according to merchants for it to be a particular. This is from the general [principle] and this does not necessitate it.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This extensive passage defines 'defect' (al-ayb) in the Hanafi school of sales law. The core definition from Kanz al-Daqaiq: 'what necessitates a decrease in price among merchants is a defect.' The goal is to protect the monetary value, and the reference is to the custom of businessmen or craftsmen. The commentator expands that this includes physical flaws (like a black nail) that do not affect function but reduce value in the market, and linguistic deficiency (a slave girl not knowing the language of her origin). However, there is ikhtilif: al-Mi'raj restricts the black nail to Turks, and al-Bazzaziyyah conditions linguistic defects on expert opinion. The passage also gives the Shafi'i rule (return for any deficiency that frustrates a valid purpose and is generally absent) and compares it to the Hanafi position. The Hanafi rule is summarized from Khizanat al-Fiqh: defect is what decreases the object or its usufruct, or what merchants deem a defect. The commentator favors this definition over the author's. Additionally, the passage discusses the specific defect of ibaq (running away) in detail, including its linguistic roots, types, and legal implications. It also mentions that returning a defective item requires the seller's consent or court order; mere action like taking the item back can constitute consent. The passage warns against invalid 'returns' where the buyer simply leaves the item at the seller's place, which does not rescind the sale, and the item perishes on the buyer. The commentary is rich with references to Fath al-Qadir, al-Mi'raj, al-Nahr, etc., providing a comprehensive view of the Hanafi position on defects.

Key Terms
العيب (al-ʿayb): A defect in the sold item that reduces its value or utility according to custom, entitling the buyer to return it.
الإباق (al-ibāq): Running away of a slave from his master without oppression, considered a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary criterion for something to be considered a defect in the Hanafi school according to this passage?
A) It frustrates a valid purpose of the buyer
B) It decreases the price according to merchants or craftsmen
C) It is explicitly listed in the Quran
D) It makes the item unusable

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, a defect is defined by the custom of merchants and craftsmen; anything that reduces market value is a defect, even if it does not affect utility.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Defining a Defect in Sale Contracts
Defining the legal concept of defect with Hanafi and Shafi'i perspectives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a defect in sale contracts and explain how it differs from the Shafi'i definition.

Core Idea
In the Hanafi school, a defect is anything that diminishes the item's essence or benefit, or what merchants consider a defect.

The definition of a defect (ʿayb) is the foundation of the right to return a purchased item (khiyar al-ʿayb). This lesson examines the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions, focusing on the broader Hanafi definition that relies on customary practice (ʿurf). Understanding this definition enables buyers to know when they are entitled to return a defective item and protects against exploitative contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Begin by noting the Arabic terms: al-ʿayb (defect), naqṣ al-ʿayn (decrease in essence), naqṣ al-manfaʿah (decrease in benefit), and 'urf (custom). These are the building blocks of the Hanafi definition.
2
Understand the Shafi'i conditions
The passage outlines the Shafi'i definition: a defect must cause loss of a valid purpose and its absence must be the norm (ghalabah) in similar items. Consider the examples given: a small cut on the thigh does not permit return, but a cut preventing sacrifice does; a non-virgin slave is not returned because non-virginity is common.
The Shafi'i conditions are more restrictive.
3
Grasp the Hanafi definition
From Khazanat al-Fiqh: a defect is what diminishes the essence (ʿayn) or the benefit (manfaʿah) of the item. Additionally, if merchants consider something a defect, it is a defect. This is simpler and does not require the Shafi'i conditions.
The Hanafi definition relies on objective harm or customary opinion.
4
Compare the two schools
Notice the practical difference: under Hanafi law, a non-virgin female slave might be returnable if merchants consider it a defect; under Shafi'i law, it is not because non-virginity is common. The Hanafi school does not require the defect to be rare.
The author prefers the Hanafi definition as 'better'.
5
Note the tarjih
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) endorses the definition from Khazanat al-Fiqh as the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position. This definition is comprehensive and applicable in fatwa.
This is the operative Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضابط عند الشافعية أنه يرد بكل ما في المعقود عليه من منقص القيمة أو نقصان يفوت به غرض صحيح بشرط أن يكون الغالب في أمثال المبيع عدمه. قالوا: وإنما شرطنا فوات غرض صحيح لأنه لو بان فوات تقطعة. يسيرة من فخذه أو ساقه لا رد، ولو قطع من يمنع التضحية ردها وإلا فلا. وشرطنا الغالب لأنه لا ترد الأمة إذا كانت ثيباً أن الثيابة معنى ينقص القيمة لكن ليس الغالب عدم الثيابة؛ كذا كما في شرح وجيزهم في المعراج وقواعدنا لا تأباه للمتأمل. وفي خزانة الفقه : العيب ما نقص العين أو المنفعة وإلا فإن أعده التجار عيباً كان عيباً وإلا فلا وهو أحسن مما في الكتاب
And the principle according to the Shafi'is is that a defect is anything in the sold item that diminishes its value or causes a loss of a valid purpose, provided that the absence of such a defect is the norm in similar items. They said: We only stipulate loss of a valid purpose because if a small cut from his thigh or shin appears, there is no return; but if it prevents sacrifice, it is returned, otherwise not. And we stipulated the norm because a female slave who is a non-virgin is not returned, for non-virginity is a meaning that diminishes value but its absence is not the norm. Thus is it in the commentary of their summary in al-Mi'raj, and our principles do not reject it for the thoughtful. And in Khazanat al-Fiqh: A defect is what diminishes the essence or the benefit; otherwise, if merchants consider it a defect, it is a defect, otherwise not. This is better than what is in the book.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents a comparative definition of a defect (ʿayb) in sale contracts. The author first quotes the Shafi'i school's definition, which requires two conditions: the defect must cause a decrease in value or loss of a valid purpose, and the absence of such defect must be the norm (ghalib) in such items. The Shafi'is illustrate this with examples: a small cut on the thigh does not permit return, but a cut that prevents the animal from being fit for sacrifice does; also, a female slave who is a non-virgin is not returned because non-virginity is common. Then the author presents the Hanafi definition from Khazanat al-Fiqh, which is broader: a defect is anything that diminishes the essence (ʿayn) or the benefit (manfaʿah) of the item; if merchants consider it a defect, it is a defect, otherwise not. The author prefers this latter definition over the one in the main text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq). The Hanafi school does not require the condition of norm (ghalabah) as the Shafi'is do; rather, any decrease in value or benefit, or even the opinion of merchants, determines a defect. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on customary practice (ʿurf) and the objective harm to the buyer.

Key Terms
العيب (al-ʿayb): A defect in the sold item that entitles the buyer to return it; defined here as anything that diminishes the essence (ʿayn) or benefit (manfaʿah) of the item, or what merchants consider a defect.
نقص العين (naqṣ al-ʿayn): A physical decrease in the substance of the item, reducing its value.
نقص المنفعة (naqṣ al-manfaʿah): A decrease in the utility or benefit of the item, affecting its use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, which of the following is considered a defect allowing return of a purchased slave?
A) The slave has a small cut on his thigh that does not affect his work.
B) The slave is a non-virgin female, which is common in her age group.
C) The slave has a chronic illness that reduces his ability to work.
D) The slave is from a large city like Cairo, which is well-known.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of a defect is broader, encompassing any loss in value or utility, and incorporates customary practice (ʿurf).
